Then incise three parallel cuts on the forehead, each about two fingers in length, one in the middle of the forehead parallel to the length of the nose, the second a little further away from the temple muscle, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries on both sides. Keep your hand away from the junction of the jaws, and leave a space of three folded fingers between each incision. Have with you prepared sponges or many dry rags to absorb the blood.
Then insert this sharp-pointed probe whose image is shown here: From the groove that lies next to the temple to the middle groove, and strip off all the skin that is between the grooves along with the peritoneum that is on the bone, then do the same from the middle groove to the third groove, then remove this scalpel. And insert into the first groove also another tool called a chisel, sharp on one side and blunt on the other, its shape is like this.
And make its sharp side face upwards towards the flesh adhering to the skin, and its flat side towards the bone, pushing it until it reaches the middle groove, cutting all vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without reaching the outer surface of the skin.
Then do the same from the middle groove to the other groove, and after a moderate amount of bleeding has flowed, squeeze the places where the blood clots have formed. Then place in each groove a wick of cotton moistened with rose water, oil or vinegar and oil.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, dissolve the sutures and use plenty of lukewarm water for irrigation, then treat it with Basilicum plaster mixed with rose oil and other wound treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five
In treating epistaxis and headaches referred to the eyes.
Whenever someone suffers from frequent, sharp, burning headaches, with the eyes appearing sunken and small, vision weakened, eyelids inflamed and lashes falling out. And there is sharp, painful headache deep within the head and continuous sneezing, know that these symptoms and headaches come from many deep vessels. The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is sharp, painful headache deep within the head and continuous sneezing, know that these symptoms and headaches come from many deep vessels. The best treatment for this is to order the patient to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly across and start the incision from the left temple to the right temple, making it reach the bone and keep your hand away from the chewing muscles of the temples until the bone is exposed and all the blood is wiped off with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with moistened cotton or linen threads, then tie them from above with bandages and moisten the bandages with rose water and oil so that no hot swelling occurs. And when it has healed, if you see that a hot swelling has broken out, do not scrape the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with the dry plaster that causes flesh to grow, like when two parts of fine wheat flour and four parts of colophony are taken and a liniment is prepared from them, which is used for the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six
In treating what falls into the ear.
All that falls into the ear is of four kinds: either a mineral stone or semi-stone like iron and glass, or a plant matter like chickpeas and kernels etc., or liquid things like water and vinegar etc.
Or an animal, so if a stone or a kind of stone that does not breed in the ear falls into it, then receive the sun's rays with the ear. If you see only the stone, grease it with some blackberry or juniper oil, then try to remove it by moving your head or sneezing with condiment powder. And close the nostrils at the onset of sneezing after putting a bandage made of rags or wool around the ear and pulling it upwards. Often the stone comes out this way, but if not, then try to remove it with a gentle hook of this shape:
If it comes out with the hook but not otherwise, then try to remove it with a gentle straight tweezer. If that does not work, then make a copper tube and insert its tip into the ear canal gently.
And close what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil so that there is no way for the wind except through the tube, then pull it out with a strong jerk. Often it comes out this way, but if not as described, then take some Nabatene pitch or prepared pitch used for birds from its tip.
After wrapping it tightly with gauze, then insert it into the ear canal gently after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out despite all these methods, then proceed to incision before heat and swelling occur.
The prescription for incision is to bleed the patient in the vein first and remove as much blood as his strength allows, then seat the patient before you, tilt his ear upwards, and make a small cut at the base of the ear at its fatty part in the lower place.
The incision should be elliptical in shape until it reaches the stone, then remove it using whatever tools you have at your disposal. Then suture the incision quickly and dress it so that it heals.
And if the matter that has fallen into the ear is one of the swellings that have grown and become inflamed, try to remove it using what was mentioned. If this does not succeed in removing it, then take a fine, thin lancet of this shape.
Then use it to cut that type of growth that has fallen into the ear. You should do this only after you are certain that the growth has been moistened by the ear's vapor, causing it to become very small pieces. Then remove them with a fine spoon or tweezers, or using suction.
As we have mentioned, it is easy to remove them. And regarding the water that enters the ear, one should not try to blow their nose first, but rather fill the ear with a piece of soft cotton while lying on the side with the water in it until it comes out.
If not, take many small round stones and heat them gently over a fire. Then insert one of them into the ear canal while standing on one leg on that side and strike it with another stone.
Continue doing this with each stone until all the water has been removed. Alternatively, take a feather or a straw and insert one end into the ear while holding the other end near a fire until it burns, then replace it with another one.
Do this repeatedly until all the water has been removed or drawn out using a tube as previously mentioned with regards to stones. And regarding removing any animal that has entered it, if it is small like a louse and similar creatures, treat it with what was mentioned in the section on insect bites.
If its body is large enough to be seen, try to remove it with tweezers and spoons. Its removal is easier than any other thing that enters the ear. And regarding removing worms that are produced in the ear, if you have treated it with what was mentioned in the section on otalgia without success.
It is necessary to look at the ear in sunlight, if anything appears of worms remove it with tweezers or soft forceps. If nothing appears, take a pipe shaped like this.
Narrow the bottom, widen the top, insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can bear, then suck with a strong syringe several times until all worms are expelled. If they do not come out, plug around the pipe with wax as I mentioned to you regarding the concretion.
If they do not come out as mentioned, use the cupping glasses that I have described and which were tried by the ancients in killing worms. You will find this in the treatise on cupping glasses. The appearance of this instrument is:
As you see, it is made of silver or bronze, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. If you wish, make the cannula inside the pipe of well-smelted copper, or take a reed and wrap its tip with tight cloth.
Then place the oil or serum or whatever medicine you want into the pipe while it is in the ear, then insert the reed with cloth from above and squeeze gently with your hand until the oil reaches inside the hearing passage and feels hot.
Let this be done while the patient is lying down, and let what you pour into the ear have been warmed slightly in fire, and beware that it not be too cold or too hot, for the ear does not tolerate that.
Chapter Seven
On treating sudden deafness of the ear.
Some children are born with their mothers' wombs and the ears of their hearing not pierced.
Also, some people may experience blockage in the ear canal due to injury or flesh that has grown there, and this blockage may be deep within the ear canal, invisible to the eye.
And sometimes it may appear slightly outside the canal and can be seen, but that which is deep within the ear canal is usually difficult to remove. But that which can be seen should have the patient's ear placed in sunlight. Look into it, and if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a soft lancet shaped like this:
Some of its tips may have slight, limited damage, others may be smooth on both sides so as not to harm the ear. If there is a plug made of flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it gently with a fine forceps and cut it off slowly until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep in the ear, take a thin, smooth probe and heat it gently in fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction and the patient experiences heaviness in hearing, withdraw it gently.
Be careful not to injure the ear drum. Then, insert a wick made to fit the size of the hole into the ear canal. Soak it in Egyptian wax for several days until the wound heals, or use a wick moistened with powdered pitch.
If you see that the ear is inflamed and hot, do not remove the wick but replace it with another one smeared in rose oil to calm the inflammation until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear. Then proceed with the other treatment mentioned in the chapter on fractures.
Chapter Eight.
On treating warts appearing on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry. Do not grasp them with forceps or cutters and remove them from their roots using scissors. Then apply powdered pitch to the site.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a fine brass instrument and press it firmly afterwards. Often, it returns if some of its roots remain. If cauterized with fire, the roots will be burned and it will not return.
Chapter Nine.
On treating sudden inflammation of the eyelids.
It often happens that something resembling coldness and stiffness appears on the eyelids.
It is called 'cold' because of the accumulation of coarse moisture in both upper and lower eyelids. If the coldness appears prominently on the surface and moves to all sides, it is easy to treat by making a transverse incision on it.
Then strip it from all sides gently until it is freed, then hang it on the hook and cut it. If you are unable to cut it until the eyelid has pierced through with the cut, this will not harm the patient at all. If the slit is large, close it with stitches and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no harm in leaving it as the salve will close it and adhere to it. If the eyelid turns inward toward the inner surface of the eyelid, flip the eyelid and hang the eyelid on a hook without needing to cut it and trim it from all sides.
If the eyelid has pierced through with the cut, this will not harm at all. Then wash the eye after cutting the eyelid with saltwater and treat the area with something that adheres until the patient heals.
Chapter Ten.
On treating the inflammation that appears in the eyelid.
Sharnqah" is a swelling that occurs in the upper layers of the eyelid, and it is more common among children. It causes heaviness of the eyes and leads to frequent colds during winter, making them unable to look towards sunlight. because tears flow rapidly from their eyes.
Therefore, they are always seen leaning on their faces and sleeping with their eyelids underneath the eyebrows wet and puffy due to the swelling of sharnqah being evident. When you press the area with your fingers, you can feel the sharnqah between them. The procedure is for the patient to place his head in your lap.
Then take a cotton cloth and prepare from it a wick. Make a circle with this wick according to the size of the sharnqah, then place it on top of it and press gently with your fingers from all sides to gather the sharnqah in the center of the circle.
Then make a transverse cut in the center of that wetness using the sharp scalpel, with the cut not being wider than the one made for bloodletting. As for its depth, do not cut all the skin until you reach the sharnqah.
In most cases, the sharnqah will come out through the cut like a white piece of fat. Pull it out using a cloth wrapped between your fingers while turning your hand right and left until it is completely removed. Be careful not to increase the cut so that the scalpel does not slip into the eye and harm it.
If the sinew does not appear at first in the cut, do not increase the cut too much gently until the sinew appears, then draw it out as mentioned before. Then dip a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the site and pull it with force, some people use crushed salt.
Then place it inside the cut to dissolve any remaining moisture, then treat it until it heals. If inflammation occurs at the site, treat it with musk incense until it heals.
The Eleventh Chapter.
On Kinds of Eye Diseases.
If supernumerary cilia grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course and below the normal cilia, it will harm the eye and cause various diseases such as continuous tearing, drooping of the eyelids, whiteness, and thickening to the point of causing blindness.
The eye can be inflamed in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or by applying a strong medication as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery, or by cutting and stitching, or by using a probe as I have described.
It is necessary to hold the patient's head in your lap, then flip the eyelid with your left hand. If it does not flip over, insert a needle into the lower part of the eyelid with thread attached and pass it through from above near the hair, then pull the thread upwards with the eyelid.
Flip it back with a probe, then cut the excess hair from inside the eyelid using a fine scissor from the larger angle to the smaller angle. Then pass the thread through and place a small cotton pad or cloth underneath the eyelid, then mark on the eyelid with ink.
Like the shape of an as leaf, except that the shape should be according to how much you want to raise the eyelid, for people differ in this regard. Some may require cutting off a suitable amount from the eyelid according to how much it droops.
Some may require less cutting according to the degree of drooping, then cut along the two lines you have marked using a fine scissor starting from the larger angle to the smaller angle. The cut should be close to the natural hair with the thickness of a probe.
Then the probe is inserted into one of the corners of the skin, and all of it is split open. Then the lips are sewn together with a fine wool thread using a needle, and blood is wiped away. The remaining threads are attached to the eyebrows with some ingenious substance.
If you wish to do this, proceed; otherwise, no harm will come of it. Then the stitches and threads remain for about three or four days before they are removed. If you wish, you may leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something that dries and constricts. For when the wound heals and closes, the eyelid will become raised; however, stitching is preferable.
This method of cauterization has been mentioned by the ancients, although it is burdensome for the patient and is not preferable due to its difficulty. There is another method of cauterization which involves teaching the eyelid to assume a shape similar to that of an oak leaf, as described. Then lift up the eyelid using three probes, spaced apart or grouped together in this manner:
Then cut off the remaining part of the eyelid with small scissors evenly, like so:
If you cannot secure the probes, take a needle with thread and insert it into the center of the shape. Then insert another thread near the larger angle and a third thread near the smaller angle. Gently gather the threads between your fingers and raise them evenly.
Raise the entire taught skin as described, then sew together the edges of the wound and treat it until it heals. If there is an inflamed swelling upon cutting or slicing, soothe it with camphor and similar analgesic ointments.
Sometimes the lower eyelid may also turn inwards, in which case the methods mentioned earlier such as cutting, stitching, cauterization with fire or harsh medications should not be employed, as they are more burdensome and harmful to the patient than the condition itself.
Cauterization with a hot wire is performed as follows: turn the eyelid inward, make an incision inside it as described, then prepare two thin wires or rods of equal length to the eyelid and width less than that of a lancet.
I have cut the corners of both sides where the threads adhere, then gently brought them together over what remains of the eyelid and tightly pulled both ligaments from both sides, leaving it for some days. If the skin is too tight, it dies and turns black and rots.
Until it falls off by itself, if delayed, cut it with scissors and treat it until it heals. If it adheres, raise the eyelid without damaging the eye lashes. This is the shape and size of the ligaments.
Chapter Twelve
Regarding raising ingrown eyebrow hairs with a needle.
If one or two hairs are causing the problem, take a fine needle and thread it with a thin smooth silk thread. Then gather its ends and tie them with a very loose knot, letting the length of the thread be about a span.
Then take another fine thread and insert it underneath the first one, tying its ends if desired. Place the eye near a light source to see clearly as this is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread under the unwanted hair at its root.
And quickly pierce the eyelid with it until you bring it out from above the natural hair. Then pull the thread upwards and insert that hair into the needle loop if there is one or two or three hairs at most.
Then pull your hand gently until the hair comes out with the needle loop from above the natural hair. If only the loop comes out without the hair, pull the thread downwards until the loop exits from the upper hole.
Then put the hair back into the loop and pull both until it comes out of the upper hole and you see it with your eye. Then cut the loop and thread and remove them, and leave the eyelid stretched for two or three days until the flesh grows on the hair.
If the hair is short, either attach a long natural hair to it so that it comes out with it, or leave it until it grows longer later on and then remove it.
Chapter Thirteen
In the treatment of eyelid spasm.
This spasm is called blepharospasm and may be either natural or acquired. The acquired type occurs from an injury, scar tissue, or tumor, etc.
The procedure is to excise the scar tissue and separate the eyelids, then insert a cotton thread between them and tie it until healed. Avoid using drying or constricting agents in treatment.
If you do this, the spasm will return worse than before. Instead, use relaxing agents like fenugreek seeds ground with water mixed with tragacanth gum and linseed oil, melted diaphoretic ointment, moistened with olive oil, and applied to a cotton thread for treatment.
The goal of treatment is to prevent the eyelids from adhering as they were originally. The procedure mentioned earlier is one way to partially repair the spasm, not to completely restore the original condition.
Chapter Fourteen
On treating lower eyelid spasm
This type of spasm at the bottom is called entropion, which may be natural or acquired. The acquired type occurs from injury, scar tissue, or tumor, etc.
The procedure is to take a curved needle with double thread, insert it into the flesh and pass it from one angle to the other until the thread appears at both ends of the flesh, then pull the flesh upwards using the needle and cut it with wide scissors.
If the eyelid does not return to its proper shape, take a probe and place it on the incision site, rotate the eyelid with it, then make two incisions inside the eyelid at both ends of the cut, so that they meet to form an angle. Until it looks like this shape:
Resembling the Greek letter lambda, remove excess flesh from the sharp edge below the eyelid, then suture it with two silk sutures. Treat it with relaxing agents and cotton threads as mentioned earlier until healed.
If a frenulum has appeared due to a cut or stitching or circumcision, then it is necessary to make an incision of moderate depth without hair on the skin above it as mentioned before, and then separate the lips by twisting as described.
The general principle in treating a frenulum, whether it is above or below, is to perform the operation according to what seems feasible based on its appearance, for it may differ greatly in shape. The skilled surgeon will find a way to manage any situation.
Until the form returns to its natural state or approximates it, the same disease will indicate what operation and tool are most suitable in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On adhesion of the eyelid to the mucous membrane or the cornea.
This adhesion occurs in many people, where the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye preventing it from easy movement and hindering its normal function. It is necessary to insert a blunt tip underneath the eyelid.
And lift it upwards or extend it with forceps, then cut the adhesion using a fine scissors not sharp-pointed to avoid hurting the patient during the procedure and damaging the eye.
And expect that once the eyelid returns to its natural position and the adhesion is completely healed, you should then instill saltwater into the eye to wash it or apply a solution of Indian bdellium dissolved in water using cotton wicks to separate the eyelid from the eye.
And place a bandage moistened with egg white on top of the eye, and after three days use padded splints until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cutting the foreskin and circumcision.
The foreskin may be of two types: either nervous, resembling a thin, hard serous membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a solid, white fluid that becomes stuck when touched by an iron tool or forceps.
And neither type becomes fixed with forceps. Both begin from the glans penis and gradually extend until they cover the eye and prevent light and movement of the eye. The procedure for cutting it is to place the patient's head in your lap.
Then he opens his eyes and lifts the eyelid with his hand, then he takes a hook with little curvature and inserts it into the angle of the eye, then he takes a needle and threads it with a horse or cow hair, and a strong thread, bending one end of the needle slightly, and inserts it into the center of the prolapse.
And pushes it in with the needle and ties the prolapse with the thread, pulling it up, then he scrapes off the side of the prolapse that is behind the cornea like he is spreading it with the hair to its end, then cuts off the rest at the root of the sac with fine scissors or a small knife and leaves the natural flesh of the sac.
Lest he expose the corner of the eye to constant weeping, and so he pulls out the prolapse with the hook alone or with the thread as mentioned earlier, then scrapes off its smooth-edged tip with a pair of scissors, the shape of which is like this:
And he should be careful not to touch the cornea with the scissors lest it cause a rent and prolapse of the eye, then after cutting he applies some powdered salt or Indian incense into the eye and leaves it for another day, then treats it as necessary until it heals.
If the prolapse is not nervous and he cannot insert a needle into it nor fix a hook, then this type of prolapse is often treated with medications until it heals as described in its section, but if you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
And take fine smooth-edged scissors like this shape: And peel off the prolapse with it gently from above, if you see that it becomes loose and falls apart and only peeling has affected it, then drop some Indian incense or red incense or powdered salt into the eye and leave it for another day.
Then prepare to perform the operation until all of it is gone, unless you encounter a hot swelling in the eye, leave it and treat the hot swelling until it heals, then continue the operation until it heals, as for the remaining flesh of the sac.
If the thread causes severe eye pain, suspend it with a hook and cut off some of its length without delving too deep lest bleeding ensue. Then apply red saffron or ginger to the eyes until healed.
Chapter Seventeen
In cutting the pterygium and any excess flesh that grows in the eye.
Sometimes, red flesh grows over the eyeball in some people's eyes, covering or approaching or overflowing onto the eyelids. In severe cases, the eyelids may invert outward, resembling pterygium.
The procedure is as follows: The patient should lie down on their back and open their eyes wide while you use appropriate tweezers to pluck out all the red flesh.
Alternatively, grasp it with forceps or a needleholder and cut the flesh bit by bit until all of it is removed. Be careful not to injure the eye during the procedure. Use either the scissors we described for cutting the pterygium or small scissors. Like those used for plucking eyebrows, as shown in the illustration in the following chapter.
After removing all the pterygium, fill the eye with finely ground salt or apply red saffron powder and other suitable medications. Then cover the eye from outside with a cotton pad soaked in egg white to prevent inflammation.
If some pterygium remains and bleeding is profuse, fearing inflammation, leave the eye alone and treat it with medications that reduce inflammation. Then perform the procedure again until it heals. Do likewise for any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, do not attempt this procedure on weak eyes or those with corneal scars. Avoid using cautery or performing any of these operations unless you are confident that the eye can tolerate them.
Chapter Eighteen
In plucking eyebrows from the eye.
Eyebrows are red veins that grow around the eye, obstructing vision and weakening the eye over time. Therefore, first examine the eye to ensure it is strong and free from other diseases besides the eyebrows.
Then, the patient should place his head in a hollow [scalpels' stand]. Then suspend those vessels using one or two hooks according to your skill. The hooks should be bent like this:
Or they may be two double hooks in one body like this:
Then, using fine forceps gently sever those vessels until you see the eye clear of them and blood ceases to flow. Be cautious not to harm the eye with the tips of the forceps. Do this for half a day facing the sun.
And be precise in your work so as not to cut anything other than those vessels. Once done, cauterize the eye with green or red wax until it becomes numb. If you cannot complete all of this at once, apply something that alleviates eye pain.
Leave him for some time until the pain subsides and the heat of the inflammation abates, then resume working on it in this manner until the eye is completely healed.
And leave him [with his head in the apprentice's stand, preventing tears from flowing, ensuring clear view of the eye and maintaining its position]. The basis of the operation is to open the eye using tweezers or a triangle held by an assistant.
And the head of the patient should be in the apprentice's stand, preventing tears from flowing and ensuring clear view of the eye. You may start the operation either from above or below the eye, but it is preferable to begin by severing two vessels at the upper canthus; one at the outer angle and the other at the inner angle.
And hold them with the middle finger and thumb, then suspend hooks underneath. Hold them with the index and middle fingers and gently elevate the skin until all the vessels are visible. Then sever the smaller branch of the temporal vein either with scissors or a fine knife.
And pull the cheek downwards until all the vessels rise above the orbital margin without cutting the lacrimal gland, for tears and blood may blur your vision.
Then cut with scissors what is above and below the canthus until only that near the cornea remains. If possible, lift it off the cornea with hooks using a gentle motion of the cheek; this is preferable.
If it is cut off, it may become inflamed for some days until the pain subsides and the hot swelling decreases; then resume treatment.
Chapter Nineteen.
In returning the reed into the nose.
The physicians call it 'nasorah' when treating it with heat or with the sharp cauterizing medicine mentioned above without success. In this case, there is no remedy except to incise upon the boil at its maturity and extract all the fluid or pus within it.

So that the patient feels wind coming out of it when they hold their hand over their nose. Then apply dry constricting medicines to the area as mentioned earlier. Chapter Twenty
Inflammation of the eye.
If the whole eye is inflamed without any damage to sight or loss of vision, it should be cooled down and then bound with a bandage. Then place a hot compress on the back of the head without insistence, and suck out some thin mucus through a narrow tube.
Then apply to the eye a poultice made of onions, myrrh, honey and egg-white. Then bind the eye from above with many layers of bandages. The recipe for the bandages is as follows: take soft cotton strips, each folded into four layers, and make many such strips.
Then place these layers one on top of another according to the size needed for the eye. Then tie them tightly with a strong bandage and reapply the hot compress without insistence. Then remove the compress and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during this period, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night as mentioned before. If the eye has not improved, repeat the process of binding and compressing until it improves.
Chapter Twenty-One
On cutting the optic nerve.
If a prolapse occurs in the layer of the optic nerve, causing it to protrude outside the eyelids like a grape and disfigure the face, and it is desired to cut it, then proceed as follows:
First, insert a needle into the base of the prolapse from below upwards. Then insert another needle with a double thread from the side of the attachment and pass it through. Leave the first needle in place.
Then cut the loop of the double thread and tie some of the prolapse around the needle. Tighten it gently, then remove the needle and place a piece of wool moistened with egg-white on the eye until the threads and prolapse fall off. Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, tie the prolapse from all sides with a bandage to reduce inflammation. Then cauterize it gently with a thin probe that reaches the depth of the eye to make the egg-white moisture flow down and dry up on the margin of the eye until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Second
In treating fractures.
This ailment called fracture, in fact, is a union of parts in the eye like flowing water without any break. The procedure for this is: Have the patient sit on an upright chair and then grasp his head with both hands from both sides.
Move him until you see the fracture drop down with your eye, then stop and do not let go, and light will be released and the patient will see things as he did before. If it does not drop down, know that it is water. If moving the head does not cause the fracture to drop, repeat the procedure several times until it does so.
If not, seat the patient in front of you and then take a fine scalpel and cut it slightly above the conjunctiva near where the fracture touches the conjunctiva in the cornea until the matter comes out.
If it comes out, pour warm water mixed with barley or hot water with cumin and barley into the eye, then treat him with other remedies until he recovers.
Chapter Twenty-Third

Then place the tip of the dropper near the cornea, pressing firmly while maintaining eye white level. Then push the dropper with force while turning it with your hand until you feel that it has reached an empty space inside the eye.
The dropper should penetrate into the depth equal to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the cornea. Then bring it back up towards where the water is located.
Then press it down repeatedly until the water flows out immediately. If the patient can see again, then let go a little. If the water rises, push it down again without removing the dropper.
If it has settled and not risen, gently remove the probe while turning your hand slightly, then dissolve some pure salt in water and use it to rinse the eye from within; afterwards, apply a compress of wool or linen soaked in water externally around the entire eye.
If it is white and red and its whites are tied together, we and the people of our time apply ground cumin with egg-white; but if the probe does not enter the eye due to its hardness because some people have very hard eyes. He should abstain from all movements and coughing, and his food should be what is soft according to his nature; he must not move his head to the right or left at all until the third day, when the bandage will be removed in that darkened room and his sight will be tested.
Show him objects, then replace the bandage until the seventh day; do not do this during treatment or immediately after the probe has been removed, but rather avoid it before water rises quickly due to excessive sight.
If there is an inflamed tumor, do not remove the bandage before the seventh day and treat it with something that calms the inflammation until it subsides; then release his sight and place a veil over his face to shade his eyes while he remains in that dark room for some days.

Chapter Twenty-Four
In the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
There may grow in the nose different kinds of superfluous flesh: some resembling many-legged spiders, others being hard, cancerous lumps of livid color, and still others being soft and not livid.
As for those fleshes which are soft and not malignant nor cancerous, it is necessary that the patient sits before you facing the sun, opens his nostril, places a hook in those fleshes, draws them outwards, then cuts off what can be reached with a fine sharp lancet from one side.
Until you know that all the flesh has gone, and if any remains which you cannot cut off, remove it with one of the gentle instruments without causing harm. If blood flows profusely or a hot inflammation appears,
If it is one of the malignant tumors, hasten to cauterize it until bleeding stops and all the flesh is removed. Then rinse the nostril with water or drink afterwards. If the nostril opens and moisture reaches the throat.
Know that he is cured if moisture reaches the throat as it should. If not, know that there is still flesh growing inside the hollowed-out bones which the instrument could not reach. In this case, take a thread of coarse linen
and tie many knots on it, leaving the space of one finger or less between each knot. The patient should insert one end of the thread into his nostril gently or as far as he can after making it like a probe and draw it out through the other nostril until it reaches the pharynx.
The patient should then bring out both ends of the thread through his mouth and collect them together. This procedure is often done by boys in schools as it is easy for those who wish to do so. Then, bring together the two ends of the thread
and use the knots on the thread to remove the flesh, continuing until all of it has been cut off by the knots. Then remove the thread and rinse the nostril with Egyptian ointment for three days or more.
Until the salve consumes all remaining flesh, then the last thing in the nose becomes a lead pipe. Sometimes it heals, but if further treatment is needed for drying, that may be used.

Chapter Twenty-Five:
Regarding the warts on the tip of the nose.
It often happens that a wart grows at the tip of the nose, becoming larger and swelling over days until it becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it should be cut at its first appearance and completely removed. Then cauterize the spot with either a cautery iron or a burning medication that serves its purpose. If cutting is delayed until it grows large, observe: if it is hard, solid like madder's color and slightly sensitive.
Do not apply the iron to it if it is a cancerous tumor. I have seen many instances where this tumor was cut off, resulting in great harm to its owner. However, if the tumor is soft and not madder-colored, cutting is possible. So have the patient sneeze and cut it off without caution or delay. Then treat the spot with something that dries and contracts until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six:
Regarding the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated due to an injury or similar, know that when such separation occurs in one of these cartilage tissues, little can be done except for some people.
If any of these tissues is injured, observe if the wound is fresh with blood. Then suture the wound edges and treat until it heals. But if the separation has completely healed with clear edges, then scrape off all edges with a thin skin blade until they bleed.
Then the lips are joined by stitching and drawn together, and powdered frankinense and myrrh are placed upon them. A poultice of fresh honey or another adhesive ointment is then applied over the bandage, and it is left bound for two or three days before being removed.
And the dressing is changed until the stitches fall out on their own, then it is treated with ointment until healed. For stitching, one should disperse the connection either by needles as we have described for abdominal suturing or with thread as mentioned earlier.
Chapter Twenty-Seventh
Regarding the extraction of cysts that appear on the lips.
Many people may have small, hard tumors resembling raisins or smaller and larger ones inside their lips. One should flip the lip over, cut around each tumor, and then hook it out with forceps.
And cut around each one on all sides, then fill the place after cutting with powdered bdellium to stop the bleeding. Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the wounds with bindings until they heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eighth
Regarding excision of excess gum tissue.
Excess tissue often grows on the gums, which ancient writers called epulis. One should hook it with forceps or hold it with tweezers and cut it at its base, then let the time bleed out, and finally apply powdered bdellium to the site.
Or use one of the dry ligatures if it recurs after treatment, cut it again and cauterize it so that it will not return.
Chapter Twenty-Ninth
Regarding scraping teeth with iron
Sometimes, rough and ugly scales accumulate on the surfaces of teeth, both inside and outside, between the gums. These may become blackened, yellowed, or greened causing decay in the gums and making the teeth unsightly. Therefore
One should sit the patient before oneself and scrape the teeth with iron, removing all rough scales and sandy matter until nothing remains. Do likewise for blackened, green, yellowed, or other discolored areas to clean them thoroughly.
If the toothache persists from the first cleaning, repeat the procedure on another day, secondly and thirdly until you achieve your desired goal. Know that a tooth requires various types of cleaning based on its shape.
For the instrument used to clean a tooth from inside is different from that used for outside, and the one used between teeth has another shape. Have all these various instruments ready.
Chapter Thirty
On Tooth Extraction
It is preferable to treat a tooth from its pain with every means and delay extraction as much as possible, for there is no recourse if it is extracted, as it is an esteemed organ. However, if it must be extracted,
If the patient decides to extract a tooth, make sure that you have the painful tooth ready at hand. Often, a patient may be deceived by pain and think that it is in the correct tooth, extracting it, only for the pain to not subside until they extract the actual diseased tooth. We have seen this happen many times with barbers.
If you have correctly identified the painful tooth, then it is necessary to circumcise around it with a sharp lancet to loosen the gum from all sides. Then, gently shake it with your fingers or gentle forceps little by little until it becomes loose.
Then firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps and have the patient sit between your legs, supporting their head to prevent movement. Gently pull the tooth straight out, taking care not to break it. If it does not come out, use one of those instruments and gently insert it underneath from both sides, then wiggle it as you did before.
If the tooth is hollow or loose, it is necessary to fill that hole with a piece of cotton and secure it tightly with a thin silk thread to prevent it from breaking apart when pulling with forceps. It is also important to circumcise around the gum on all sides very well.
Be careful not to break the tooth, as leaving a part of it would cause greater pain than the original complaint. Avoid doing what ignorant barbers do when they rush to extraction without following these instructions.
Frequently they perform great injuries upon people, the easiest of which is for a tooth to break and its roots remain whole or some of them do. Or else some of the jawbones are extracted as we have seen many times, then they rinse their mouth with wine or vinegar and salt afterwards. If bleeding occurs from the place, it happens frequently; therefore crush some Frankincense at that time and apply it to the place.
Otherwise, if Frankincense does not help, the shape of the mercy tongs used to move teeth should first be long in the tips and short in the handle, thick so it does not bend when you grasp it for a tooth. And this is the shape of large tongs.
It should be thick in the grasp so that it does not give itself nor bend. It should be made of Indian iron or high-quality steel, with sharp edges at its tip that fit into each other tightly when grasped.
The tips can also be made in the shape of a probe to make them strong for gripping.

Chapter Two-Thirty.
Regarding the extraction of teeth growing upon others.
If teeth grow in a direction other than their natural course, this mars the appearance, especially if it occurs in women or slaves. If one tooth has grown behind another, it should not be extracted.
If its extraction is not possible nor its reduction, then remove it. But if it is firmly attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument whose image is this:
And it resembles a small beak and should be made of sharp Indian iron, with many teeth for cutting due to the hardness of the tooth. And if the tooth is protruding but still firmly attached, reduce it using this instrument whose image is as follows:
It is entirely made of iron, with its tip being very thinly engraved like a needle-making file. Use it to cool the tooth gradually over several days, gently so as not to cause the tooth to fall out prematurely. Then, remove it completely using some tweezers.
If a tooth has broken off, causing discomfort to the tongue while speaking, it should also be smoothed down gently until the sharp edge is gone and it no longer causes irritation to the tongue or interferes with speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three
In fixing loose teeth with threads of silver or gold.
If the front teeth are loose and shaken by a blow or fall, so that the patient cannot bite anything without them falling out, and they have been treated with binding drugs without success.
The procedure is to draw them together with a thread of gold or silver, gold being preferable because silver tarnishes and decays over time while gold remains permanently unchanged. The thread should be moderate in thickness according to the space between the teeth.
The method of tying is to take the thread and insert its bend between two sound teeth, then sew it through the loose tooth or teeth until the sewing reaches a sound tooth on the other side.
Then repeat the sewing back to where you started and pull gently and wisely so that it does not move at all, pulling the thread at the roots of the teeth so that it does not slip out. Then cut off the excess ends with scissors, gather them together and tuck them in between the sound tooth and the loose one.
So that it does not harm the tongue, then leave it tied like this indefinitely. If it breaks or loosens, reinforce it with another thread which will remain thus forever. This is a diagram of the teeth and the method of tying in two sound teeth and two loose ones as shown here:
It may happen that a single tooth or two fall out and return to their places and be tied as described, but this is done by an experienced operator who may carve a bone from ox bones in the shape of a tooth and place it in the space left by the fallen tooth. And he ties it as mentioned above and it remains thus.
Chapter Thirty-Four
On cutting the frenulum that prevents speech.
The frenulum under the tongue may be either natural, present from birth, or acquired due to an injury that has healed.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth and tilt their head back, raise their tongue, then cut the frenulum horizontally until the tongue is released from its restraint. If it is somewhat hard and complex due to scar tissue from a healed injury, use a scalpel to incise and sever the frenulum horizontally until it is completely detached and freed.
Beware that the incision is deep in the flesh, for it may cut a blood vessel there and cause bleeding. Then, rinse after cutting with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Finally, place under the tongue a piece of cotton held by the patient each night.
If it should not adhere again, then if bleeding occurs, apply powdered tragacanth to the spot. If it continues to bleed, cauterize the place with an appropriate eye-glass. Then treat him with other remedies until he is healed,
Chapter Thirty-Five
In the removal of a toad born under the tongue.
It may happen that under the tongue there appears a tumor resembling a small toad, preventing the tongue from its natural action and perhaps even filling the mouth. To treat this, open the patient's mouth towards the sun and look at the tumor.
If it appears red or black and hard, causing no sensation to the patient, do not touch it for it is cancerous. But if it inclines towards whiteness with moisture, prick it with a needle and cut it out with a delicate lancet from all sides.
If bleeding occurs during your work, apply powdered tragacanth until the bleeding stops, then resume your work until you remove it completely. Then rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat him with other suitable remedies until he is healed,
Chapter Thirty-Six
Treatment of tonsillar tumors.
And if other tumors appear inside the throat, resembling those outside called tonsils, and they are not cured by the treatment mentioned in the previous sections,
If the tumor is hard, dark red and insensitive to touch, do not treat it with iron. If it is red with a thick base, also do not treat it with iron due to fear of bleeding. Leave it alone until it matures, for it will either atrophy or burst on its own.
If it is white, round with a thin base, this is the one that should be cut. Before operating, ensure that its heat has completely subsided or somewhat decreased.
Then sit the patient facing the sun with his head in your lap, open his mouth and take a spatula in your hand, and press his tongue downwards as shown in this picture:
Make it from silver or brass, thin like a blade. When you press the tongue down with it and see the swelling appear on the tonsil, take a needle and stitch it into the tonsil, then pull it outwards as much as possible without pulling any of the surrounding tissue.
Then cut it with this instrument, its shape resembles a scissor but both of its ends are bent so that each of its two sharp edges faces the other. It is made from Indian iron or Damascus steel:
If you do not have this instrument, then cut it with a knife shaped like this:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other. After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner, then gargle the patient with cold water or vinegar and water.
If there is bleeding, gargle with water in which dried figs or oak leaves have been steeped until the bleeding stops, then treat it until it heals. Other growths may appear in the throat besides the tonsils, cut them as mentioned for cutting tonsils.
A woman was treated for a growth that had appeared inside her throat, striking the epiglottis and causing difficulty in swallowing. She could barely breathe through a narrow passage and was unable to eat or drink.
And she was on the verge of death if left for another day or two, when suddenly a portion of the growth protruded from both of her nostrils. I quickly inserted a needle into one of them and pulled it out, removing a suitable piece.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril, and did the same for what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the needle into the growth itself, and cut off some of it without much bleeding.
So the woman's throat was relieved, and she immediately began drinking water. She then started eating, but we kept cutting away at the growth for a long time, as it would leave behind a replacement for what was cut.
An abscess appeared inside the throat, then stopped growing and traveled to another side; I do not know what happened to it after that.
The Thirty-Seventh Chapter.
Regarding the removal of a throat tumor.
If a cold descends upon the throat and becomes inflamed and is white, long, and rectangular in shape, it is called a 'column'. But if it is thick at its base and round in shape, it is called an 'apple'. If treated with what was mentioned in the section but no improvement resulted.
I saw a warm tumor that had subsided and was thin; it should not be cut. If it was thick, round, and short with no length, or bloody, purple, black in color, or without sensation, then it is permissible to cut it.
Therefore, it is beneficial for the patient. If you see it with the characteristics mentioned - white and long in shape - tell the patient to sit facing the sun and press his tongue with the instrument described earlier, then insert the needle into the 'apple'.
And pull it downwards and cut it with one of the instruments mentioned for cutting tonsils, but do not cut more than what is beyond its natural state, as excessive cutting can harm speech.
After cutting, use what was described for cutting tonsils to treat it until it heals. If the patient is too weak to cut it, do not attempt it without fear or caution. The only way to proceed in this case is with a strong medication: place the patient's head in a vice.
Then press the patient's tongue with the instrument mentioned, take the strong water described in the chapter on cautery, and soak a wick in it without extinguishing it. Make sure it is neither too thick nor too thin, then fill the tube of this instrument.
The tip of the tube into which you place the medication should be like the cup of a spoon. Place the instrument with the medication on the throat while the patient is lying on his side so that saliva does not flow from his mouth into his throat and harm him.
Then grasp your hand with the medicine and squeeze it on the throat for half an hour until you see that it has darkened and the sting of the medicine has subsided. If you wish, you may take a cotton wad, wrap it around the tip of a wand, and dip the cotton in the medicine.
Then insert the wand with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton adheres to the throat. Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired level of cleansing, then leave it alone; it will rot and detach after three or four days. If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.
After cleansing, wipe around the uvula with a cotton swab soaked in olive oil and use it to dry any surrounding medicine. Then gargle with cold water and treat both externally with poultices and internally with gargarisms until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and far from frightening.
The throat can also be treated more gently than cleansing and cutting. It is treated with incense in this manner: take equal parts of fenureek, zofa, saeter, sabsabillah, sheikh, babunj, qaisum, and similar plants. Collect them all or some in a pot and soak them in vinegar.
Then boil the pot gently over a slow fire until the wisdom's tin melts, and make a hole in the middle of its lid to fit the curved instrument made of silver or copper.
Insert the tip with the funnel into the patient's mouth until the steam reaches the uvula through the tube, gently warming it. Repeat this several times until the inflammation subsides, but avoid using this treatment at the onset of the swelling.
For steam inhalation is often effective in increasing the swelling. It is preferable to use this method only after the hot inflammation has subsided. If you do not have this instrument, you can use a reed with an egg shell attached to its tip to prevent burning the patient's mouth.
Because the egg shell prevents the hot steam from burning the mouth, making it a safe and effective treatment.
Chapter Eighty-Three
On extracting splinters and other foreign bodies from the throat without causing harm.
Frequently, a bone or fish spine gets stuck in the throat; it is necessary to remove what is visible by pressing the tongue with an instrument towards the sun.
It is necessary for you to discern what is in the throat, and whatever does not appear to you; it is advisable to make the patient vomit before his food settles in his stomach, perhaps the stuck object will come out with the vomiting or the patient may swallow a piece of cloth or a stalk of celery.
Or he may swallow a piece of dry bread or take a piece of soft seawater sponge tied to a thread, then swallow it; if it reaches the place of the splinter, quickly pull out the thread and do this repeatedly.
Frequently, the splinter or bone sticks there and comes out; if it does not come out as mentioned above, then use this lead instrument.
This instrument should be a little thicker than a reed and have a hooked end; the patient should insert it gently into his throat while raising his head upwards, taking care not to touch his larynx to avoid coughing. The doctor may also insert it with his hand,
And the patient inserting it himself is better, as he knows the location of the stuck object; he should push it downwards or pull his hand back upwards with the instrument according to what is easiest for him until it comes out,
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On Removing Foreign Bodies Stuck in the Throat.
If you have treated the foreign body with the medications mentioned earlier and it did not help, then look into the patient's throat at the sun after pressing his tongue with the instrument I described to you.
If you see the foreign body, then grasp it with a small forceps or a delicate hook. If you cannot do this, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the foreign body; then insert a red-hot iron probe into the tube.
Do this repeatedly and let the patient abstain from water all day; then give him a cup of cold water to rinse his mouth without swallowing any, shaking it occasionally with his hand.
If the phlegm falls onto the threshold when it senses water, if it does not come out as described, then clear the throat with urine and use the instrument mentioned in God's book to cauterize the tonsils. This should be done repeatedly until it falls.
As for the procedure with incense, take a suitable quantity in which you have placed burning charcoal from a fire. The vessel should be covered by a lid with an opening at its center into which you insert the tip of the instrument. Then place the incense and make sure the patient places his mouth over the other end of the pipe while closing his mouth so that no smoke escapes until he feels that the incense has reached his throat.

Chapter Forty.
In it, he describes how to speak about the matter of tumors and their removal.
There are many types of tumors, each differing from the other in their nature and in the liquid they contain.
The second type differs according to the location of the tumors within the body, as a tumor in the head is not like one in the buttocks, and a tumor in the area of heat is not like one at a joint. Each has its own ruling regarding treatment.
And among tumors, some are not to be incised except after the pus within them has ripened and fully matured, while others are to be incised when they are still fresh, before their pus has fully ripened, such as those that occur near joints.
Because if a tumor occurs near a joint and continues until it rots around it, perhaps it will damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus becoming a cause for lameness in that limb. Or the tumor may be near an important organ.
For if you delay its incision until it ripens, you will harm the main organ or [if it is] near the bladder, then incise it fresh. Indeed, it is necessary to incise the tumor when it is fresh and not yet fully ripe, which occurs near the bladder, lest the wound become gangrenous and penetrate into the bladder, causing it to become perforated.
And it may become difficult to heal, and one should know the appropriate time for incising tumors that have fully ripened: when there is no pain from the tumor and fever has abated, redness has decreased, pulsation has stopped, and the head of the tumor is softening along with other signs.
It is preferable to locate the incision at the lowest point of the tumor, if possible, so that it may be easier for the matter to flow downwards, or in the thinnest part of the tumor and where it is most projecting. And let the incision be extended along the length of the body if the tumors are in the manner of the hands or feet.
And places of muscles, tendons, nerves, and blood vessels, and in general all smooth places that do not bend are suitable for incision. But if the tumor is in a place where there is bending, then let the incision follow that place. And if the tumor is in fatty places, it is preferable to leave it unincised.
Until its ripening is complete, as we have said. For if you incise it before that, the discharge of pus from it will be prolonged and abundant, and its wound may become hard and dirty, and some tumors may be incised across the body when necessary or according to what the organ requires.
It is necessary to use one incision for small tumors and a wide incision or many incisions according to the size of large tumors. Some tumors may necessitate excising the skin and cutting it if it has become like rags.
And sometimes, as in many books and repositories, it has been reported that he died like this. Some have three-angled incisions, others are cut like the leaf of a willow tree for an arboreal tumor.
Among them, the round incision and the crescent-shaped incision are used. For those without a head like smooth, flat tumors, it is necessary to use only one simple incision. If the tumor is large and has accumulated for a long time with putrefaction, do not rush, lest all the pus comes out at once.
But some of it is taken out, then the tumor is tied for another day, and then some pus is also taken out again. This process is repeated gradually until all of it is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, a pregnant woman, an infant, or an elderly person, the animal spirit may be greatly depleted with the sudden exit of pus, sometimes leading to death without any sensation. Therefore, beware of this matter very carefully.
After performing the incision on these tumors, it is necessary to cleanse the wound and observe. If the tumor's cut is small or if there was only one simple incision, then use cotton wool soaked in olive oil for dressing. But if the tumor is large and many incisions were made,
it is necessary to insert a wick into each incision so that they reach one another. If some of the skin or flesh around the tumor has been cut off, it is necessary to fill it with dry cotton wool soaked in olive oil or linen bandages and tie it tightly for three days.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar mixed in a linen cloth to absorb the blood and apply it to the bleeding site repeatedly.
If bleeding persists, it is advisable not to resort to the treatments and remedies mentioned in various parts of this book or the sections. If it occurs during winter and the location has many nerves, one should apply poultices made from hot water and oil.
And apply them to the location. If it occurs in summer and the location is fleshy, then the poultices should be applied with cold water and oil or water mixed with oil. All of this should be cold until on the third day, as mentioned earlier, one should remove the tumor and cleanse it.
And employ similar treatments until it heals, for this is the general knowledge of treating tumors. As for specific methods, I have detailed each tumor and its treatment method concisely.

Chapter Forty-Nine
On incising a tumor appearing due to an artery or vein, called an aneurysm
If the artery is injured and the skin above it becomes adherent, a tumor often appears due to this. Similarly, an aneurysm may also appear in a vein accompanied by swelling and a tumor.
The signs by which it is known whether the tumor and aneurysm are from an artery or a vein: if from an artery, the tumor will be elongated and collected deep in the body. If you press on the tumor with your finger, you will feel as if it has a creaking sound.
A tumor from a vein will be round and appear on the surface of the body. Incising these tumors is dangerous, especially if they are located in the armpit, groin, neck, or other many parts of the body where they are very large. It is necessary to avoid treating them with caustic iron.
As for those that are located in the limbs or head, it is also necessary to avoid treating them with caustic iron. If it is an aneurysm of the facial artery, make a longitudinal incision through the skin, then open the incision with forceps and remove the artery from its sheaths.
Then it is exposed, and a needle is inserted underneath it and passed through to the other side. The artery is tied with a doubled thread in two places as you were shown regarding the arteries of the testicles. Then the site between the ligaments is cut with scissors until all its blood flows out.
The tumor dissolves, then use the treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the tumor is from a laceration of the vein, then grasp as much of it as possible with the skin in your hand. Then insert a needle below the place where you grasped it and pass it through with a doubled thread until it exits on the other side. Then tie the tumor tightly on both sides with the thread ends, as you were taught regarding tying a grape. If you fear that the threads may slip, insert another needle underneath the tumor at the intersection of the first needle with another thread.
Pull the threads in four places, then cut open the tumor in its middle until its contents come out. Then cut off a small piece of skin and leave what was tied attached. Then place a dressing soaked in a mixture of honey and oil on it. Then apply the treatment with bandages and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
Regarding the tumor that results from nerve twisting.
This happens with tumors in arteries and veins, likewise with nerves when there is a blow or extreme fatigue, etc. This occurs more often in the joints of the hands and feet.

But it is under the skin and moves in all directions except front or back. Tumors in joints should not be treated with iron as this may cause bruising. Those on the head or forehead may be treated thus.
He lanced the skin with a scalpel; if the tumor was small, he grasped it with forceps and severed it from its origin. If it was large, he attached it with a hook and sliced it off, then removed it and sutured the wound, treating it until it healed.
Chapter Fifty-One
Regarding the removal of hemorrhoids in the abdomen
Many people have a condition called piles, where they develop fleshy growths resembling mushrooms in their anus and other parts of the body. These are often small but can sometimes become quite large, with swollen heads and thin stalks that resemble lips.
I saw a man with two large fleshy growths in his abdomen, resembling mushrooms; they were white and thin-stalked, with lips that had split open and leaked fluid continuously. I removed them, and one weighed about eighteen ounces while the other weighed about six.
When removing them, if the lesion is moist and the color of the polyp is white and wet with a thin stalk, cut it off with a wide scalpel. Keep caustic substances ready nearby in case much blood flows out during removal.
If much blood flows out, cauterize it. If the patient is afraid of being cut with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the polyp that has these characteristics. Leave it for two days, then tighten the lead further each day until it falls off.
Gradually tighten the lead until the polyp breaks off and falls off by itself without pain. If the polyp has a thick stalk, especially if it is in the abdomen, there may be some fear and hesitation about cutting it off completely, but it can sometimes be cut partially or halfway.
Then cauterize it to prevent its return, then treat the wound until it heals. Beware of cutting a polyp that is dark-colored and insensitive to touch with an unpleasant appearance, as it may be a cancerous tumor. We will discuss cancer later on.
Chapter Fifty-Two
Regarding the treatment of anal fissures
The causes of hernia are many, either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen which protrudes the hernial ring or the gut, as mentioned elsewhere in cases of fissures, or from blood issuing from a vein or artery, as previously stated, or from wind trapped therein.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of the hernial ring, then the color of the swelling will resemble that of the body, it will be soft without pain, and it will change with changes in posture. If it is due to protrusion of the gut, its position will be more variable than what we have described. If you press on it with your fingers, it will disappear and then return. There may also be a gurgling sound associated with it, and it becomes more pronounced during bathing or severe exertion. If it is due to fluid accumulation, it will feel soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed with the hand, nor increases nor decreases in size, and if it is due to blood, then the swelling will appear dark-colored along with these signs. If it is due to a solid growth of flesh, then the swelling will be hard and fixed in size.
If it is due to wind, it will feel soft, and the procedure in such cases is to observe. If the hernia is caused by arterial or venous bleeding or wind, one should not hesitate to treat it, as there is fear and danger involved, as I have mentioned earlier in the chapter on tumors caused by arteries and veins.
If the hernia is due to the gut or hernial ring, then order the patient to stand upright with their back extended, and mark around the entire swelling with ink. Then order the patient to lie down on their back in front of you, then incise around the swelling with a wide blade at the marked location. Then insert a large sound through the center of the swelling upwards. Finally, tie the incision tightly with strong thread or silk.

Cut away the excess, for if you encounter an artery or vein, ligate it properly and return to your task. Or, if there is moisture present, cauterize the tumor as mentioned earlier and remove any blood or moisture found within it. Then treat the site with something that will heal it.

The Sixty-First Chapter.

Chapter Fifty-Five
In treating varicose veins
The occurrence of these varicosities is due to a fissure that appears in the extended peritoneum on the abdomen, near the sides of the navel, from which the vein extends to one of the testicles.
This hernia occurs either due to a rupture of the peritoneum or its extension, and both types have many causes such as trauma, jumping, shouting, lifting heavy objects, etc. If it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, it usually happens gradually over a long period of time.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum, it happens suddenly and the tumor appears deep under the skin. However, if it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, it occurs gradually over time and there may be pain present from the beginning. The tumor will appear different externally near the skin.
This happens when the vein and its tributaries protrude from the peritoneum, exiting the body. This condition is called varicocele-thrombosis. The vein may also contain feces which can become trapped there, leading to the patient's demise.
This condition causes severe pain and cramping, especially during exertion. It is dangerous to use iron for its treatment, so caution should be exercised. The treatment consists of having the patient manually push the protruding vein back into the abdomen if possible.
Then, have the patient lie on their back in front of you and raise their legs. Then order the servant to pull up the testicle and let go of the adhesion behind it completely, then search with your finger to ensure there is nothing twisted from the intestine in the white, solid peritoneum; if you find anything, push it back into the abdomen below.
Then take a needle with a thick thread that has been twisted from ten threads, insert it into the end of the peritoneum under the scrotal skin near the incision, then cut off the excess thread ends so that there are four threads remaining. So that no inflammation occurs from it and also a second ligature is made outside the first one, about two fingers away from it, and after these two ligatures, leave about one finger's length of peritoneum under the scrotal skin.
So that no inflammation occurs from it and also a second ligature is made outside the first one, about two fingers away from it, and after these two ligatures, leave about one finger's length of peritoneum under the scrotal skin.
