Chapter Ninety-Six
In Castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I do not intend to mention it in this book of mine. However, I have mentioned it for two purposes: firstly, so that the doctor may know about it if asked; and secondly, because we often need to castrate some animals for our own benefit.
Castration can be done in two ways: either by cautery or by cutting. The method using cautery is performed as follows: sit the animal in warm water until its genitals relax and soften.
Then cauterize it with your hand until it melts away and cannot be felt. As for castration by cutting and severing, it is preferable to hold the animal and squeeze its scrotum with your left hand, then tie the cords and make a single cut on each testicle.
So that when the testicles come out, cut them off after peeling them, leaving nothing of the membranes except for the thin membrane that is on the vessels. This method of castration is better than the one done by cautery.
Because cauterization may leave some testicle tissue, which causes the animal to desire copulation. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy
On treating hermaphrodites
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two types: one where it appears externally as a vulva with hair, resembling a woman's vagina; the other is located inside the scrotum, where urine may also flow from it.
In women, there is one type: a small penis-like structure grows above the vulva on the pubic area, resembling a man's glans penis. Both types are treated as follows:
The two types of men and the one type of women should have those superfluous flesh cut until their scars disappear, then treat them like other surgeries until they heal. But for the second type of men who emits urine in a foreskin, there is no procedure nor any cure at all.
The Seventy-First Chapter
Regarding circumcision and cutting away excess flesh from women's genitals.
The foreskin may exceed its natural state, becoming thickened and repulsive in appearance, and in some women it may become so large that it resembles a man's penis and can be used for sexual intercourse.
It is necessary to hold the foreskin with your hand or forceps and cut it, without cutting too deeply at its root lest there be bleeding.
Then treat it like other surgeries until it heals, and as for the excess flesh, it is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And it may prolapse externally, resembling a hernia. Therefore, some early physicians called it 'the disease of the prolapse,' and it should be cut like circumcision, regardless of its size, and treated until it heals.
The Seventy-Second Chapter
Regarding the treatment of hemorrhoids.
Hemorrhoids are when a woman's vulva is not ruptured or has a small rupture, and this may be either natural at birth or acquired due to some cause, with the acquired type being either excess flesh that has grown.
Or thin or thick peritoneum, located either inside the womb, on its sides, above or below it, preventing sexual intercourse and pregnancy, as well as childbirth, and possibly preventing menstruation. The knowledgeable midwife can determine this.
If the obstruction is visible and close by, search for it with your fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is made of thin peritoneum and is near the labia, immediately cut it open by making an incision like the opening of the vulva, then place your thumbs from both hands inside.
The woman lies on her back with her legs spread, then she pulls the labia forcefully until the thin peritoneum is torn open and the obstruction opens. Then take some wool soaked in oil and place it on the spot, and have sexual intercourse every day to prevent the wound from healing again.
If the spleen is thick and dense, it should be cut with a wide scalpel resembling a fig leaf. If the bridge is made of flesh, adhere it to the clamps and sever it; have remedies at hand to stop bleeding without cauterization, such as aloe, shilajit, and wax mixed with egg whites.
Then use a wide brass tube to prevent the wound from closing quickly, hold it in place for several days and apply a dry linen dressing. Then treat according to other methods of healing until it heals; sometimes there may be another flesh growth in the uterus which should not be cut as described.
If it is not a cancerous tumor, the cancerous tumor that occurs in the uterus should never be treated with iron.
Chapter Seventy-three.
In treating piles, warts and red blisters appearing on women's genitals.
As for piles, it is the dilation of blood vessels until they discharge much blood continuously. When piles become wartsy, I have mentioned its types and signs elsewhere.
I say that piles and warts, if they are in the depth of the uterus and have not appeared externally, there is no treatment with iron for them. But those that appear at the mouth of the uterus can be treated.
It is forbidden for a woman to enter a cold house, then stretch the warts with a nail or rough cloth and cut them at their roots. Then apply one of the blood-stopping drugs without cauterization.
Such as aloe, shilajit, wax and the like. Then raise her legs against the wall for a while then dip them in cold water. If bleeding persists, seat her in a tub of samag, pomegranate peel, carob pods and the like. Then take some barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar.
And anoint her back with it. If bleeding stops, wrap the lumbar spine and breasts tightly without restriction. Then take some cotton wool, dip it in a decoction of purslane or colostrum or cucumber juice mixed with carob syrup and apply it to the spot.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with an ointment until it heals. As for a red boil, it resembles the heads of thorns in appearance and should not be cut according to what was mentioned regarding warts. Treat it until it heals.
The Forty-seventh Chapter.
Regarding the tumor that appears in the uterus.
A variety of tumors may appear in the uterus, such as cancer, calcified growths, ulcers, warts, eating sores, polyps, varicose veins, fissures, and hot swellings. All these diseases and their types and symptoms have been mentioned in the classification.
If we discuss in this chapter the hot tumor that appears in the uterus, especially if it is one of those tumors that gather and become hard like iron, do not examine it. If the pain of the tumor at its onset is severe with inflammation and fever.
And if it appears red, do not hurry with its incision. Treat it with what promotes ripening until these symptoms subside. Then, the woman should sit on a chair with two seats and lean on her back.
And she should gather her legs towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs wide apart. Her arms should be placed under her knees and tied together with a suitable band.
Then, the midwife should sit on the right side. She should use the instrument for opening the mouth of the uterus, whose image is shown later. When using this instrument, it is important to estimate the depth of the woman's uterus so that what enters from the instrument does not exceed its depth.
If the instrument hurts her, then if it is too deep for the uterus, place pads on the lip of the cervix to prevent the whole instrument from entering the depth of the uterus. The loop that runs through the instrument should be at the top side.
And you hold the instrument while the assistant turns the loop until the cervix opens. If the tumor appears and is touched by hand, it should be cut at its head with a wide scissor if it is soft. Once all the contents have been expelled.
It is necessary that a soft wick, soaked in rose oil or green oil with some wax, be inserted into the wound and the wick should exit from the opening of the neck of the uterus. Then, pure wool soaked in boiling water mixed with bread dough should be applied externally on the uterus and the groin.
Then treat it two days later with the ointment suitable for this until it heals.
And the uterus and wound may be washed by injecting honey water, then water boiled with celery or long pepper seeds. Then return to using the ointment if the discharge is hidden and entering the uterus.
It is not permissible to treat it with cauterization by iron, but rather treat it as mentioned in this section.
Chapter Seventy-Five.
On teaching midwives how to treat live fetuses that emerge abnormally.
The midwife must first know the signs of natural labor. These include: if the woman begins to push downwards and feels an urge to breathe deeply, and she finds relief in her pains.
If the fetus emerges quickly, know that this is a normal birth with the head emerging first, followed by the umbilical cord or attached to it. If you see these signs, do not press on her belly to speed up its emergence.
But if it emerges head-first, the umbilical cord will also come out with it and should be cleaned thoroughly. Any other position is abnormal and reprehensible, as the fetus may emerge feet-first or hands-first before the head.
The midwife must be skilled and careful in all these matters to avoid errors and mistakes.
And I have described all these shapes and the remedies for each, so that you may refer to them when a fetus emerges head-first but the woman is struggling with labor pains and has weak contractions. Then make her sit on a chair and tell women to support her.
Wash the uterus with cooked fenugreek and wet cloths. Then, insert a small probe between her fingers and cut the umbilical cord until it bleeds, squeezing her belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, one should inject the woman with frankincense juice mixed with castor oil. Then she is to be ordered to sneeze and cough using henbane, while her mouth and nose are held closed. The child will then exit immediately. If a hand of the fetus comes out first, they should gently push it back slightly.
If entry is not attained, place the woman on a pulpit and elevate her legs above. Then shake the pulpit on the ground while the woman holds on to prevent falling. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and gently pull them out, as the fetus emerges feet first. Once the fetus emerges feet first, do not push its legs up but turn it slightly until it is positioned correctly, then take one of its legs and gently extend it.
If they come out up to the thighs, command her to sneeze and cough using henbane so that it comes out. If it does not come out as described, gently fold the fetus back until it returns to its natural position, at which point it will exit smoothly. If it still does not come out despite all these measures,
Take equal amounts of saliva from the dog's mouth, frankincense juice, and castor oil. Mix them with tragacanth gum powder in a mortar and pestle until smooth. Then apply it to the woman's vulva and lower abdomen before placing her in warm water up to the navel.
If you see that her lower part has softened, prepare a compress of myrrh for her to hold. If she holds it for some time, seat her on a chair and make her sneeze while holding her mouth and nose closed, gently pressing her lower abdomen. The fetus will then emerge immediately, coming out on its knees and hands.
If it comes out in this manner, gently insert its hands, then have the woman lie on her back with her hips elevated and legs hanging down while lying on a bed. She should bring her knees up to her chest, then gently press her abdomen. Then remove what is under her legs of the cushions until she is elevated at the bottom. If the child does not come out, then take both of her legs entirely and shake them vigorously, then press upon the uterus a little bit by little until the child ascends above. Then the midwife enters her hand and arranges the child a little bit by little.
The woman is commanded to squat so that the child comes out. The exit of the child is resisted and delivered with one hand, holding it in its hand if it cannot be returned. If she cannot return her hand, then make the woman stand up and walk. If she cannot walk, then lie down on.
Then lie her on her bed and shake her legs vigorously. Then use the suppository that I have described from the medications. If it takes effect, then seat her on a chair. Then treat in returning the hand and arranging the child in the natural way, and make the woman sneeze, commanding her to squat until it comes out.
The exit of the child is upon its back with hands spread out and its face towards its mother's back. It is necessary that the midwife takes hold of the hand of the child, then turns it a little bit by little and arranges it gently. When it stands still, then apply on it what we have described from ointments and suppositories before its birth.
Then make her move a little bit. When she does that, seat her on the chair and command her to lean towards the right side. Then make her squat and sneeze with henna, for it will come out standing upright on its hip.
If it descends to the mouth of the uterus in this manner along with the placenta, then cut the placenta with your finger until it is emptied of its moisture. If it sticks to the face and neck of the child, then cut it from its source so that it does not suffocate and die. Then push the child back inside until it assumes its proper position.
Command the woman to squat several times until it comes out easily. The exit of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins are often born and sometimes three or four may live, but this is rare. As for five, it is against nature and they never live.
[The twins] are surrounded by a single placenta, and though they may be many, they separate through a partition between them, fastened at the navel of each. They emerge like a single fetus, easily and naturally, or in an abnormal manner as we have described.
If some [twins] are difficult to deliver at birth, and thus deviate from the normal shapes we have mentioned, treat them as we have described. Let the midwife be gentle and proceed with care and patience, adapting her technique to each situation in order to ensure safety.
It has been reported that embryos in the womb may fall out, sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even more than ten. I have personally witnessed a woman who aborted seven and another who aborted fifteen, all of them formed.
Praise be to Allah the All-Knowing. This is all knowledge that should be possessed by a midwife, for she may encounter such cases one day and thus deal with them appropriately.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
Regarding the delivery of a stillborn fetus.
If you have treated the stillborn fetus according to what was mentioned in the previous section, but it did not emerge and you were forced to intervene manually, then look: if the woman is strong and healthy with no signs of symptoms that cause fear of harm, she should lie down on her back on a bed.
And her head should be tilted downward and her legs raised. An assistant should hold or tie down her body on both sides so that her body does not move when the fetus is pulled out.
Then moisten the mouth of her womb with damp cotton wool soaked in honey, gum tragacanth, and ground linseed seeds. The midwife should also moisturize her hand with these substances before gently inserting it into the womb to locate suitable positions for inserting the forceps among the parts of the fetus.
Then look: if the fetus is presenting headfirst, insert the forceps into its eyes, throat, mouth, palate, cheeks, beard, or armpits, or near its ribs or hips.
If his descent is upon his legs, then it is in the groin and middle buttocks. It is necessary to hold the cannula with the right hand and its curve should be between the fingers of the left hand. Then gently insert the hand and pierce the cannula into some of those places mentioned.
Until you reach something empty, then pierce another cannula or a third one if necessary, so that the traction is even and does not lean towards any direction. Then extend it in a straight manner, but do not extend it only in a straight line; rather move it towards all directions.
It is not easy to extract it as done in the case of an abscess. Between this, it is necessary to relax the traction. If any part is obstructed, then grease some fingers with oil and insert them from that side to rotate what is obstructed. If some of the fetus comes out, then move the cannulas to other places.
Raise it a little and do this until the entire fetus comes out. If one hand comes out before the other and cannot be pushed back due to its tightening, then wrap a cloth around it and pull it until it all comes out, then cut it.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow, and you should do this with the other hand and the feet as well. If the head of the fetus is large and there is pressure on it during delivery or if there is water accumulated in its head, then insert a pointed scissors between the fingers.
And cut the head with it so that water comes out or use the instrument called 'Al-Mishdak' whose image is shown along with other instruments in the following chapter.
Similarly, if the fetus has a large head, you should cut the skull or crush it with 'Al-Mishdak' as mentioned earlier. Then extract the bones using forceps. If the head comes out and is stuck at the neck, then make an incision until the chest cavity collapses.
If the chest adheres at that time, if the chest does not adhere and there is no way out, then cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is warm or there is heat in it, then bathe it until the moisture comes out.
If the fetus is on its back, its extraction is easy and pushing it towards the mouth of the womb is simple. But if it is compressed in the abdomen or chest, it must not be pulled out by a cloth wrapped around the hand until the abdomen or chest has been opened so that what is inside can flow out.
If the rest of the organs have been removed and the head has returned but is obstructed, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, she should enter her hand into the neck of the womb, seek out the head with it, grasp it with her fingers and pull it towards the mouth of the womb. Then she should insert a tube or two tubes and pull it out with them.
If the mouth of the womb has adhered to a hot tumor that is projecting, it should not be handled roughly. Rather, at this point, moist compresses should be used and poultices applied, sitting in warm water that relaxes and softens.
And if the fetus is on its side, if possible it should be laid flat using what has been mentioned for live fetuses. But if this is not possible, then it should be cut and removed. Nothing of the afterbirth should be left inside at all. After you have finished treating it, use what has been mentioned for the treatment of hot tumors that appear in the wombs.
If bleeding appears, let her bathe in constricting waters and use other treatments. I have seen a woman who became pregnant, but the fetus died inside her, then she conceived again afterwards.
Then the other fetus also died, and after a long time she developed a tumor in her womb which swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her and treated her for a long time without the wound healing. Therefore, I applied some strong drawing ointments.
So a bone came out from the place, then after some days another bone came out. I was amazed by this because the belly is not a place where there are bones. I thought that they were bones of the dead fetus. So I searched the wound and found many bones of the head of the fetus.
I continued this until I extracted many bones from it, and the woman was in the best of health. She remained so for a considerable time, with only a slight discharge issuing from the place. This is the remarkable case I have reached.
This contains knowledge and assistance for what the practicing physician attempts by hand in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.

This dilator, with which books are leveled, has screws at both ends; however, this screw should be gentler than the dilator and made either of ivory or ebony. Each wooden part should have a thickness of two fingers.
Each has a length of one and a half cubits, with two projections in the middle, made of the same wood, each about half a cubit long or slightly more, with a width of two fingers or slightly more. These are the projections.
These are the ones that enter into the mouth of the womb to dilate it when using the screws. Here is another tool for this purpose, gentler and lighter:
It is made of ivory or ebony in the shape of tongs, except that it has two projections at its tip, each about one cubit long and two fingers wide. When you wish to dilate the womb with it,

Chapter Eighty-Seven:
In expelling the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, order the midwife to grasp herself, then plug her with a tampon and pull your hand on its mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, otherwise take as much as you can.
And pierce the lid of the pot with a hole, place in it opened herbs like fennel and rue and hyssop and basil and silique and celery and celandine each or some of these drugs and immerse them in water and put them on fire then place one end of a silver tube on the hole of the pot's lid and the other end in the mouth of the uterus.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then plug as we have said. If the placenta remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in oil of rose or spittle.
Then she inserts her hand into the vagina and searches for the placenta. If it is found, grasp it and gently pull it out. If it is attached deeply in the uterus, insert the hand as described until you find the placenta, then gently pull it out with care.
So that the uterus does not fall out due to excessive pulling, but rather move it gently to the sides right and left, then increase the amount of pulling gradually until it comes out and detaches. If the mouth of the uterus is constricted, we have described the treatment with plugging and pot treatment and herbs.
If despite all that has been described it does not come out, beware of violence in pulling again, but rather tie what has come out from it to the mouth of the uterus to the woman's thigh, then anoint her with the four ointment plaster. It will rot it after a few days.
And it dissolves and comes out until it has rotted, for then a bad smell rises from it to the stomach and head, which harms the patient. Therefore, it should not be used.
And some predecessors have tried using incense of herbs and dried figs, an image of the apparatus used for vaporizing it in cases of menstrual obstruction and retained placenta, etc.
It is made of brass or copper, shaped like a funnel. The narrow end is placed over the vulva and the wide end over fire containing incense on embers. Hold it until the incense finishes, then replace with fresh incense.
Chapter Ninety-Seven
In treating an unperforated anal fistula.
Many boys are born with an unperforated anus, which is plugged by a thin membrane. It is not permissible for the midwife to pierce this membrane with her finger, but rather she should use a sharp probe to avoid harming the muscle.
Then place on it a sponge soaked in syrup and oil, then treat with ointment until healed. If you fear that it may close again, insert a brass tube into the hole and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
And if the anus becomes obstructed due to healing of a wound or tumor, do not cut into that scar tissue. Rather, treat it with the aforementioned treatment and ointment.
Chapter Eighty
Regarding the treatment of fistulas that occur below.
The fistulas that occur below are complex and hardened, near the anus externally or in the space on one side. They may be singular or plural.
If that complexity opens, a clear watery discharge or thin pus may flow from it. Some of these fistulas may extend to the bladder and rectum, while others do not. The connected ones can be identified by the passage of feces and wind during defecation.
And sometimes worms may come out of it. If they are in the space, they may extend to the bladder and urethra, or to the hip joint and scrotum. Also, those connected to the anus can be identified without being connected.
Insert your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe made of copper or iron into the bladder. If there is no obstruction in the bladder, then insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair probe if there is.
If you do not feel the probe or hair with your finger and there is no release of feces, gas, or discharge as mentioned earlier, then know that it is obstructed. Proceed to treatment.
If the bladder is open into the bladder or the urethra, then its discharge will be through urine. If it's open to the hip joint or the prostate gland, then the probe will reach there.
If it is not obstructed by bone, and there is pain on one side with continuous discharge of pus that does not improve with treatment and does not adhere with ointment, then these open bladders are incurable.
And the treatment for these open bladders is futile, as those who attempt it are ignorant doctors. However, those that are not open and not chronic can be cured by iron, as I will describe and have proven.
Place the patient on their back with legs raised and knees bent. Insert a copper or lead probe into the urethra until it reaches the bladder, so you know where to stop. If the patient feels the probe near the anus, do not insert your finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe has entered by itself, exposed and without feeling any resistance between your finger and it, then know for certain that it is open. Do not waste effort on it, as there is no cure for it, as we have said.
It has been said that some people are cured by heating a thin ironing stone according to the size of the bladder and inserting it into the urethra until it reaches near the anus, then repeating this two or three times.
Until you know that all the excess, thick flesh resembling a bird's quill has burnt off. Then treat with hot oil to remove the burnt flesh and apply adherent ointments. If it heals.
Otherwise, he will not recover without it forever. If you inserted the trocar but it did not reach your finger inside the abscess because there was a thick barrier of flesh or peritoneum between them and you saw the pulsation beneath the skin then tear the skin from its beginning. Then, as you pass through the incision with the trocar being in the pulsation until you reach where the tip of the trocar ended and it gets released and falls off. Then cleanse those rotten flesh resembling a feather pipe leaving nothing of it.
Then treat it with caustic ointments until it heals. If blood overpowers you and hinders your work in cutting that flesh, then the best thing to do quickly is cauterize or use a sharp medicine because cauterizing by fire has two advantages: burning the excess flesh.
Then stop the bleeding and dry the moisture, then treat it with a linen bandage dipped in butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until it becomes inflamed. Then remove those burnt flesh through pus. Then dress the wound with caustic ointments that soften hard flesh.
And these are the medicines that cause coagulation and drying. If it heals, then it is healed, and there will be no recurrence. But if the pulsation has extended to the depth of the abscess and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the abscess and search with it.
If you feel the trocar and there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh between it, and it was close to penetrating, then its treatment is only by patience and hope. And in this case, one of three methods should be used: cauterization as mentioned earlier.
Or you can cut it until you reach its bottom so that you can insert the linen bandage and treat from nearby without cutting too much to avoid damaging the muscle surrounding the abscess, causing uncontrollable passage of feces. Then treat it as mentioned before, perhaps it will heal.
Or pierce that barrier after cutting it near the abscess as mentioned earlier, either with the trocar or another sharp-pointed instrument. Then cleanse all the rotten flesh in the incision as much as you can.
Then, sew up the entire wound along with the split and upper mouth until it closes, leaving the anus open inside the rectum. This will be easier for the patient, although the anus may remain sutured in this manner.
If the speculum is inserted into the anus and leans towards the side of the rectum towards the body's surface with the skin, then use a pierced speculum like the carpenter's awl in this fashion:
Insert a thread made of five or similar strands into the speculum, then insert the speculum with the thread into the anus until it reaches its depth. If it protrudes at the edge of the rectum from inside near.
Then pull out the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the rectum and pulling out the end of the thread. Gather all its ends and tie them, leaving it for a day or two. Whenever the thread cuts through the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues between the ends of the threads fall off and drop, then treat the wound until it heals.
If the mouth of the anus is not protruding, then protrude it as much as possible without going too deep lest you cut the muscle. Then treat it as mentioned until it heals.
The pointed end of the spinal lancet should be very sharp to incise the anus, while the other end should not be sharp lest it cut unnecessarily, as you can see:
Chapter One Hundred and Eighty.
On the incision of haemorrhoids that bleed profusely, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds: either they are inside the rectum resembling red grapes, or they resemble raisins and vary in size, with blood flowing from them constantly.
And they may be single or numerous, and may be external to the rectum, with their tips protruding except for those that are outside the rectum which are usually less moist, secreting yellow water or little blood, appearing always pale in color like the skin.
The treatment for those inside the rectum is to order the patient to evacuate and cleanse until the hemorrhoids appear. Then quickly grab them with forceps or pinch them with your fingers, and cut them at their base. If the forceps do not adhere due to moisture and relaxation.
Remove it with a coarse cloth or a piece of softened leather or pull it out with your fingers, then cut it. Afterwards, sprinkle some sharp salts on it to serve as cautery or proceed as mentioned earlier under the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with other treatments until it heals. If the womb does not come out, inject the patient with a mixture containing a little honey to wash out what is inside the womb and help it come out quickly when the patient empties. Then, tie off the prolapsed uterus.
Either take it with your fingers or suspend it with forceps and cut it, then treat it as mentioned earlier until it heals. If you dislike cutting with iron, use a curved needle instead.
Then pull the mole upwards and pierce its base on the other side with a needle, wrap the ends of the thread under the needle while it is inserted, tighten the thread pulling the mole tightly, tie the knot and remove the needle. Do this for all moles but leave one unpunctured.
To discharge some blood from it, then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or cypress oil on the womb and leave the patient still until it falls. If the prolapsed uterus falls off, treat it with ointments and other treatments until it heals.
As for treating piles, it often appears due to inflammation and constipation. If it persists without improvement despite medication, scrape off the hard skin with a razor or your fingers until it becomes soft and moist, and remove the upper layers that prevent adhesion.
Then treat it until it heals as usual. If it does not heal, scrape it harder than before until it bleeds well and peels off, then treat it again; it will heal.
Chapter Two Eighty.
On the treatment of ingrown and non-ingrown nails, hard moles, and warts.
A screw is nothing but a round knot resembling the head of a nail, found in all parts of the body, especially at the soles of the feet and toes. Pain is felt from it during walking.
It is necessary to incise around the screw, grasp it with tweezers or forceps and remove it from its root, then treat it as mentioned earlier in this chapter. As for dry warts, each one of them is a small rough, diffuse, round elevation on the surface of the body.
Warts often appear on the hands of children. Their treatment involves squeezing them and cutting them off, or removing them by tying a silk thread or hair around them until they fall off. Alternatively, you may cauterize them with fire or apply a caustic drug as mentioned earlier.
The inverted and non-inverted warts may be removed using the instrument mentioned earlier in the treatment of the ant. The ant is a small, diffuse, thick node on the surface of the body that goes deep into it. When it gets cold, one feels something similar to the bite of an ant.
In the body as a whole, and more commonly in the hands, there is an anthrax. Its treatment involves taking a quill from an ostrich or eagle feather and making it into a tube around the anthrax until its circumference fits snugly. Then rotate your hand with the tube to incise around the anthrax.
And it goes deep into the flesh and removes the anthrax from its root. Alternatively, you may use a copper or iron tube in this manner:
The upper part of the tube is closed and flattened to facilitate gripping and twisting it with the fingers. Alternatively, you may cauterize the anthrax as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Chapter Eighty-Three: On the various instruments used for injecting remedies into cases of hemorrhoids, diarrhea, and dropsy.
The syringe may be made of silver, bronze, or polished or chased copper. They are made in various sizes according to the users' needs. Syrup used for treating young children, especially those with narrow or obstructed anuses, is quite gentle. It resembles a large syringe.
The upper part that holds the catheter is wide with a stop at its entrance for the catheter as you see. Its lower end, which enters the rectum, is smooth and rounded on one side there are two small holes, while on the other side there is one.
The diameter of the hole should be equal to or slightly thicker than the thickness of the reed. The catheter into which medicine is inserted, made from an animal's bladder or young goat's bladder, should resemble a small funnel with a width of about one shitra (a unit of measurement).
And it should be pierced with many holes, like a sieve, with one hole every fingertip thickness apart. Then, insert into these holes a closely woven thread of ten or twelve yarns folded over and gather the catheter like a funnel. After putting medicine inside, hold the top of the syringe tightly between the two stones with a tight grip. Then, squeeze it to inject. This is an illustration of the catheter and the hole.
And the method of administration is to fill the rectum or bladder with the medication while it is warm, taking care not to make it too hot or too cold. Then, the patient lies on their back and lifts their legs upwards.
And place a cloth or pad underneath him. Then, grease the tip of the catheter with oil, egg white, or barley mucilage, and insert it gently into the rectum. Then, a servant takes hold of the catheter with both hands and squeezes it firmly until all its contents are emptied into the intestines.
Then, remove the catheter and have the patient bring their legs to the wall. If the medication causes the need for evacuation, they should hold it back as much as possible. If the medication remains with them throughout the night, it will be more effective in treating piles (hemorrhoids). If used for treating diarrhea and causing urgency of bowel movements, repeat the enema until recovery.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, use a very gentle and smooth syringe similar to those used for children. This is an illustration of a gentle syringe with its skin drawn tight:
Chapter Eighty-Four
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned in the section on treating injuries like burns, and I am here mentioning their treatment by hand and drugs to perfection; so I say that wounds differ according to the thing by which the wound is caused and according to the place where it occurs.
The things by which wounds are caused are many, such as a blow from a stone or a cut from a sword or knife or a thrust from a spear or stake, or an arrow and the like among many other things.
And as for wounds according to their location on the body, there is the wound that occurs on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and the like of the organs.
And I will describe the treatment of some wounds as a guide and rule for other wounds, and I will begin with simple head wounds particularly because composite ones will be mentioned in the first chapter of this book.
So I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture of the bone, then if it is from a blow of a stone or the like and has only torn the skin but the wound is large and you fear hot inflammation for the patient, hurry and shave around the wound immediately. Do not delay that, and let out as much blood from him as possible if it does not hinder cauterization particularly if the blood coming out of the wound is slow, and let the cauterization be done on the opposite side of the wound.
And warn the patient against overeating and drinking, especially against rough foods. If cauterization is hindered by some obstacle, then let him reduce his food intake and avoid meat and drinks. And if hot inflammation occurs on the wound, apply a cloth soaked in olive oil alone or with the decoction of rue to it.
And if you have prevented the hot inflammation, then apply to the wound if it is fresh with its own blood and do not expose it to dry air which I will describe to you later. But if it has been exposed to dry air, apply some ointments to it.
Until pus comes out, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and is from a sword cut or the like and its lips do not meet with the forceps, then sew it up according to what I will describe in sewing abdominal wounds.
If the skin has been exposed to bone and adhered but its attachment is only slight, cut it off. Then treat the wound with medicines that induce strong scar tissue to replace the skin. Order the patient to consume nourishing foods such as brains and game for strength.
If foulness occurs in the skin without adhering to the bone, cut off all the affected portion and throw it away. Then treat the wound. If there is an artery or vein bleeding in these wounds and the blood does not cease with medication, search for the artery.
If it does not heal, cauterize it with the scalpel and bind it. If necessary, when the previous methods are ineffective, evacuate until bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and numerous, simply smearing this ointment on them will suffice.
Before exposure to air, the ointment is made by taking one part of frankincense, two parts of gallnuts, and three parts of slaked lime or unslaked lime. Grind all ingredients together, sift, and apply to the wound, pressing firmly until it adheres well.
The wound is tied with thread, then a bandage is applied from above and left untouched until it heals. The lime can be used alone if frankincense and gallnuts are not available.
This action can also be performed on its veins in many large wounds, if correctly applied and tightened, provided the wounds are fresh with blood. However, if they have already been exposed to air, especially in summer.
There is no point in applying this ointment if you then apply other prepared ointments or a gruel of barley flour made with water and honey to encourage pus formation, as it will still need to be treated with other remedies until healed.
If a fracture of the bone occurs with the wound and it is minor, treat it with traction using weights. I have mentioned the treatment of skull fractures in the chapter on head surgery; there is no difference between treating wounds of the neck and simple wounds of the head.
If he has cut a nerve from the neck or an artery, if it is a nerve then there is no treatment for it other than to abstain from its care with something that causes severe constriction such as ginger and pitch and similar substances because they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
No cold substance should be placed on the wound, for the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain which is the noblest organ, therefore the remedies used for its treatment should be unctuous such as washed date honey diluted with sweet water or rose oil or turtledove grease.
If the wound is washed also with sweet water and other mineral stones in this manner, and if the wound is large then use suturing or press its lips with compresses until it heals.
If the wound has a depth and there is a cavity beneath it where pus has collected, then drain it at its lowest point. If an artery has been severed in the wound and blood is flowing profusely, cauterize it or tie it off or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has cut some or all of the tonsils and the uvula is severed, then gather the skin edges with sutures on the tonsillar pillar without touching the tonsils but instead preserve their natural shape, then tie it gently and leave it for a period.
Then treat it with remedies that cause constriction and drying until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, search for it. If there are bone fragments in it, remove them all or as many as possible. If you cannot, leave it until the wound softens and heals and makes its removal easy.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle on it the dusting powder we have described. If bone appears in the wound and it becomes hard and turns into a scab, treat it according to what is mentioned in this book under the chapter of chest surgery and that between the shoulders.
If a wound is from a spear or a knife and you see that it has depth, observe. If air comes out when the patient breathes, know that it is a fatal wound. If there is no depth and it is fresh with blood, do not apply dusting powder at first nor compress it lest blood becomes trapped in its depth.
It extends to the heart, killing the patient. But make in it an attractive plaster and dissolve it during the day twice. If no ointment is available, put in the mouth of the wound an old cloth to absorb its secretions. And let the patient sleep on the wound.
If no discharge occurs within it, even if three days or more have passed without causing the patient convulsion, palpitations, or shortness of breath and other conditions appear good, then know that the wound is sound. Treat it with ligature, along with other treatments until it heals.
If it cannot be seen and the discharge continues, know that it has become a fistula. Treat it from its opening; let the patient lie on his wound to drain it. If the wound is in the chest or back, treat it as mentioned earlier with suturing.
If it is large or deep, if small, search the wound. If it has affected the bone and pieces have broken off, hasten to remove those fragments if they are pure. Otherwise, leave them alone until...
If the wound is infected, it facilitates removing the fragments. As for other wounds in various organs, their treatment follows what has been mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Five.
In abdominal wounds and prolapse of intestines and its suturing.
A wound exposing the abdomen may be large or small, with varying degrees of difficulty; a large one may have intestines or several loops protruding, making their reinsertion and suturing difficult.
Even a small wound can pose another challenge, requiring more difficult reduction. If intestines do not protrude immediately upon injury and become edematous and thickened later, it becomes difficult to reduce them back into the abdomen. Therefore, the optimal wound is moderate in size as it does not pose this challenge like the two extremes.
Know that abdominal suturing has four aspects. The two common ones are suitable for abdominal and other bodily wounds, while the other two specialized ones are mainly used for abdominal closure but can also be applied to other wounds.
And I shall describe them for you one by one with explanation and clarification, so I say that if the wound is small and something from the intestines emerges and it is difficult to push back, this has two causes: either because of the smallness of the wound as we have said, or because the intestines were exposed to inflation due to the expulsion of air.
If it is like this, then it is necessary to warm it by dipping a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone or cooked with onion, celery, and fennel until the inflation dissolves.
And he may do this with a drink that has an astringent effect, which is better than water alone in dissolving the inflation. When you see that the inflation has dissolved, moisten the intestines with water cooked with rue or wild carrot, for it will facilitate its re-entry with ease.
If its return is still difficult after this treatment, then make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used for cutting ligatures. The first treatment, if successful, is better than the incision, and one should resort to incision only after necessity and failure of other remedies. This is an illustration of the instrument:
The one end of the instrument is bent and limited, while the other end is not limited. The thin tip should not be too sharp like a lancet, but rather somewhat blunt, resembling a small awl as you see here. If the wound becomes wider and the intestines enter, then its return should not be in its natural form.
And its specific place, if you can manage it, is the best. But if the wound is wide and located in the lower abdomen, then the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head. If it is located in the upper abdomen, then he should keep his head and chest raised above his feet.
And similarly, if the wound is on one of the sides of the abdomen, then your aim and intention should always be to keep the side with the surgery higher than the other side. This is also how great surgeries and medium surgeries should be performed.
And as for small surgeries, place him according to what you can manage. Then prepare beside you an assistant who will hold the wound with both hands and gather its edges together, revealing a little at a time for the suturer.
Here is the method of common suturing: Take one or several needles according to the width of the wound, then leave a thickness of skin like that of a finger nail at the edge of the cloth and insert a single needle without threading it with thread into the edge of the skin. And when sewing the peritoneum that is under the skin from inside until it appears from that side, having gathered both edges of the skin and the peritoneum together in four layers, then pull tight with a double thread around the needle on all sides so that the lips of the wound meet neatly.
Then leave a thickness like that of the finger again and insert another needle, then intertwine it with the thread as was done with the first needle, continuing thus until the entire wound is closed, using needles of moderate thickness.
For fine needles quickly cut the flesh and thick ones are difficult to penetrate the skin, so they should be of moderate thickness in both respects. Do not insert the needle too close to the edge of the skin.
Lest the flesh be quickly cut and the wound opens before healing, nor too far apart from stitching lest the wound refuses to heal. Then cut the ends of the needles so they do not hurt the patient during sleep, and make padding of linen cloth on each side to hold the needle ends.
And leave it until you know that the wound has healed. This method of stitching with needles is most effective in small wounds as they can be closed with one or two needles at most.
Or the method of second common stitching is to gather all four edges, namely the skin edge and the peritoneum edge, in one stitch using a needle with a medium thick thread, then when this needle has passed through all four edges, bring it back from the same side where you started.
So that the thread is interwoven from above the wound, making the stitching like the stitch used in sewing a purse, leaving a thickness of little finger between each stitch. This stitch is known to everyone.
With this stitching, the surgery was performed on a man in his abdomen who had been stabbed with a dagger. The cut of the surgery exceeded a span and approximately two spans of the middle intestine had prolapsed from his anus. The laceration was in the center of the abdomen.
He was discharged after his intestines remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in approximately fifteen days, and he was treated until he recovered fully. He lived many years afterward, active in all his affairs, despite physicians believing that he would never recover.
It is remarkable that I did not treat him with an ointment, as I was in a place where there were no available medications. Instead, I applied wet cotton twice daily to the wound and had it washed with honey water until it healed.
Of the two types of suturing, I recall one specifically as described by Galen: begin stitching from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside. Proceed straight through the skin and muscle along the entire length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on one side intact, inserted the needle into its other edge from inside to outside, and then passed it back through the same edge again from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side intact, inserting the needle into its other edge from inside to outside, and passing through it along with the peritoneum until you have pierced all of its edges.
Then begin again from this side, stitching with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side. Exit the needle at the nearby skin and then reinsert it into that same skin, stitching together the other edge of the peritoneum along with this edge of the omentum.
And exit the needle from the skin on this side, repeating this process until the entire wound is closed in this manner. The characteristic of this suturing is that it resembles the stitching of furriers sewing fur.
To stitch the peritoneum once on one side along with the skin edge and leave it unattached on the other side until empty, as Galen also described this second specific method of suturing.
He says that you should suture it in the manner that some healers do, by joining each part to its counterpart. The difficulty lies in bringing together the edges of the spleen and the edges of the liver.
You should insert the needle into the nearby edge of the liver from outside, and pass it through to the inside alone. Leave the edges of the spleen, then bring back the needle and pass it from outside to inside in both edges of the spleen.
And pass it from inside to outside in the other edge of the liver that is on the opposite side. This method of suturing is better than simple, common suturing, as this is what Galen has stated. Some have said that the abdomen can be sutured with two needles. This is so that you can pass a single thread through them both, starting by inserting the needle from your side and passing it to the other side. Then insert the other needle from the opposite side of it and pass it out on your side, just like sewing a bag.
Know that if the wound is in the center of the abdomen, its suturing will be more difficult than other parts of the belly. But as for treating the wound, its ruling is like that of other wounds, so if you catch it while it's still fresh with its blood before the air has altered it, return the intestines, suture and close it, then apply a meat poultice.
But if the wound has already been altered by the air, apply one of the ointments that you have prepared until it becomes inflamed and the sutures fall out and the spleen and liver adhere. Then treat it like any other wound until it heals.
If you do not have any remedies prepared, apply boiled cotton wool soaked in hot water to it from the beginning of inflammation twice a day as I have instructed you until it heals. For usually there is no other treatment needed for simple wounds except that you fear that the wound may affect the main organs.
It is recommended that you soak clean wool in moderately warm oil or rose fat and place it around the area between the armpit and thigh. If he feels pain or swelling in his abdomen, this often happens, so treat him with a laxative black drink.
If the gangrene has reached the intestine and caused a penetrating wound into its interior, know that the thick parts are easier to heal while the thin ones are more difficult.
And as for the intestine known as the 'fasting bowel,' it does not undergo healing through surgical intervention due to its abundance of vessels, bones, and thin tissue, and its close proximity to the nature of nerves. But if a prolapse occurs and is found still fresh and pliable, then it should be replaced according to how you would replace the intestine itself.
If it has passed some time and turned green or black, then do not tie it with a thread above the place where it has turned black lest it lead to bleeding. For there are vessels and arteries within the prolapse. Then cut off what is below that tie.
Leave the ends of the thread attached from below the wound, hanging outside so that it is easy for you to pull and remove them when the prolapse falls and the wound becomes inflamed. Concerning the wound that occurs in the intestine, if it is small...
...it can sometimes be tied in some people, as I have seen a man who was pierced with a lance in his abdomen, the wound being to the right of the stomach. The intestine became prolapsed and was protruding, emitting feces and wind. So I treated him without expecting recovery. I continued to treat him until he recovered and the site healed. When I saw that the site had healed, I feared some misfortune might happen inside him. However, nothing untowards occurred, and he remained in good health. It is said that this method is correct, as the patient can eat, drink, copulate, enter the bathroom, and serve himself. Some practitioners have mentioned that if a small wound appears in the intestine, it should not be sutured in this manner.
This method involves taking large-headed ants, then bringing the edges of the wound together and placing an ant with its mouth open on one edge. When it bites down and its head is cut off, it sticks and does not come apart. Then another ant is placed nearby.
She still does it by applying several ants according to the size of the wound, then she returns them and sutures the wound. Those heads remain stuck in the intestines until the intestines are healed and recover without causing any harm to the patient. It is also possible to suture the intestines with a fine thread.
After taking a thread from the sinew, one should remove it using tweezers. Then, tie a thin and twisted cotton thread to its end before inserting that thread into the needle. And thus, the sinew thread is used to suture the intestines and then returned into the abdomen. This method of suturing with ants and sinew is only done out of desperation and hope.
But if the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thinner intestines, there is no remedy or cure for it.
Chapter Eighty-Six.
In treating catarrh and sneezing.
Know that any wound or tumor, if it persists and deepens until it becomes an ulcer and does not heal, constantly oozing pus, is generally called 'catarrh' in any organ. We also call it 'nasal catarrh.'
It is indeed a thick, turbid, solid, white fluid without pain, having a cavity like that of bird feathers.
Therefore, some have named it 'feather' due to its similarity in appearance. At times, it may be moist and constantly ooze pus, while at other times, the moisture might cease. This moisture can sometimes be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
And as mentioned in the section on wounds, every ulcer that does not heal or become flesh-grown has one of nine causes: either insufficient blood in the body, or its thickness, or there being solid flesh inside it and at its edges preventing proper flesh growth. Or because the ulcer is foul and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is corrupt and its substance is of poor quality, or because the treatment used is inappropriate, or because there has been an outbreak of a contagious disease in the area, or due to some local peculiarity.
Or because the ulcer is foul and dirty, or because the ulcer itself is corrupt and its substance is of poor quality, or because the treatment used is inappropriate, or because there has been an outbreak of a contagious disease in the area, or due to some local peculiarity. In the city of Zaragoza, where diseases take time to mature and the healing of tumors is delayed naturally, either due to the presence of a single bone or multiple bones, I have demonstrated all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medications in the division [of this work].
I should now inform you about the treatment of nasal polyps and catarrh, which occurs through manual operation. Know that polyps can occur in all parts of the body, some ending in large veins and arteries, others in nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib from the ribs, or to a vertebra from the vertebrae of the back, or to a joint from the compound joints such as the hand and foot, and there are those that approach a principal organ...
If any of these polyps are of this nature, they are among the difficult diseases to heal that do not accept treatment and effort due to their difficulty and ignorance. However, if there are polyps that have not reached these places mentioned above, one can hope for and strive to treat them.
To determine this, take a probe made of copper or iron. If the polyp is straight, search for it with this. However, if there is a bend in the polyp, use a thin lead probe, as lead softens its body.
If upon entering it turns towards the bend, and if the polyp has many openings that cannot be discerned by the probe, then inject one of its mouths with liquid. The moisture will follow towards the other mouths and flow.
Then, thoroughly examine in whichever way you can to determine if there is bone or nerve nearby, or if the base of the polyp is far or near, or if it is a single polyp with many openings. Then, according to your ability to investigate and explore its presence, press on the spot with your finger.
And similar signs and causes that indicate the presence of a tumor. Once you have confirmed all this, then proceed with confidence to the treatment, which is to look (examine) if it happens.
The surgeon appears nearby or in a healthy remote location from the joint, nerve, artery, vein, or one of the places mentioned earlier. He incises according to the aforementioned description and removes what is inside: the accumulation of pus, rotten flesh, and excess flesh.
And whatever grows in his mouth, such as flesh or warts and the like, treat it until it heals. If the wound is deep and straight, it is preferable to cut into its depth as much as possible before removing all the rotting flesh.
Then use the wick dressing with caustic drugs and insert it into the depth of the wound that you grasp with an iron instrument, doing so repeatedly until the medication consumes all the remaining corruption in the bottom of the wound.
Then apply the ointments that promote the growth of healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal, repeat the procedure mentioned earlier. If the cause of the wound is bone and you confirm this, search for it and cut according to what I have instructed you, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If you expose bone and find some corruption or darkness, scrape it until its corruption disappears. Then treat it with what adheres to it until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus continues as before,
Know that you have not completely removed all the corruption, so uncover it again and scrape and clean it thoroughly. Then treat it again. If it heals, well and good. Otherwise, know that the corruption is deep and far-reaching, so uncover everything once more.
And thoroughly scrape and clean it with your utmost effort. If the bone was small and rotten, remove it using the gentle forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, remove them all without leaving any behind with your utmost effort. If something prevents you from removing them due to ligaments, cut those ligaments and flesh if they are present, and free the bones.
If you are unable to remove the bones at that time, pack the wound with something that will protect it and leave it for a while until what is around the bones in terms of flesh and ligaments rots. Keep the wound clean so that the incision does not heal during your work.
Should you fear this, place upon it a cloth soaked in sulfur powder mixed with oil or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For if these ligaments rot and the bones become softened, their removal and extraction will be easy.
If it is a single large bone such as the thighbone or shinbone, and only its tip has decayed, scrape it clean until the blackness and decay disappear, then cauterize the wound. But if a large portion of it has decayed and the decay has reached the marrow, it must be excised.
Cut off all of it until the decay ends, for then healing will occur until it unites. I shall tell you about a case of gangrene that appeared on a man's leg as an example and aid in your treatment; this man was elderly.
About thirty years old, he felt pain in his leg due to an internal cause until the matter reached the leg and it swelled and became inflamed, but there was no external cause. Then as time passed with the negligence of physicians, the abscess opened.
And much matter came out from it, and his treatment was neglected until the leg gangrened and many openings appeared from which pus flowed. Physicians treated him for about two years, but there was no one skilled in manual surgery among them.
Until he came to me, and I saw his leg with much pus flowing from those openings. The man had wasted away and his color was yellow. So I inserted a probe into one of the openings until it reached the bone, then I explored all the openings.
I found that they communicated with each other from all sides of the leg. So I incised over one of those openings until I exposed some bone, which was decayed and had eaten away and blackened and softened until the hole reached the marrow. Then I excised what was exposed to me.
And I removed the decayed bone, believing that there was no other decay in the bone besides what I had cut and excised. Then I applied cautery to the wound for a longer duration than usual, but it did not heal. So I returned and reopened the wound above the first incision.
I found decay connected to the bone, so I exposed what was visible of it and tried to force it out but it did not budge nor adhere. Then I uncovered it further but continued breaking off pieces of bone bit by bit until I cut about a span from the bone.
And I extracted it with forceps, then dressed the wound with medication which adhered quickly and healed completely. However, this repetition in the procedure was necessary due to the patient's weakness, inability to tolerate treatment, and fear of death because he suffered from excessive vomiting at all times.
And in the place of the bone, solid flesh grew and his condition improved in his body. His strength returned and he functioned well without any harm affecting him during walking. If a protruding bone was broken in some part of the body.
It is necessary to expose it in this manner: take a bandage, tie one end of it around the protruding bone and command someone to pull it upwards while preparing another bandage made of thick wool.
Then tie it around the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end downwards to draw the flesh down. Expose the flesh from the place where you intend to expose, so that the saw does not harm the flesh. Then place a plank or board underneath the bone from below.
For if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the decayed bone. It is necessary to expose only slightly above the place of decay so that there is no decay in the cavity of the bone appearing on its surface which would necessitate exposing it again.
If the bone is decayed but not protruding, rather connected to itself with decay in its midst or some parts of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides and place the board underneath. Then expose it from the first side where there is decay.
After finishing exposing one side, expose the other side keeping it slightly away from the decay as mentioned before. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and expose the bone on the other side where it connects.
If the defect is in the joint of two bones, there is no remedy but scraping it off. But if the defect is in the palm of the hand or the sole of the foot, its treatment is very difficult; nevertheless, one should scrape away and cleanse whatever defect appears.
And whenever a vein or nerve does not interfere, you should use any suitable saws and chisels to cut these bones according to their shape, position, thickness, fineness, size, smallness, hardness, and hollowness. Therefore, it is necessary to have for each type of work an appropriate tool for that work.
And know that the operations themselves may indicate the type of tool needed if you have extensive practice and knowledge of the arts of this craft, for expertise in surgery lies in having seen many cases of diseases. He may also devise for himself suitable tools for each disease, and I have depicted for you at the end of this chapter several such tools.
Make them as examples to follow and measure by them others, if Allah wills. Picture of a saw:
