The Seventy-Eighth Chapter.
In cutting off limbs and separating bones.
If the extremities become putrefied, either due to an external cause or an internal one, and you have treated this corruption with medicinal treatment without success, and you find that the corruption is advancing in the member without ceasing.
It is necessary that you cut off that member where the corruption has reached, so that the patient may be saved thereby from death or great harm which is greater than the loss of the member. And a sign of this is when the member turns black as if it had been burnt by fire.
Or if it becomes putrid after turning black, so that this corruption advances towards the other member and begins to affect the body, then hasten to cut it off. Likewise, if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of some poisonous creatures such as scorpions or snakes or spiders, etc.
If the corruption or bite is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger without delay lest the corruption spread to the hand. Similarly, if it affects the hand, cut it off at its root without delay lest the corruption spread and affect the arm.
And likewise, if it affects the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself. But if the corruption has reached towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for that would mean certain death for the patient. In such a case, resort to other treatments according to one's ability.
And do likewise with the leg if corruption affects the toe, cut off the toe at one of its joints. If it affects the sole of the foot, cut off the entire foot. If it reaches the knee, cut off the thigh at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave it alone and submit the patient to death. To cut off or separate a member, tie a bandage below the place where you intend to cut, and another bandage above the place.
Have one assistant hold the lower bandage downwards and another assistant hold the upper bandage upwards. Then you strip away the flesh between the two bandages with a wide lancet until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or separate it. It is necessary to place cotton pads on all sides.
To prevent the saw from slipping off the intended spot and causing unnecessary pain and inflammation, if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize or apply pressure with some absorbent cloth until it stops.
Then bind the wounded member with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals. I will describe to you an example of this case, which occurred in the leg of a man who suffered blackness and burning like fire.
The corruption first appeared in his toe, then spread to the whole finger. When the man saw that the decay was progressing despite the intense pain and burning, he severed it at the joint, and it healed.
So, after a long period of time, the same corruption appeared in his middle finger. He came to me and I applied medicine to it after cleansing its body, but the decay did not abate and spread to the other finger.
Until the decay reached his hand, he asked me to amputate it, but I refused, hoping to cure it. I also feared that he might die from cutting off his hand because his strength was failing. He then gave up hope and returned home.
I heard that he eventually cut off his entire hand and recovered. I tell this story so that it may serve as an aid in similar cases, and as a guide for reference.
Chapter Eighty-Eight.
On treating sinuses and their injection with medications.
If a tumor appears in some fleshy member and persists for a long time until it bursts or bursts out and all its contents come out, leaving the place empty like a vessel.
And the skin over it is like a rag, having become thin and not much affected such that it does not affect bone, nerve or ligament. Therefore, it deserves to be called a 'sinus' and not a 'ulcer' unless its duration becomes prolonged enough to affect one of these parts.
At this point, it is called a 'ulcer' or 'scab'. Its treatment involves cutting away all that skin around it, especially if it has become thin like a rag and is not adherent to the part due to its corruption.
If you desire the skin to adhere but it has not reached that level of corruption, and there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, treat it with an injection. If the abscess is large and emits foul-smelling pus, cauterize it with the Egyptian drug.
Take equal parts of celery, oil, honey, and ginger. Mix them together in a container and cook over fire until the medicine thickens like honey. Then take what you need, dilute it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess. Tie its mouth closed and leave the drug inside for two hours.
Then squeeze it out using a press. Do this repeatedly until the abscess is cleaned and the bad odor has gone. If you do not have this drug, you can use ash water instead, which is made by taking the ashes of grapewood or oak wood and mixing them with water.
Then purify it and inject it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed clean. If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian drug or ash water, use a mixture of water and honey with some ground ginger added.
Or inject a mixture of honey and wine, as honey cleanses and washes while wine makes the wound adhere, especially if it is strong and dry. Once you have done this several times and are certain that the abscess has gone, then inject something to stimulate flesh growth.
For example, take the lion's grease ointment and mix it with rose oil and astringent wine. Or inject some of the other drugs or ointments whose properties we have described in our chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the abscess is too narrow for the injection tube to fit.
Widen it slightly with an iron or insert a wick soaked in the Egyptian ointment or the squirrel ointment until it is wide enough. If its mouth is also too wide, sew its lips together and leave just enough opening for the injection tube to enter without any extra space.
And if the mouth from which pus is flowing is located high up, cut it lower down so that the pus will flow downward. For if pus accumulates in the depths of the abscess, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot make the incision towards its base as desired, then place the organ in such a way that pus drains from it easily according to your preference. Ensure that no pus remains trapped within at any time.
To apply the medicinal poultice externally on the abscess, take one of the medicated poultices suitable for your purpose and spread it onto a linen cloth. Ensure that the cloth is large enough to cover the entire abscess. Then, using scissors, cut a hole slightly wider than the mouth of the abscess opposite its opening. Spread one of the soft medicated poultices on another linen cloth to match the size of the hole and place it there, keeping it away from the mouth of the abscess to facilitate pus drainage. Do not remove the large cloth until afterwards.
For several days, remove the poultice at the mouth of the abscess frequently to allow the accumulated pus to exit and observe whether the discharge is profuse or scant, thick or thin.
Additionally, examine the site of the abscess itself to determine whether it is painful or not painful and whether there is swelling or no swelling. If your treatment has progressed thus and you find that the material exiting the mouth of the abscess is minimal
then know that the abscess has adhered or is nearly adherent. In this case, open it the next day or on the third day. When you have opened the wound, replace the cloths with fresh ones and renew the poultice. If thin pus continues to discharge after a long period, do not despair of its healing.
The recovery is near because some people's flesh grows slowly in wounds, while others have the opposite tendency. If thick pus continues to discharge from the abscess after many days, know that the abscess has not healed. If the healing of the abscess is delayed
If the case drags on, increase the drying property of the medication and use remedies that are moist in consistency but dry in potency, such as the medicated wax if you have already increased its amount of calamine. The most effective remedy to use is the medicated wax itself.
He mixes rose oil and sprinkles it evenly over the wound. Then he kneads it in and uses it, or if rose oil is not available, he can do the same with honey ointment after heating it until it liquefies. Alternatively, he may take equal amounts of gum arabic, wax, and storax, grind them together, and mix them into heated honey. He then soaks a cloth in it and applies it to the cavity, or he uses heated honey until it thickens, sprinkles drugs on it, places a cloth over it, and applies it.
He may also use long saffron, blue iris root, senna leaves, carthamus flower powder, and red poppy seed alone or mixed together. Grind them, sift, and mix with the honey previously mentioned. If the cavity persists without healing, know that there is some underlying corruption.
He also treats fractures of bones or other solid nerves and ligaments with the treatment for dislocations mentioned above.
Chapter Ninety-Nine.
On the treatment of hammer toes, claw toes, supernumerary toes, and separation of joined toes.
Hammer toe is a thick flesh that grows under the toenail of the thumb or any other toe, causing swelling and heat if left untreated.
Or if it becomes putrid and gangrenous to the point that it consumes the nail root, or even reaches the bone causing a foul odor. The tip of the toe may become bare and turn black.
Then you should cut away all the excess growth with an iron knife, and cauterize afterwards. Cauterization in this case is very beneficial.
If the nail angle has pushed in and embedded the flesh, you should place a thin wedge under the nail angle to lift it up and cut away the embedded flesh carefully.
Then apply burning ointments until all the flesh is destroyed. Treat it with ointments to heal. But if the bone has been affected, you must cut away and remove the rotted bone entirely.
If you see corruption and decay spreading in the finger, cut it off at one of its joints as previously mentioned, then treat it until it heals. If the nail receives a blow or becomes sore and experiences severe pain, it is not advisable to bleed the patient initially.
Then cut the nail with a sharp scalpel, making an oblique incision from top to bottom, taking care not to reach the flesh underneath the nail. If you do so, it will cause the patient severe pain and lead to excessive flesh growth in that area. Then treat the area with something that alleviates pain.
As for the extra finger that occurs in some people's hands, sometimes it is entirely flesh, sometimes it contains bones, and sometimes it has a nail. Its growth may be at the root of one of the fingers.
Or its growth may be on one of the finger joints. Those that grow on a finger joint do not move, while those that grow at the finger's base may sometimes move. If it is purely flesh, it can be cut off easily with a wide scalpel at its root.
But those that grow at the root of a joint are difficult to treat and should not be cut off. Those that grow on a finger at one of its joints should first have their flesh cut away in a circular motion towards the bone, then the bone should be scraped away using one of the appropriate scrapers.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that occurs between fingers, this often happens, especially as a result of birth defects or from wounds caused by burns and similar injuries. It is not advisable to cut through this adhesion.
So that the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place a piece of linen or cloth soaked in rose oil between them to prevent them from adhering quickly and to keep them separated until the area heals as it should.
If adhesion occurs between some of the fingers at the hand's palm, cut through that adhesion according to what is appropriate and beneficial for the shape of the affected member.
Chapter Ninety.
In the section on the treatment of hemorrhoids.
Hemorrhoids are twisted, hardened veins filled with blood and black bile that occur in most parts of the body, but they occur more frequently in the legs, especially in sailors and porters. It is recommended first to cleanse the body from black bile by powerful purging.
Then bleed the patient with cupping glasses, and as for its treatment by iron [surgery], it is of two types: one is to cut and remove the black blood, and the other is to cauterize the vein and remove it entirely. But cutting should first be done after warming the leg with hot water.
Until the thick, congealed blood is liquefied, then tie the leg of the healthy person above his buttock to below his knee with a bandage, and cut the vein in one place or two places or three places with wide incisions.
Then remove the black blood from below the leg to above and from above to below until you see enough blood come out or as much as the patient can tolerate, then bind it and instruct him to avoid foods that produce black bile. Then repeat purging and bleeding.
When the veins are filled and cause harm to the patient, then for its treatment, shave the leg of the patient if it has much hair, then enter him into the bathhouse and warm his leg with hot water until it turns red and sweat comes out or he engages in vigorous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available to warm the limb, then cut the skin against the vein lengthwise either at its upper end near the knee or at its lower end near the heel. Then open the skin with forceps and scrape off the vein from both sides until it becomes visible.
When it appears, you will see it red and bloody. When freed from the skin, you will also see that it resembles a tendon. Then insert a probe underneath it until when it rises and comes out of the skin, tie it with a smooth, blind forceps. Then cut another incision beside that one about three fingers' breadth.
Then strip the skin from around the vein until it becomes visible, then raise it with tweezers as done before and attach it to another forceps as was done initially, then make another incision or several incisions if necessary.
Then pull it out and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then pull it out and strip it until it exits from the second incision, then pull it up to the incision above it and do this until you pull it out from the third incision above all incisions, then cut it off once it is all out.
If pulling and stripping does not work, then insert a needle with a double-threaded strong thread and tie it, then pull it out and place tweezers underneath and wind it around your hand in all directions until it comes out, but be careful not to break it, for if it breaks, it will become very difficult to strip.
The patient may experience pain from it. Once you have stripped all of it, place wool soaked in a mixture of honey and rose oil or olive oil on the site of the incision and treat it until it heals. If the vein is inflamed with twists and turns, relieve its tension by pulling it gently towards its direction.
And if it is not straight as we have mentioned, do not cut into it at every turn and twist, but rather attach tweezers to it on both sides until you strip it completely. Be careful when cutting into it not to cut or injure the vein, for this will make stripping difficult.

Image of the blind forceps:
The forceps should not have teeth like other forceps, nor be sharp at the tips so as not to injure the vein. It should be thick and smooth, for if it is thin it will cut the vein with its fineness. Instead, it should have a thicker edge as mentioned.
Chapter One Hundred Ninety-Nine.
On stripping the temporal vein.
This vein originates in the legs, particularly in hot countries such as Hijaz and other sparsely populated regions with abundant heat. It may also originate from other parts of the body besides the legs due to foulness that occurs underneath the skin.
In living bodies, it is known that worms and insects occur internally, including scabies mites and those born between the skin and flesh. The first sign of this affliction appearing in the leg is intense heat followed by sweating at the affected site, after which the eruption begins to emerge from the location of that sweating.
It seems like the source is a plant or an animal. When its tip appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing between one to two dirhams and tied, then left hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it externally, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it becomes too long, cut off some of it and wrap the remainder without cutting it from its origin before it has all emerged, for if you cut it, it will shrink back into the flesh causing swelling, inflammation, and an ugly wound at that site.
Therefore, it should be treated and drawn out little by little until it emerges entirely from the body without any remnant remaining inside. Sometimes, this eruption can reach a length of five or ten spans.
I have heard that for one man, it emerged up to twenty spans. If it breaks during your treatment of it, insert a probe into its hole and push it back deeply within the body until all its contents are emptied out, then attempt to heal the site with medications over some time.
Then treat it with a treatment for tumors, as this eruption may have many branches, especially if it appears at the knee joint or on the leg itself. In such cases, it will have many mouths from which multiple shoots emerge. Treat each branch as mentioned earlier in this section.
Chapter Two Hundred and Ninety
Regarding the treatment of scabies mites that occur beneath the hide, which is known as cattle disease.
This disease is called "Biqr's affliction" in some of our lands because it often affects cattle. It is a small, single mite that breeds between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the entire body, ascending and descending.
One can sense its movement from one organ to another until it breaks through the skin, creating a hole where it emerges. Its composition is formed from some corrupt humors, like that of other worms and insects found in the belly.
And it is expected that harm from it will happen if it spreads in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, perhaps it may open it and come out thus abolishing the eye. This occurs frequently. If you desire treatment for it and its removal, this can only occur when it has spread and become perceptible.
It is necessary to bind what is above and below it with a tight band, then cut upon it and remove it. If it has penetrated into the flesh and cannot be found, cauterize the place with fire until it burns out. The greatest harm expected from it is corruption of the eye, as mentioned.
If you see it has reached the head near the eye, bind tightly beneath it on the forehead, then cut upon it and remove it. It is necessary for the patient to treat himself by purging his body with drugs that ease corrupt humors and avoid foods that cause putrefaction.
Chapter Three-Ninety.
Regarding the incision for the disease known as 'Nafar'.
This disease, which we call 'Nafar' in our land, is a pain that appears in some organs and then spreads from one organ to another. I have seen it as I have described to you.
I was called to a woman patient in some villages. She uncovered her arm and showed me a slight swelling in the vein of the forearm. After an hour, I saw that swelling spreading upwards towards the shoulder like a worm or quicksilver.
If it flows from one place to another, the pain leaves that spot and settles in the shoulder. After sitting for an hour, it spreads throughout the body until it reaches the other arm. She then told me that her entire body was rotating as I witnessed.
I was amazed at the speed with which it moved from one organ to another, having never seen this disease manifest in such a way before, except that I had heard of others experiencing pain moving from one organ to another, but not witnessed it personally as I did in this woman.
I concluded that this was due to the woman being from a desert region with a dry body and prominent veins, making it easier for the pain to spread visibly. It would not appear in this manner among those of comfortable living with moist bodies and concealed veins.
If you wish to treat him and his companion feels pain, when it appears before you as we mentioned, then apply pressure above and below it swiftly and cut upon him until that retained wind escapes, and the space is cleared.
If you do not see it with your own eye, treat him by shaking the body and using winds dispelling remedies such as ground peppermint seed or ground purslane seed and similar drugs.
Chapter Four Ninety.
In extraction of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types and the locations on the body where they strike, and as for their variation in type, some are large and others small, some have hollow glass tips and others solid ones, while still others have three or four corners.
Some have blades, others have splinters. As for those that strike vital organs, they fall into two categories: either the organ is hollow like the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, etc., or it is not hollow. When an arrow strikes one of these organs and the signs of impending death become apparent, as I will describe later, it is imperative that you do not attempt to remove the arrow, for death usually ensues. However, if these signs of ill omen do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared deep within the organ, then remove it and treat the wound. A sign that an arrow has struck the brain is severe headache, epistaxis, and dizziness, especially if the arrow has pierced the skull bone and injured the meninges covering the brain.
However, if these signs of ill omen do not appear and the arrow has not disappeared deep within the organ, then remove it and treat the wound. A sign that an arrow has struck the brain is severe headache, epistaxis, and dizziness, especially if the arrow has pierced the skull bone and injured the meninges covering the brain. Symptoms include redness in the eyes, reddening of the tongue, convulsions, mental confusion, fainting, epistaxis or otorrhoea, loss of speech and voice, and the discharge of a white, milky fluid resembling gruel from the wound.
If these signs become apparent, do not attempt to treat the patient or remove the arrow if it has not yet emerged. Signs that an arrow has struck the heart include severe pain near the left breast, as if it had struck a solid object rather than an empty space.
Additionally, the arrow may pulsate like a heartbeat. If black blood issues from the wound, followed by cold extremities, sweating, and loss of consciousness, then death is imminent. A sign that the lung has been injured is the emission of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels following the neck become swollen, and the patient's skin color changes. He breathes heavily and desires to inhale cold air. If an arrow has struck near the heart, it is usually close to the ribs. In such cases, breathing becomes very difficult with severe pain.
He sighs deeply and moves all his lower limbs. If the liver is struck by an arrow, severe pain follows and blood similar to liver in color emerges from the wound. If the stomach is hit, undigested food may emerge from the wound.
If the abdomen is struck and the bowel is perforated, or if there is a rupture of the intestines, there is no hope for his recovery nor any means to remove the arrow. But if the bladder is pierced, urine may emerge.
If something protrudes from the wound and pain increases, know that he is doomed. Otherwise, most other parts of the body such as the face, eye, neck, shoulder, upper arm, back, thigh, leg, etc., can generally recover.
When an arrow does not hit an artery or vein and is not poisoned, I will tell you about some cases I have witnessed that may guide your treatment. Once, an arrow struck a man in the root of his nose.
I extracted it from the other side under his ear, and he recovered without any harm to his eye. In another case, I removed an arrow that had pierced a Jew's lower eyelid, leaving only its small tip sticking out.
The arrow was large, one of the composite bow arrows with a square iron head, smooth without ears. The Jew recovered without any harm to his eye. I also removed an Arab arrow from the throat of a Christian.
Another arrow, which had ears, was stuck between his shoulder blades in the back of a man. We were able to pull it out carefully until it was removed. The man survived and recovered.
About thirty days later, without any change in his condition, we opened the arrow and removed it. He recovered without any harm. I also saw a man who was struck by an arrow in the back. The wound adhered to the arrow.
After seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of his thigh. I saw a woman who had been struck by an arrow in her belly; the wound adhered and the arrow remained unchanged. Her condition did not alter in any way nor did she find it harmful in any of her natural actions.
I saw another man struck by an arrow in his face; the wound adhered and he found it only moderately painful. Many such cases exist. I also extracted an arrow from a soldier of the Sultan's guard, which had pierced him in the middle of his nose, inclining slightly towards the right side.
The entire arrowhead had vanished, and I was summoned to treat him three days after the arrow struck. Upon examination, I found the wound of the arrow very narrow; I probed it with a thin probe but did not feel the arrowhead. He felt pain and soreness beneath his right ear from the wound's opening.
I suspected that the pain was due to the tip of the arrow, so I applied a dressing with a drawing agent and allowed it to remain for several days in hope that the place would swell and the mark of the arrowhead would become apparent. But no such indication occurred.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened. The wound healed over during this time, and the patient became resigned to leaving the arrow where it was until one day he felt it inside his nose and informed me of this. I then applied a powerful corrosive medication to the wound for several days.
Until the wound opened up and I probed it, feeling the fine point of the arrowhead adhering to the bone. Then I increased the application of the powerful corrosive medication until the tip of the arrowhead became visible. I followed this course for about four months.
Then when the wound had widened enough for me to insert forceps, I grasped and moved the arrowhead but it did not budge. So I continued treating him with various devices until one day I succeeded in firmly grasping it with forceps as shown at the end of this book.
The physicians had judged that his nasal cartilage could not be restored, but I managed to restore it. The wound healed and the patient recovered completely, never feeling any discomfort afterwards. I tell you this method of extracting some arrows so that you may use it as a guide for other cases which I have not mentioned.
It is impossible to grasp the intricacies of this art through description alone or encompass it entirely in a book. Only the skilled practitioner can measure the small by the large, the present by the absent, and deduce new works and new tools for unusual occurrences.
If an arrow is removed from this art, I say that it exits from the organ in which it lodged in one of two ways: either by pulling it from the place where it entered or by pushing it out from the opposite side.
Either the arrow is sticking out at a flesh wound, in which case pull it and remove it. But if it does not come out within its original time, leave it until the surrounding flesh rots, making it easier to grasp and remove. Similarly, if it has lodged in bone.
Leave it also if it does not come out, and return to pull and move it daily. If it still does not come out after several days, you should then perforate around the arrow in the same bone on both sides with a fine awl until its path is widened, and then grasp and remove it.
If the arrow has lodged in the bone of the head and penetrated into one of the ventricles of the brain, showing some of the symptoms mentioned earlier, do not attempt to pull out the arrow. Leave it alone until its course is cleared after several days.
...attempt to pull out the arrow and remove it. If it is too deeply lodged and cannot be pulled out, then use the awl around the arrow as described earlier, and treat the wound until it heals. But if the arrow has disappeared within a part of the body... ...and search for it with a probe. If you feel it, then grasp and remove it using some of the instruments suitable for its extraction. However, if you cannot do so due to the narrowness of the wound and the arrow's depth within the cavity, and there is no bone, nerve or vein in the way...
It troubled him until the wound widened and the arrow came out with ease. If it has ears attached, hold them firmly; free the surrounding flesh from all sides as much as possible, using all your skill. Should you fail to detach the flesh in breaking the ears and twisting them off, strive to release it nonetheless.
If you attempt to remove the arrow from any location, use tweezers on all sides until it is dislodged. Take utmost care lest the arrow break and become difficult to grasp and extract. If you are unable after some time.
Leave it for some days until the surrounding flesh putrefies, then attempt again; this will be easier. If bleeding intervenes, use the treatment mentioned earlier. Take care not to cut any vein, nerve or tendon, and exercise caution.
Do your utmost to remove the arrow gently and steadily, as described. When pulling out the arrow, ensure the patient is in the same position they were when it entered; this is preferable.
If you are unable to do so, use whatever position is possible. As for the arrow exiting from the opposite side, it may have already protruded slightly or you may detect its tip near the skin's surface by touch.
Make an incision over the arrow, as wide as your tweezers will allow, then pull it out gently. If it is stuck in bone, twist your hand around the arrow's tip until it affects the bone and creates space for itself, then pull it out.
If it does not come out, leave it alone until it can be removed. If the shaft of the arrow is still attached, use it to push it out. If the shaft has fallen off and you wish to use a pushing tool, insert the hollow probe into the wound until its cavity fits around the butt of the arrow, then push it with this.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out using a tool that fits inside its cavity; this will facilitate its removal. If the arrowhead is poisoned, excise all the affected flesh if possible, then treat it accordingly.
If the arrow is embedded in the chest, belly, bladder or flank and is close enough to be felt with the probe and you can make an incision upon it, then do so. Be careful not to cut any vessels or nerves as you extract it.
Then suture the wound if necessary and treat it until healed. Image of the tongs used to draw out arrows:
Their ends resemble the beak of a bird and are made in such a way that when they grasp an arrow or any other object, they do not release it. Tongs can be made in large, small, or medium sizes according to the thickness and length of the arrow and the width and depth of the wound.

Chapter Fifty-Nine.
In letting blood from the veins.
The veins commonly bled in the body are thirty, of which eighteen are in the head: the two temporal arteries behind the ears known as 'hassisin', and the two superficial temporal arteries. The two veins in the eye sockets known as 'naazir.'
And the vein that stands out in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, the two jugular veins in the neck, the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth and lower lip, these are known as 'jaharak', and the two sublingual veins.
And as for the veins bled in the arm and hand, there are five: one is the cephalic vein on the lateral side, commonly known as the 'head vein'; another is the median cubital vein, composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic vein, commonly known as the 'body vein'.
And the basilica vein on the medial side is also known as the 'armpit'. The vein commonly called the 'abdominal vein' and the 'arm vein' is located on the forearm and appears clearly above the thumb. This is known as the cephalic vein.
This is the vein that lies between the index and middle fingers, with two branches; in the leg and foot there are three veins: one beneath the knee joint on the outer side, the second behind the ankle on the inner side, and the vein of the buttock located on the outer side at the heel.
In the other leg, there are three veins like them. As for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit lies in treating frequent colds, migraines, and head wounds that do not heal.
Shave the head of the patient and rub his buttock with a coarse linen cloth at the location of the veins, rubbing vigorously. Then tie a handkerchief around the patient's neck until the two veins appear behind the ears in their lower locations on the head. Feel for their pulse with your finger, and where you feel it, mark the spot with ink.
Then take a sharp lancet, known as the 'Nashli', and insert it under the vein in the skin until it reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein along with the skin in a piece about two fingerbreadths long.
Or similarly, allowing as much blood to flow out as desired, then tie them with sutures and leave them until they heal. As for the two arteries in the temples, their benefit lies in treating chronic migraines, severe headaches, and persistent eye inflammation with profuse discharge.
To treat them as described: tie a handkerchief around the patient's neck until the two arteries become clearly visible to the touch and their pulse can be felt under your finger. Then mark the spot with ink, and then raise the skin above the artery with your index finger.
Insert the lancet 'Nashli' from below, raise the artery upwards and cut it as done with the other veins. Allow blood to flow according to your need, then release the patient's neck and press your finger on the artery for a while.
Then place a bandage and dressing on it, tie it tightly from above, and leave it until it heals. The mention of their (artery and vein) piercing, cutting, and tying is found at the beginning of the book. As for cupping to draw out the vein of the forehead, its benefit is in treating chronic facial conditions such as epilepsy, ulcers, and stubborn acne.
To perform cupping on the forehead in the manner I have described for you, suffocate the patient's neck with a scarf until sweat appears. Then take the instrument called the 'axe', which is shaped like this:
Place the protruding spike at the head of the axe on the same vein and strike from above with a comb or similar object in this manner, allowing the blood to flow into the desired vessel. Then release the patient's neck and leave it until it heals.
And it may be opened with a wide-bladed lancet, but it should not be as sharp-pointed as other lancets. Rather, its point should be somewhat blunted to prevent injury to the nearby bone, for if it were too thin, the lancet might break upon it.
As for the veins at the angles of the eyes, their cupping is beneficial for treating eye diseases such as itching, inflammation, and optic nerve issues, as well as facial diseases. To perform this cupping, have the patient lie on their back with their head facing you, then place the cups on the temples at a slight angle towards the lengthwise direction using a small, blunt-tipped lancet.
If there is no flesh at the site, then if the lancet point is too thin it may break. Then send forth some blood according to your need and place a bandage on both wounds, tying them tightly for one night before removing them. As for cupping the veins of the nose, it is beneficial for treating acute fever.
And for severe headaches and facial diseases such as red hives appearing on the nose, especially if they are chronic, cupping should be performed by having the patient lie on their back while you hold their nose with your left hand and take a long, thin lancet.
Pierce it in the middle of the nostril itself between the nasal septum, for the vein does not appear there by touch. Send forth some blood according to your need, then tie it tightly with a bandage overnight as it heals quickly.
And as for the poultices, their benefit is that they relieve shortness of breath and treat the onset of leprosy and black diseases that appear on the skin's surface such as black patches, boils, and bad sores. Their method of application is to have the patient lie down on their back with a bandage tied around their neck, while the practitioner stands at the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, then the bloodletting is done vertically down to the elbow in a wide vein. Then, an appropriate amount of blood is drawn or according to what is seen as necessary, and this is done again with the other vein. Finally, the bandage is untied.
And tie the veins moderately to prevent the patient from fainting, then leave him until the next day as this will heal the wound. And as for the veins of the arms, their benefit is that when they are bled after bleeding the head vein, they alleviate pains in the mouth and cure bad gums, canker sores, cracked lips, and bad sores.
That are in the nose and around it, and their method of bleeding is that the patient sits facing you with a scarf tied around their neck. Then pull on their cheeks to expose the veins on either side of the lip, from which you draw blood according to their darkness.
And around them are small, dark veins which you cut off completely. If you are unsure about these, then look for the largest and most visible ones, and do the same with the veins on the lower lip.
And as for the two veins beneath the tongue, their benefit is that when they are bled after bleeding the head vein, they alleviate quinsy in the throat from diseases of the tonsils and mouth. And their method of bleeding is to have the patient sit facing you with the sun at their back, raise their tongue, and look underneath it on one side for a vein and on the other side for another vein, both of which should be dark in color.
So bleed them, but be cautious not to cut too deeply as there are arteries beneath them that may cause bleeding if cut. And the three veins that are bled at the elbow are those which it is customary to bleed in most people, and their bleeding should be done on both sides.

Or take a piece of linen and cut it in half, tie one half around the wound tightly with knots and bandages. If bleeding stops by the next day, well and good; otherwise treat it as mentioned earlier by using ligatures to stop the bleeding, for cutting the blood vessel is not difficult in most cases.

It is preferable for the tie to be on the neck from its origin, for beneath it there is a nerve. If the ligature is too tight and the needle punctures the nerve, numbness will occur which may not recover, and sometimes it does not heal at all; this nerve is often palpable.
If the vein is hidden in some people and too thin to be felt, then make your incision with a scalpel avoiding the nerve at all costs; if the vein is between two nerves, cut it lengthwise. The cephalic vein is beneficial to tie off as it attracts blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, particularly if you wish to ligate this vein. Use the olive-pointed needle or the wide reed needle; all veins are suitable for this because there is no artery or nerve beneath it.

If the procedure is used for diseases and that which is used for preserving health and maintaining it, and preventing the occurrence of diseases, then bloodletting should be done in one of the three veins at the elbow called the cubital vein, cephalic vein, or basilica vein. Bloodletting should be done on a Sunday or Tuesday after noon, three hours have passed. However, bloodletting used for diseases has no specific time limit; it is performed whenever necessary, day or night.
And at every hour and at all times, except for children until they reach fourteen years of age or the elderly who have exceeded seventy years. Anyone intending to undergo bloodletting should do so regardless of the reason.
It is necessary to cleanse the bowels before bloodletting with a gentle enema if there is abundant retained feces, lest putrid matter from the intestines be drawn into the veins during bloodletting and harm the vital organs. One should not undergo bloodletting while intoxicated or drunk until that state has passed.
Also, one should avoid bloodletting after an attack of fever, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual intercourse, fatigue, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and any other physical or mental exertion that weakens the body. Then examine the thinness of the humors before proceeding if the blood is thick.
If possible, prepare the body with suitable foods, drinks, and medications. Then enter a bathhouse if there is no impediment or engage in some exercise to make the blood thin. Bloodletting should be performed during midday as mentioned earlier, aiming to empty the chest of all negative emotional states that day.

Then the phlebotomist should sit on a cushion higher than that of the patient. The patient should extend their arm, and the phlebotomist should rub it twice or three times with his hand before tying the tourniquet using the bandage and twisting it twice or thrice. The tension should be moderate.
For tension, when it is not moderate, either by excess of tension prevents the flow of blood, or by relaxation also prevents the flow of blood. Afterwards, upon releasing tension, one should gently rub the joint with both hands.
Until the vessels swell and become visible to touch, then gently rub the joint with old oil particularly. Then place the left hand's index finger on the same vein beneath the point intended for ligation, slightly pressing so as not to allow the vein to escape the strike. Therefore, the ligator should be steadfast and deliberate in all these matters. Then let him lower the lancet; if the vein opens from that stroke, otherwise let him repeat it once more beneath or above that point with haste if the point does not become swollen.

If blood from an artery flows, compress it with a cloth to stop the bleeding, then treat it with other remedies until it heals. Each person should have blood drawn from them according to their strength and the predominant color of their blood.

This should not be done if one intends to relax the arm again.

Do not touch it until the swelling has subsided. If there is a necessity to evacuate blood again and it cannot be avoided, then perform phlebotomy either above that spot or on the other arm or vein.
As for bloodletting from the upper arm vein, it is performed instead of cupping and leeching when they are not available or if they are concealed because it consists of both. Chapter Sixty-Nine.
On Cupping and its Method of Use.
There are cupping glasses made from horns, wood, copper, and glass. There are two types of cupping: one with cautery and bloodletting, the other without cautery.
This cupping without cautery has two aspects:
The temples are on both sides of the neck, and the chin is below the lower jaw of the mouth, and the shoulders, and the buttocks on the back of the hip, and the armpits which are in the middle of the arms, and the thighs, and the calves.
And bloodletting is indeed the drawing out of blood from the veins in the back of the neck that are scattered throughout the flesh, and because of this it does not cause weakness like cupping nor should it be used with fire or without it for any disease caused by fullness until the entire body has been emptied.
If bloodletting is necessary due to illness or custom, it should be performed at the beginning, middle, or end of the month and at any other time as well.
And among people, some find heaviness in their heads and dizziness when blood increases excessively and needs to be removed by bloodletting. Some experience fullness and redness in their faces, heads, and necks, while others suffer from itching in their faces and behind their ears, as well as darkness and dimness in their eyes. Still others scratch the site of their cupping.
And some laugh excessively, while others taste blood in their mouths and gums, spit out blood, or experience vomiting of blood. Some sleep too much, while others see visions of blood, redness, wounds, and surgeries during their dreams, so whenever we observe any of these symptoms
Especially if it is in the middle of the month, we would then order bloodletting after two or three hours have passed since dawn. The benefit of cupping with a needle is that it helps alleviate heaviness in the head and prevents water from flowing into the eyes.
However, this should be done after the entire body has been emptied. This cupping can serve as an alternative to cupping of the skull, but it is not recommended for those with a cold temperament or a fever, as it can cause great harm.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly, nor by those whose heads have cold diseases, nor by those who are addicted to it as it can cause forgetfulness.
Therefore, it is necessary to instruct the cupping practitioner to lower his hand slightly with the cup, fearful of causing profuse bleeding. As for cupping of the nape, it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the temples and phlebotomy of the side.
Thus, cupping benefits those suffering from shortness of breath, asthma, headaches due to difficulty breathing, coughing, and congestion. It is recommended that the cupping of the nape be lifted slightly so it does not descend too much, as this could cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for cupping of the temples, it benefits those suffering from sudden headaches, eye inflammation, migraines, nasal congestion, and toothache. It is a substitute for phlebotomy of the side, and it is necessary to instruct the practitioner not to insert the needle too deeply to avoid cutting an artery and causing bleeding.
And cupping below the navel benefits those suffering from mouth sores, tooth decay, and other oral diseases. It can serve as a substitute for phlebotomy of the cheeks. And cupping of the shoulders benefits those suffering from palpitations caused by congestion and heat.
And cupping of the lower abdomen benefits whatever phlebotomy of the three veins (basilic, temporal, and cephalic) benefits, as it attracts blood from all those superficial veins in the flesh and draws blood from deeper veins until it reaches the three deep veins.
It is necessary to instruct the practitioner not to insert the needle too deeply, as the area lacks flesh and has nerves and arteries underneath. And a single cup placed on the buttocks benefits hemorrhoids and anal sores.
It is necessary to instruct the practitioner that the cup should be large and made of copper, as the area requires strong suction. A glass cup may break and necessitate many punctures. And cupping of the legs reduces congestion noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the entire body, benefits chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, regulates menstruation, treats boils and skin diseases, and can serve as a substitute for phlebotomy of the forearms and calves. However, it tires the body greatly and causes dizziness in most people. Cupping of the calves has similar benefits to cupping of the legs.
To apply the cupping glass: first place it empty and draw out moderately without lingering too long. Instead, apply it quickly and remove it swiftly to encourage an even reception of humors into the site until you observe the area reddening and opening.
Upon observing the redness of blood, one should then gradually withdraw the suction, looking at the condition of the skin. If the person has loose flesh and open pores, cauterize only once to avoid causing a raw wound.
The cupping practitioner should widen and deepen the cauterization slightly, adjusting the suction gently with delicate movements. If there is thick blood present, he should cauterize twice: first to open a path for the thin blood and its fluidity, then again to thoroughly remove the thick blood.
If the blood is extremely thick, cauterize a third time to achieve the desired result. In general, one cauterization is sufficient for removing a small amount of blood, but multiple cauterizations are required for a large quantity, especially if the blood is thick.
It is not permissible to cauterize too deeply; the moderate depth should only reach the skin. The cupping practitioner should avoid using hot coals, water, or anything else that might cause harm, especially when dealing with individuals who have thick, hard, rough skin and narrow pores.
One should anoint the sites of cupping with melted, refined, and dissolved fat. However, during summer, one may use olive oil, rose oil, or sweet almond oil instead.
In winter, however, one may use narcissus oil, saffron oil, or other fragrant oils like musk or civet. If the fat used is coarse and cold, prefer oils such as myrrh, nard, balsam, or storax.
If the cupping practitioner has large pores and loose flesh, he should avoid using fat. Such individuals should wash their cupping glasses after the procedure with rose water, cold water, or waters of fox grape, watermelon, or pomegranate, especially if they have moist blood.
He washes his cupping glasses with vinegar or water from the well and pomegranate, etc., but for those whose blood is thick, he washes them with old wine, or water of marjoram, or decoction of colocynth or papaw, etc. It is advisable to avoid cupping in baths and after baths.
It is preferable to use cupping after leaving the bath by an hour or two, and it is not advisable for anyone to sleep afterwards. The patient and the cupper should not prepare food before or after cupping.
First, look if the patient or cupper is yellowish, as blood is usually hot and inflamed. Therefore, they should take coolants such as pomegranate, radishes, chicory, parsley, celery, etc., in vinegar. They should also eat lean meat, sheep liver, and similar foods.
For those with a cold temperament, they should drink honey syrup or mint syrup decoction, and have moderate wine. They should eat sparingly and their food should be liver, quails, pigeons, and young poultry. On the day of cupping and venesection, drink more than usual.
It may be advisable to give some people theriac, musk remedy, or coloquintida before cupping or venesection, and after it, to strengthen the main organs and relieve the blood. Avoid giving it to those with weak stomachs.
As for cupping without restrictions, it is done on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, heart, kidney area, and buttocks, as these organs cannot withstand restricted cupping. Its aim is to draw blood from one organ to another.
When cupping is placed on the breasts for weaning, or used to remove cold wind from an organ, it should be done on the abdomen and chest. This loosens the organ, warms it, and alleviates pain by expelling the wind.
Placing pressure on the kidneys when a stone or calculus is present may, with forceful pulling, open the passage or dislodge the stone from its position. This can also be done for the liver and spleen when affected by wind (flatulence). These cupping glasses are used empty.
It can also be used with fire, and can be employed filled with lukewarm water for treating chilblains. This is done by filling the cupping glass and making it large, using either hot water alone or water that has been boiled with some herbs suitable for this purpose.
Then it is placed on the location and held while suction occurs, repeated several times according to necessity. This is the image of the cupping glass used with fire:
The width of its mouth is two fingers open, as shown, and its depth is half a cubit. On one side, halfway down, there is a small hole the size of a needle, used for inserting the fire. It is made of Chinese or yellow copper with thick rims.
Smooth and flat, polished so as not to harm the organ when placed upon it. In its center there is a cross-shaped copper or iron tube where the fire candle is placed. These cupping glasses can be made large or small according to the condition being treated.
And its use is that the cupping glasses for children and thin individuals are not the same as those used for men or strong-bodied patients. As for how to place this cupping glass with fire on the organ, it is done by lighting a piece of tight linen or a small candle made of wax and placing it on the center of the raised tube in the middle of the cup.
Thus, the fire rises up towards the bottom of the cup to avoid burning the patient's body. Then the cup is placed on the organ with a finger covering the hole mentioned earlier until, when holding the cup, one can remove the finger.
As a result, steam escapes through that hole and the cup adheres to its location. Then extinguish the flame using water according to need. As for the cupping glass used for treating chilblains with water, it does not have a raised tube or hole.

Chapter Seventy-Nine.
Regarding the treatment of dislocations.
The splint is used in most cases on members that cannot have bandages applied to them due to their small size, such as the lip and gum, etc., or because the member is bare of flesh like the finger and nose, etc.
And its method of use is to choose a splint that has been in fresh, pure water from springs for a day and night until it hungers and nothing remains inside it. Then the body is first evacuated by cupping or bloodletting, then the injured member is wiped clean.
Until it reddens, then place the splint upon it. If it becomes full, remove it. If possible, suck on the spot with a cupping glass; this is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the spot with vinegar first, then with plenty of water and rub and squeeze. If bleeding persists after removing the splint, apply a cotton cloth dipped in cold water as a compress.
And place it on top until the bleeding stops. If blood is abundant, sprinkle powdered pitch or gum tragacanth or similar substance to stop the bleeding, or apply crushed barley grains ground with water.
And leave it until the barley grains stick to the spot, then remove the splint if needed. If the same splint is reused, wash the spot with fresh blood before reapplying.
Or insert a needle into the spot until some blood comes out, then place the splint. If it feels any blood at all, it will stick to the site. If you wish to remove it, pour some resin or salt or ashes upon it.
Chapter Three.
On Dislocations.
This chapter is also about the remedies needed in the art of medicine, which is the reduction of fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know ye, O my children, that many physicians and laymen claim proficiency in this chapter (of surgery), yet they have not consulted the ancient writings nor read a single word from them.
And because of this, this art has become extinct among our people; I have never found anyone skilled in it. Indeed, I have only benefited from this art through my extensive reading of the ancient books and my diligent study of them until I extracted its knowledge, then I remained devoted to practice and experience throughout my life.
And in this chapter (of surgery), I have drawn for you all that I know about it. After long practice, I have made it accessible to you, freed it from superfluous detail, condensed it as much as possible, and clarified its language; I have also illustrated for you many figures of the instruments used therein.
This is in addition to the explanation given in the two preceding chapters, and there is no power except with Allah, the Most High.
Chapter One.
And in the first chapter, there are many concise accounts about fractures of bones. And it is proper to begin by discussing them before we proceed to mention the individual members that have been broken or dislocated one by one; therefore, let us commence this chapter with general statements and sections. First of all, you must understand and grasp its true meaning, you and whoever else is eager to learn this noble art.
So I say that whenever a fracture or dislocation occurs due to falling, striking, or twisting, one must not rush immediately to reduce (realign) it or manipulate it, nor both at once. Unless there is a hindrance to doing so, such as weakness of the patient's strength, or if he is a child, an old man, or if it is extremely hot or cold.
Then his diet should be restricted to cold pulses, poultry, and eggs; he should avoid intoxicants, tough meat, foods that cause fullness in the vessels, and any food that causes blood congestion until the swelling has been relieved and no further oozing of fluid is anticipated at the site.
When the bone fragments are set in traction, the patient should then return to his usual diet, avoiding foods that are too thick or sticky, such as dates, rice, millet, wheat, barley gruel, and beef brains. The whites, fresh fish, thick drinks and the like; through this arrangement, healing of fractures becomes quicker and better. Know that broken bones in robust men and elders cannot join and unite as they originally were.
The ends of broken bones and their flexibility; what was once hard bone becomes like the soft bones of children. However, nature generates a glue-like substance on all sides of the broken bone, which is thick and sticks, holding them together until they unite.
And it binds together until it becomes as strong and firm as it originally was, so that nothing hinders his actions.
Therefore, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are tough and thick, as mentioned earlier. Know that fractures differ in type according to the difference in organs, for a fracture of the leg bone is not like a fracture of the skull bone, nor is a fracture of the rib bone like one of the spinal vertebrae.
And likewise, all other organs are different from each other. We will come to a detailed description of each type of fracture in its respective chapter, and even the type of bone fracture may differ.
For a fracture may be cracked without any splinters, or it may occur lengthwise with splinters and fragments that are sharp and blunt. It may also happen with wounds and tears in the skin, or it may be a slight fracture.
And each type has a specific method for its treatment, as will be mentioned in detail later. One way to recognize a bone fracture is by its deformity, swelling, and prominence to the touch, and by its crunching sound when pressed with the hand.
Therefore, if there is no apparent deformity, no crunching sound, and no disturbance felt when pressing the bone, nor does the patient complain of much pain, then it is not a fracture but rather a sprain or slight fracture or minor fracture. In such cases, one should not manipulate or press the limb excessively.
But apply to it the medications that will be mentioned later, according to its location, then gently immobilize it. Know that if a bone is completely fractured into two pieces without any splinters, each piece is entirely separated from its fellow.
Act promptly to straighten and align it before inflammation occurs. If inflammation has already occurred, leave it alone for a while until the inflammation subsides, then straighten it using whatever gentle means you can.
Know that straightening and aligning it is easier than a bone with fragments. Then, as will be mentioned later, if there are fragments in the bone, it is necessary to extend the broken limb on both sides whether it is an arm or a leg.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two cords, or with both cord and hand, ensure that you position the limb on a flat surface in its natural shape until the broken bone extends. Then gently press back those fragments into their places using whatever means of skill and gentleness you can.
Strive to not cause any pain or inflammation to the patient through your actions. Strive to join one bone with its partner in the best manner possible, touching and feeling them with your hand. If you find anything deviating from normal, correct it and straighten it as much as you can.
Beware of excessive stretching and strong pulling as many ignorant ones do. Often they cause inflammation or dislocation in the limb, as I have seen happen many times.
After straightening, perfecting, and securing the limb, keep it at rest and prevent it from moving during wakefulness or sleep, turning or straining, or during urination. Advise the patient to ensure that the splint is positioned in a way that prevents pain.
Whenever the patient feels pain during splinting, he should move it to another position where there is no pain. He should ensure that his splint remains straight and even.
Otherwise, the limb may become deformed when it heals. Know that broken limbs differ in size and shape, so those that are small like the arm, finger, and wrist...
It is necessary that the bandages be long enough to encircle the limbs and not too thin, nor should they be rigid like a thigh or back or chest. The wide bandage must be evenly tensioned around the large member without any gap.
Then, after finishing wrapping, you should apply the appropriate plaster. First, apply a soft pad at the fracture site. Then begin wrapping with the bandage over the fracture itself in three or four layers depending on what the limb deserves. Tighten your hand slightly on the bandage and then bring it up to the upper side of the fracture.
Wrap more gently than at the fracture site, then space out the wrapping slightly away from the fracture and loosen the tension gradually until you reach the appropriate area. Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fracture.
Wrap again, this time going to the lower side of the fracture. Tension and loosening should be done as mentioned earlier for the first wrapping. Then place a soft pad or cloth between the wrappings to straighten the fracture.
If there is no deformity, do not place anything between the wrappings. Then wrap with another bandage and secure it properly if there is no swelling or heat around the limb.
If there is swelling or heat, apply something to reduce the inflammation and subsidence. Leave it for some time, then secure with bandages. The splints should be made of half-rounded reeds prepared skillfully or made from ebony wood.
Which are made from pinewood, palm fronds, calamite, or broomstick and similar materials readily available. The size of each splint should be in this shape and form specifically.
Except for the splint placed directly on the fracture, which should be thicker and wider than the others. The length of the splint should be made according to the size of the broken limb. Splint shape:
Then you tighten it with another bandage according to your first tightening, then tie it from above with fine sutures according to what was mentioned about the tightening, such that the tightening is stronger at the fracture site. And as for the parts distant from the fracture, they are tightened less.
It is preferable that the sutures be moderate in thickness and strength, preferably made from moistened cotton thread, for if they are thick like some ignorant people do by using coarse cotton strips, it is a grave error.
For tight sutures will cause excessive tension, and fine ones are unsuitable as they do not achieve the desired tension. It is preferable that there be no less than a finger's width between bandages.
If the patient is bothered by the edges of the bandages after proper tightening, place padded gauze or loosely woven wool underneath them so that they do not cause discomfort. As for fractures with wounds and tears in the skin, we will discuss that separately later on.
Know that not every fractured limb should be immobilized with bandages from the first day. If the limb is large, it is preferable to wait five to seven days or more until swelling has subsided before applying bandages.
This is the description of the splints used by earlier practitioners for fractures, dislocations, and luxations. It is a concise general description suitable for most constitutions, especially children and women, as it does not aggravate heat or cold.
Take the dust from the millstone, which is the flour that adheres to its walls during grinding. Mix it as is without adding eggs, making sure it is not too thick or too thin. Then apply it.
Another recipe for setting fractures, dislocations, and luxations: Take ten dirhams each of millet (mash), lentils (ladan), onions (aqaliya), raisins (rassan), fenugreek (maghatha), cumin seeds (sukk), five dirhams each of pepper (mar`), ginger (sabur), and twenty dirhams of frankincense (uhl). Mix them with Armenian or Roman clay (tin armani/rumi). Everyone should be beaten and mixed with water of the mastic tree or white of eggs if the patient's temperament is dry, then this bandage should be applied as it is well composed, it quickly sets broken bones and suits most people for balance.
A second bandage that is also beneficial for fractures and dislocations: Take opium, mastic, and white wax from each one ten dirhams, myrrh and saffron from each five dirhams, acacia six dirhams, Armenian earth twenty dirhams. Grind them all finely, sieve, mix with water or egg white, apply.
A bandage for joints and loose bones from their places, to alleviate sudden pain and headache caused by bone dislocation or fracture: Take pure wool, dip in vinegar and cooked oil, apply on the affected area. This bandage has no setting power.
However, it is beneficial in reducing hot swelling and relieving pain particularly. Another bandage that sets broken bones: Take leaves of figs and poplar bark, grind them together, apply wet. Another concise bandage used for forced fractures.
And if you wish to analyze the remainder of the swelling, take equal parts of opium poppy, myrrh, and powdered bdellium, grind them together and mix with plaster if the member is not inflamed, or with water if it is inflamed. Apply it.
Another bandage, stronger in analysis than the first one, used when solid swelling occurs due to forced bone fracture: Take equal parts of opium, saffron, tragacanth, senna, frankincense, bdellium, and poppy.
Grind all ingredients together and mix with sour grape juice, water, or plaster according to the heat of the member and its calmness. Hippocrates did not mention in his book that it should be applied to the broken limb when setting it.
But Qirritius advocated a plaster made only from wax and oil, moderate between thickness and softness. Galen recommended applying things that are dry with some heat, like myrrh, saffron, and honey, etc., to the broken limb during setting, as mentioned before.
A plaster that is beneficial for pain and stiffness. It consists of: ground fenugreek, crushed chickpeas, burnt human hair or bird feathers, saltpeter, salt, and equal parts of each ingredient are mixed together. The amount of plaster to be applied depends on the size of the bandage.
Then, if it is removed [the plaster], one should observe: if there is no pain, itching or movement of the broken bone from its position, then do not remove it too soon. But if there is severe itching, distressing pain or swelling at the site, then remove it.
So act quickly and remove it [the plaster] at the right time, without delay. Then take a soft cloth or sponge soaked in warm water, wring it out and wash the area until the itching stops and the pain subsides. Then allow the limb to rest for a while.
Then, apply clean, soaked wool in vinegar and oil or rose oil ointment, wrap it around the area overnight to prevent hot swelling and reduce inflammation. Then resume gentle traction and light plastering without applying as much tension as before.
And treat it until it heals. If you find that the swelling, redness, pain, inflammation and all symptoms have disappeared completely and there is no longer a need for plastering and traction, then repeat the original procedure if necessary, otherwise leave it alone.
Remove the plaster after three, four, five or seven days, or even up to twenty days depending on how the limb appears as mentioned earlier, until the fracture has healed and the flesh has adhered.
So increase the tension at this point beyond the initial traction, and also increase the thickness of the diet for the patient as mentioned earlier. If you find that the fracture site has become dry and wasted more than it should, then know that the nutrition is not reaching it.
Wash it with warm water every time you remove the plaster, doing so every three days and slightly reducing the tension. By this action, nutrition is brought to the limb and it heals quickly. However, do not follow those who foolishly break the limb again.
If he does not first attempt what is necessary, and attempts instead something twisted, it is an error on his part and a great fault. Had it been correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books, and I would have acted upon it; yet I have not seen any trace of it among them at all. It is correct to not act upon it,
Chapter Two.
On Fractures in the Head.
The types of fractures that occur in the head are numerous and varied, with different causes. Some fractures result from a sword blow, causing either all the bone to break off up to the diaphragm underneath the bone. As happens with wood.
And this type of fracture is called 'qadumi', and sometimes the sword cuts only part of the bone, breaking its surface without penetrating completely; this kind is called an 'absolute excision'. The wound in both types may be large or small.
Among fractures are those that are crushed or depressed, caused by a blow with a stone or falling onto one; these may either approach the membrane beneath the bone or remain on its surface.
Both types of fractures may result in a large or small wound. Another type is a hidden fracture within the bone, which causes only slight concussion.
This type is called 'shari'i' fracture. Another type occurs from a fall or stone blow, causing the bone's surface to sink inward like dented copper.
More often than not, this occurs in the soft bones of children's heads. All these types of fractures may have sharp and blunt fragments, which will be discussed later along with their treatment. First, we shall identify all these types of fractures by examining them.
The hairy type can be identified by uncovering the bone and wiping it with ink; if a fracture is present, it will appear black. As for treating fractures, first consider the patient's symptoms.
If the patient displays obvious symptoms indicative of fear, such as vomiting blood, seizures, loss of consciousness, fainting, high fever, and intense eye redness or similar signs, do not approach him or attempt treatment. Death is likely in most cases with these symptoms.
Should you encounter symptoms that do not alarm you and hope for safety, then treat him at the onset of his injury. This is especially so if it occurred during the winter days.
It is not advisable to attempt removing the bone before the fourteenth day, even if it were in summer. It is preferable to hasten its removal before the seventh day lest the tissue beneath the bone becomes corrupted and exhibits the symptoms previously described.
If a bone fracture has reached the dura mater surrounding the brain, and is accompanied by bruising and inflammation, then cut away the affected portion as I have described. First, shave the head of the injured patient.
Upon exposing the bone, proceed according to what is feasible and based on the shape of the wound and the patient's condition. If you find bleeding or a hot swelling around the bone during examination, respond accordingly.
And you should fill the site with a linen cloth soaked in a decoction of rose and honey, then press it to reduce swelling and secure against bleeding. Afterwards, begin to extract the bone fragment by one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a fine, narrow-bladed knife; its shape is as follows:
And then use another cutting edge that is slightly broader than the first one; its shape is as follows:
And use another cutting edge that is even broader than the second one. It is essential to have various cutting edges of different widths and lengths, all being very sharp at their tips, made from Indian steel or good quality iron.
And strike gently on the cutting edge so as not to dislodge the head and cause injury. If the bone is hard and sturdy, do not drill around it with those drills called 'non-immersed drills' before using your cutting edges.
For these drills do not exceed the thickness of the skull bone due to their round tip below the sharp point, resembling a small ring or circle that prevents them from penetrating too deeply into the bone.
It is necessary for you to prepare many of these drills in various sizes, each suitable for the thickness of a bone, so that you may have for every skull a drill whose tip's length and width match the thickness of that skull. Here are three types. Of the drills, one is large, another medium, and the third small:
And the manner of drilling around a broken bone is that you place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you feel that the bone has been penetrated, then move the drill to another location, leaving about the thickness of a reed between each drill.
Then cut with the cutting tools between each pair of drills, doing so with as much gentleness as possible, until you can remove the bone either by hand or using some other tool prepared for that purpose.
Such as the forceps and gentle tongs, and it is necessary to be very careful that the drill or cutting tool does not touch anything of the peritoneum. When you have completed the drilling and freed the bone from the adhering peritoneum, if any, do not strip it.
And smooth the roughness of what remains of the bone with another tool resembling the cutting tool, but it should be finer and gentler than the rest. If there remain any small bones or fragments, remove them gently using whatever tools you have.
Then treat the wound afterwards with dressings and ointments as mentioned. The other aspect of this procedure is a simple one, far from error, as Galen has mentioned and greatly praised, and these are his words: It is necessary for you first to expose a portion of the bone from the place where the fracture is most severe and prominent, so that when you have exposed that portion, you may insert under it the tip of this adze-shaped cutting tool, which looks like this:
So that the adze-shaped part is smooth and does not cut anything, while its sharp edge is on the sides extending lengthwise as you see here, so that the adze-shaped part rests on the peritoneum and the cutting edge faces the bone, then strike the tool from one side only.
With a small hammer such that it can gently break all the bone, as if rotating while being secure from the membrane; no frightening incident will occur, even if the practitioner is the most ignorant and clumsy of people. Indeed, even if he were asleep.
If there remains any portion of the membrane attached to the bone in certain places, scrape it off gently using the edge of the same cutting instrument; this will detach it without harm or fear.
But if the bone fracture did not reach the membrane and what was broken off from the bone is its tip with roughness and small splinters remaining, then it is necessary to remove that roughness and extract those splinters gently using a variety of soft forceps of different sizes suitable for each situation according to the requirement of the task.
And regarding the broken bone end and its shape, it is preferable to first use the widest forceps for removing the bone, then proceed with narrower ones, continuing in this manner until the finest and thinnest are used.
As for minor cracks and gentle fractures, each one should be treated according to what promotes its healing, which is evident to anyone with even the slightest practice in this field; refer to what has been written and explained regarding large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, take a piece of cotton cloth corresponding to the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil; then place it on the mouth of the wound, followed by taking another folded or triangular piece of cloth. Soak the second cloth in rose water and oil, then place it on top of the first one; do this as gently as possible to avoid weighing down the abdominal cavity. Then use a broad bandage from above without pulling too tightly, only enough to hold the wound closed.
Leave it under this dressing for a day or two until the heat of the inflammation is secured, then remove it and afterwards use powders made from beneficial ingredients such as onion bulb, crushed cinnamon, almond flour, and caraway seeds among others.
Press on the wound as it is dry and generally use a medicine that cleanses without causing inflammation. Make every effort in treating the wound to keep it clean, without fat or dirt, and do not allow pus to accumulate.
If pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and inflames it, causing great harm to the patient. It may also appear in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if left untreated.
If blackness appears on its surface, know that if the patient exhibits the symptoms mentioned, it is certain that he will die. However, if this blackness occurs due to a medicine applied to it, then the power of that medicine caused this blackness.
It is necessary to take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, and apply it to a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum. Then treat him with appropriate remedies until he recovers.
Chapter Three.
How to set a broken nose.
Know that the upper part of the nose alone breaks, as it is made of bone. The lower part consists of cartilage and does not break; rather, it experiences swelling, dislocation, and fracture.
If one of its sides is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and use your index finger from inside and your thumb from outside to realign the fracture gently, without causing pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, use a thick round-tipped instrument to realign it. If both sides are broken, do likewise. Perform this reduction on the first day after the fracture.
If you cannot reduce the fracture on the first day, wait until the seventh or tenth day after the swelling has subsided. Then insert a wick of cotton gauze into the nostril, using one wick for a unilateral fracture and two wicks for bilateral fractures.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril, and some earlier cauterizers have mentioned that the wick should be smeared with fat and changed daily. However, I do not recommend this; rather, it should be dipped in beaten egg white mixed with flour.
Then leave the wick until the bone hardens and the cartilage stiffens. You may also insert pipes of goose feathers into the nostril where the wick was placed, after wrapping them in soft cloth to strengthen the packing and prevent breathing difficulties.
If you wish, you may perform this procedure or not. If the nostril becomes inflamed during your work and a hot swelling occurs, apply cotton soaked in cypress oil or hydrated lime water, rose oil, or Diacelon ointment to the nostril.
