If no heat inflammation occurs, it is preferable to apply a poultice made of semolina flour and almond dough mixed with beaten egg white from outside the nostril without any binding. If the nasal bones are broken slightly, this will not cause any harm.
Or if it is crumbled, you should incise upon it and remove it with the appropriate instrument. Then suture the incision and treat it with a suitable adhesive ointment that promotes healing. If an injury occurs inside the nostril, treat it with the wick and use lead pipes. Until healed.
The Fourth Chapter.
If the lower lip is fractured...
If the lower lip is broken without laceration, its recognition is easy.
It is necessary that if the fracture is on the right side, you insert your left hand's index finger into the patient's mouth and likewise for the left side with the right hand to gently elevate the fractured prominence from inside out.
And with your other hand from outside the bone, you guide it to align the fracture. If the jawbone is fractured in two places, then use a splint on both sides straight until alignment is achieved. If the fracture occurs at the teeth level.
And you should separate it by pulling what has been hoped to remain with a golden or silver thread, or a brass one. Then place it on the broken beard's tip, followed by a folded cloth placed over it, then cover this with a tight bandage or a piece of leather shoe sole equal in length to the beard. Then secure it from above however you prefer, binding it and compressing it tightly so that it does not dislodge. Command the patient to rest quietly and make his food soft and nourishing. If on the third day you find that any part of the shape has changed in any way, hasten to release it.
Then secure it from above however you prefer, binding it and compressing it tightly so that it does not dislodge. Command the patient to rest quietly and make his food soft and nourishing. If on the third day you find that any part of the shape has changed in any way, hasten to release it. Then repair what you have bound with flour and egg white, or fine barley meal after removing the catgut. Apply this gently to the bandage, so long as it adheres without changing the bone's condition, leave it.
Do not remove it until the wound heals and the fracture solidifies. Often this happens in three weeks. If inflammation appears during that time, use what has been mentioned repeatedly to alleviate it until the swelling subsides.
Now, if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, observe whether any bone splinters or fragments have become separated from it. If so, carefully remove those splinters using whatever tool you prefer to use for their extraction. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, dilate it with scissors according to your need.
Then, after removing those splinters and leaving nothing of them behind, if the mouth of the wound is wide, stitch it up; otherwise, apply one of the salves suitable for that purpose. And allow the wound to heal.
Chapter Five.
On Setting a Collapsed Rib.
The rib most commonly fractures at its anterior end near the shoulder joint, and this fracture usually occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks into two pieces without any fragments being detached.
Or it may occur with fragments detached, which is more difficult to reduce; or it may be accompanied by a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants ready: one to hold down the arm adjacent to the fractured rib, and another to extend the neck towards the opposite side.
Then, align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be without any offset or distortion. If you need more extension, place a ball of rags or wool under the patient's armpit.
And its bone should be of the required length, extending and raising the clavicle while pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture aligns as it should. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has sunk too deeply, then the patient should lie on their back.
Then place a moderate cushion under the shoulder blade and press down on it with your hand until the depressed clavicle rises upwards. Then adjust the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken off and is moving around, then carefully incise upon it and remove that fragment gently. But if the fragment is embedded in the bone, then change your approach to cutting it out with one of the prepared saws. Afterward, place underneath the clavicle an instrument that holds the diaphragm in place, made of wood or iron, and shaped as follows:
Similar to a spoon without distortion, its width should correspond to the size of the bone needed, and its length according to what is practical for working with. It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower as shown.
If the wound made for removing the bone fragment is wide and there is no fear of bleeding, then close the wound edges with stitches. But if the wound is small or you are worried about bleeding, then pack the wound with rags and dressings according to its size.
If there is active bleeding, then pack the wound with a mixture of rose oil and honey or vinegar and hold it in place. For reducing a fracture without an incision, apply a bandage made from flour mixed with egg whites onto the bone. Then place the linen splint on it, and if necessary, place a ball under the armpit. Take a long bandage with a width of about an inch or more, then fold it over the splint and bandage. Finally, take a thin wooden board for a sling. It should be three fingers wide and likewise in length, then wrap it in a bandage, then insert this bandage with the dressing into the position on the turban that corresponds to the fracture site, then tie the turban over the fracture as usual. And here it is on his neck and under his armpit, both his own and the patient's. You have seen how tightening sets the fracture correctly, which should be clear to you.
And all of this indicates that the dressing should not be removed from the broken bone. Therefore, you should visit the patient every day. Whenever the bandage slackens and you see that the dressing has shifted, adjust it and tighten the bandage again. Then have the patient sleep on his back with a small pillow under his armpit at night.
Lift his arm with a pillow so that the collarbone fracture is raised as high as the shoulder, or tie his arm to his neck. Do not untie the bandage until twelve days have passed, unless there is an incident of itching or swelling at the site.
Then renew the dressing if necessary. Tighten it and leave him like that until the fracture becomes firm and solid, which should happen within twenty-eight days. And for some people, it may take less than that.
Chapter Six.
Chapter Six: Setting a Shoulder Fracture. The shoulder blade rarely breaks in its broad part; rather, it is the points that break. When a point or the center breaks, it can be identified by touch. Set the fracture according to its shape and form.
Set it back into its natural shape as much as possible, then apply a plaster made of flour, egg whites, and linseed oil over the site. Then place a wide dressing made of linen on top.
Make it as wide as the shoulder blade or slightly wider. If there is any unevenness under the dressing, smooth it out with linseed oil until the dressing lies flat, then tie it securely with a long turban from above.
Press the dressing firmly and ensure the bandage remains in place. Check the knot every day, tightening it whenever it loosens. If the dressing slips from its position, reposition it. Have the patient lie on their right side with their shoulder elevated.
After one to twenty-five days, when this period is complete and you are secure that the bone will not break or become dislocated, untie the bandage. If a splinter of bone protrudes and pierces beneath the skin, make an incision over it and remove it. And perform what has been mentioned regarding relieving inflammation in collarbone fractures if such a situation arises.
Chapter Seven.
On the treatment of rib fractures.
When the ribs are broken in the middle, it rarely occurs. However, their ends often break and shatter. Symptoms include severe pain when leaning forward, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
And perhaps blood will spurt out, and the cracking of the broken bone is evident to the touch. For treatment, have the patient lie on their back with a pillow between their shoulder blades. Then press down on their elbows, bring together the ribs at both sides using your hands, and smooth them as much as possible.
And arrange it until the bone returns to its proper shape. Then apply a bandage and padding, placing on top of it a splint made from a thin strip of willow or elder bark, wrapped in cloth. Then carefully tie the bandage around the broken bone so that it does not slip off. Pass the knot around to the back repeatedly, tightening it firmly each time. Then check the knot at all times and tighten it whenever it loosens.
However, if necessity demands removing the bandage due to pain or swelling at the site, promptly do so. Remove the padding and treat the matter as needed, then replace the padding if appropriate. And press it firmly until healed.
Chapter Eight.
If a rib is fractured, know that the fracture occurs only in the hard places near the back and its extremities facing forward. This is because they are cartilaginous, and this can be detected by touch during examination with fingers.
And reduce it by aligning the fracture with your fingers on the convex surface until the shape is as it should be, then bandage and apply traction to the fractured bone if necessary. If the rib fracture is inclined inward, it causes severe pain in the chest.
And like a cough, which those who have it suffer from because the fractured bone irritates the pleura and causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and spitting up blood. This is difficult to treat, and many methods were used by the ancients for its treatment.
Some said it is appropriate to make the diet of the patient produce wind and gas so that the abdomen swells, expands, and pushes the fracture outward. We disagree with this because it may cause inflammation if there was none, or increase it if it had already occurred.
Some recommended placing a cup on the site and applying suction with force, but we fear that it may draw the skin towards the site due to its weakness. Others suggested covering the site with wool soaked in hot oil.
And pack the space between the ribs until it is filled so that the bandage becomes even when wound around. Then treat the patient for cough with diet and medication. If the patient's condition is severe, they will not be able to endure it. And the fractured bone was irritating the pleura with a harmful cough. We feared for the patient and therefore it is necessary to incise at the site and expose the fractured rib, then place underneath it the apparatus that protects the peritoneum (which has been described earlier) and cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then bring together the edges of the wound if it is large by suturing and dress it with ointment until it heals. If inflammation occurs during this time, quickly apply poultices of rose oil on the site and treat the patient internally with remedies that calm inflammation.
He lies on the side that is difficult for him to sleep until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
Force beads onto the back and neck.
If bones of the neck are broken, little harm comes to it. Most often this happens due to dropsy (edema), and also with the vertebrae of the spine. If this happens to someone and you want to know whether it will heal or not: If you see that his hands have become paralyzed, numb, and lifeless, unable to move them or open or close them, and if you prick him with a needle or press on them hard, he does not feel it nor does he complain of pain, then know that in most cases he will not recover; he is doomed.
But if he moves them and feels the pinching and pressing, know that the marrow of the bone is intact, and the patient will recover with treatment. If a similar condition affects the spine and you want to know whether it will recover or not:
If you see that his legs have also become paralyzed, and what happened to the hands has happened to them too, then if he lies on his back, wind and feces come out without his will, and if he lies on his belly, urine comes out without his will. But if he lies on his back and cannot control urination, then know that he is doomed; do not bother with treating him. But if none of this happens, the case is easier to treat. The treatment for what has happened is to apply a plaster to relieve the hot swelling.
That is, you apply pure rose oil alone or with fried garlic cloves to the affected vertebrae three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then apply one of the strong, moistened bandages and bind it tightly with a linen cloth, commanding him to remain still and quiet.
He should not sleep except on the side where he does not feel pain until he recovers. But if a fragment of bone has come off due to dropsy or something similar that has healed, do not cut into his skin and remove that bone. Then bring the edges of the wound together if it is large with stitches, and treat it with healing salves until it heals. But if the last vertebra of the spine, which is the tailbone, is broken, do not insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus.
The broken bone is reduced with the other hand according to its capability and then secured with a splint and bandage if necessary.
If you feel a fractured splinter therein, cut upon it and remove it, treating the wound as mentioned previously until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
To treat a hip fracture.
When the thigh bones do not break easily, if they do break, it is usually in such a way that the ends shatter and split lengthwise, tilting inward, exposing the marrow, causing pain in the area, numbness, and weakness in the limb.
And treat it by running your hand along it until you reach the fracture, and if the fracture is only at its ends, reduce that fracture according to what is feasible for you until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has tilted inward, place the patient on their stomach to facilitate reduction of that fracture. Once reduced, apply a splint, then place a bandage made of wood or leather over it and secure it.
Do not fear displacement of the fracture or loosening of the splint, and pack the hollows between the toes with material that fills them until tension is evenly distributed. Order the patient to sleep on their back or unaffected side, if swelling becomes inflamed.
Stop massaging it and treat it until the inflammation subsides, then apply what is necessary to relieve it as mentioned previously. Then return to its reduction and secure it as appropriate. If there are splinters or fractures at its ends, do not remove them.
And do not touch it but reduce it from outside, as we have mentioned, and leave the splint until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On treating fractures of the arm.
The elbow is between the wrist and the shoulder. If it fractures, treat it in one of two ways. Take a smooth, hardened rod of medium thickness.
Tie two bands at its ends, hang it from a high place, and have the patient sit on a chair. Then place their fractured arm on the rod so that their armpit adheres to its middle curve. Then hang something heavy above it.
And a servant extends him to the bottom, then the physician sets the bone with both hands until it returns to its proper position. Alternatively, the patient lies on their back and their arm is suspended from their neck by a bandage.
Then, two servants are ordered: one to hold above the fracture with their hands and another below it. Each pulls in his direction. If you wish for greater traction, tie a bandage beneath the fracture and another above it, and each servant pulls towards his side.
If the fracture is near the elbow, then the bandage should be placed midway between the armpit and the elbow. Similarly, if it is near the wrist, the bandage should be placed on that spot and also on the wrist itself.
Then, the fracture is gently aligned without force until it stands correctly and fits well. If there is no signs of inflammation, then it should be tied. But if there are signs of heat, leave tying for seven days, then apply a bandage soaked in vinegar and smeared with rose oil.
Until the inflammation subsides, then it may be tied. The method of tying is: place a bandage on the fracture, then wrap a fresh cloth around the bandage, bring the arm close to the chest and place the hand open on the shoulder.
And hold the cloth and tie it to the elbow and arm, so that the arm acts as a splint if there is no obstacle preventing this. If you fear any change in the broken bone, then use splints.
And place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than others, leaving one finger's width between each splint. Let the length of the splints be according to the fracture plus three fingers' width on both sides.
Then tie them with the bandage mentioned at the beginning of this book.
And that the tying be tighter at the site of the fracture.
And as the distance from the fracture increases, the tension should decrease. If you see that the splinting and tying have been done correctly at this moment, then proceed.
If you fear inflammation from the hot tumor, leave the bandage and splints for seven days as we have said. Then examine the ligature every three days to prevent irritation, swelling, or obstruction of nutrition to the member due to excessive tightening; all this should be managed as mentioned.
If you are confident that nothing of that sort will happen, do not remove the ligature until many days have passed. The patient should lie on their back with their hand on their abdomen and a moderately warm woolen pad placed under their armpit.
And examine at all times, day or night, to ensure that the shape of the broken member does not alter or the ligature relax. Restore all this with effort; maintain the patient's diet on the level previously mentioned, starting with soft food until the bone begins to harden.
It is not permissible to make the patient's diet harsh, for it is usual for the arm and leg to harden in forty days. At that point, the ligature should be removed and the patient should use the bath and treatments appropriate for such fractures.
Do not remove the ligature until fifty days or two months have passed.
Chapter Twelve.
On setting a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius and ulna. One, the smaller radius, lies next to the thumb; the other, the larger ulna, is situated beneath the radius at its lower end. Either bone may fracture alone or both may fracture together.
If the upper end of the radius fractures, its reduction is easy and its approximation is quicker. If the lower end fractures, its reduction is difficult and its approximation is slow. The most difficult case is when both bones are fractured together.
The doctor should make the reduction gently and carefully until it is complete. If only the ulna is fractured, the reduction should be made more forcefully; if both bones are fractured together, the reduction should be made very strongly indeed.
The hand's shape should be placed with its palm downwards on a cushion, the fingers stretched out, and the thumb raised above all other fingers. The little finger should be lower than the others, while the wrist is bent beneath itself.
And let the cushion be at his level so that the patient does not exert effort.
Then an assistant extends the arm from below, either by hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extends it from above. The doctor then aligns the bone until it returns to its best possible position; if there are fragments of bone, they should be removed.
Return each fragment to its place as best you can. If there is a protruding fragment that pierces the skin and cannot be reduced, incise over it and remove it according to the method mentioned earlier. If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, proceed accordingly.
If inflammation and heat appear at the beginning of reduction, take a cloth moistened with rose oil and white wax, neither too thick nor too thin, and apply it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the cotton and apply the prepared bandage, dusted with flour and egg white. Carry out the splinting, ensuring that the splint placed directly on the fracture is wider and stronger. Know that in most cases, there are six splints for the arm.
If the fracture is in one elbow or both, make the bandage around the fracture site tighter and stronger. Whenever you turn the bandage upwards or downwards, loosen it slightly as mentioned earlier.
And let the bandages wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not too hard. Let the thread used to tie them be made of medium-weight cotton, as described earlier. Examine the limb and the bandage after a few days.
If something needs to be fixed, such as itching appearing on the limb, warm water should be used to wash the limb until the itching subsides. Leave the limb unbound overnight so that it can rest, then resume bandaging.
If the tension has relaxed and the bone has been removed, or something similar, then restore that entirely with your utmost effort. Also examine whether food can reach the member due to excessive tension; if not, then slightly relax it and leave it for some days until food reaches it, then tighten it again.
If nothing as mentioned appears on the patient, then it is not permissible to remove the bandage until after twenty days or thereabouts. Then tie the patient's hand to his neck with the arm held evenly and the patient should exert himself in avoiding sudden movements, and let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days, or sometimes forty-three days, perhaps even twenty-eight days, all according to the states of temperaments and strengths.
Chapter Thirteen.
On Setting Fractures of the Hand Palm and Fingers.
Fractures of the hand palm and splints of the fingers rarely occur, but injuries do happen often. So when a fracture or injury occurs in the hand...
The patient should sit with his legs crossed before him on an even chair.
Then he should place his hand on it extended, then the bonesetter extends the broken bone and the doctor aligns it until it bonds well. Then, if there is no hot swelling, a splint bandage is applied, followed by a plaster cast according to the location.
And if the fracture is towards the palm of the hand, then make a ball-like shape from cloth and tell the patient to grasp it with his fractured hand. Then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the plaster cast be made of soft leather that adapts to the skin and allows for bending of the whole hand. If the fracture is on the outside, then make another plaster cast from above and another from below the hand to keep it open and upright.
Then apply tension as the hand rotates and tie between the fingers. If the fracture is in one of the finger splints, especially the thumb, then align it properly and bind it with the hand. You may also wish to make a small upright plaster cast for it.
Let it not be moved, if the fracture is in other fingers like the middle or index or thumb or ring finger, then align and bind it with the next healthy one, or bind them all together. This is better. Alternatively, place a small upright splint on it.
As we said concerning the thumb, observe carefully during the setting and afterwards keep it away from heat. If anything happens as described repeatedly, deal with it accordingly.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
On setting fractures of the thighbone.
The femur often breaks and is recognized by its forward or backward displacement. Its reduction involves pulling a band above the fracture and another below it, while the patient lies on their face.
Then each assistant extends their band gently towards its side, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone. But if it is near the hip's origin, a soft band like wool should be used.
At the hip's origin towards the pubic area, so that the first band passes upwards and the other below the fracture. Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the band should be placed near it so that its end is downwards. Then the physician uses both hands to realign the bone until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone should be immobilized well. If there is no inflammation of the limb, then start applying bandages and traction. But if inflammation occurs, wait until it subsides before resuming treatment.
And for traction, use a broad, firm band twice or thrice around the fracture without tightening it too much. Then fold the leg so that the heel is near the buttock, and insert a long thread between the thigh and leg near the knee from below. The ends of the thread should be above on both sides.
Then wind the remaining bandage around both the thigh and leg. Place splints on the thigh at the fracture site, making one splint for the leg bone. Then fill the space between thigh and leg with soft cotton to balance traction.
Then begin by applying strong traction from the center over the fracture site for three or four turns, and each time you bind further away from the fracture site, let your traction be gentler and milder and relaxed.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, tie with it what is next from above of the ligatures, then pass one end of the thread down until it reaches the ankle, and tie with it also the other ends of the ligatures on the opposite side.
So that the bandage does not slip from its place, then leave the traction on it until the limb does not complain of pain or swelling or inflammation or suchlike. For if any such thing occurs, hasten to untie it and rectify all that has happened according to what we have mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a splinter of bone that has pierced, do not try to reduce it if you are able; otherwise, cut over it and remove it, and treat the wound as mentioned previously until it heals. For this fracture may be reduced in the thigh without adding ligatures to the leg.
As we mentioned regarding the arm and forearm, except that if it is reduced alone without including the leg, its owner will certainly remain lame forever.
Know that the thigh becomes strong in fifty days or a little more or less, all according to the varying constitutions and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteen
On fractures of the knee-cap.
The knee-cap rarely fractures, but dislocation occurs frequently. If it does fracture, it is either aulsion or fragmentation of its parts, with or without wounding.
Examine all of this by touch, and reduce it by pressing together what has been displaced of its parts with your fingers until it meets and fits together according to your ability in reduction, gentleness, and care. Then apply a bandage and use a padded splint if necessary.
Apply traction above according to that, then follow all the conditions mentioned in other fractures such as heat and inflammation etc., until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen
On the reduction of fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones: one thick called the tibia, and another thin called the fibula; they are subject to various types of fractures similar to those of the arm. Therefore, its reduction is like that of the arm, whether one or both bones are fractured. If both are broken, the leg will be displaced in all directions; if only the thin bone is broken, it will be displaced forward; and if only the thick bone is broken near the bottom, this should not escape your notice.
Apply traction and reduction, and bind the splints according to the fracture's condition. However, if it is a severe fracture with many fragments, use gentler traction and be careful when reducing it.
In addition to what is done for the arm, when you have finished applying the splints and completing your work on the leg, take two small pieces of pinewood used for flattening the spaces between the cracks in boards or made from date palm ribs.
Select two pieces that are slightly thick but not too thin, and their length should match the distance from the knee to the ankle. Then wrap each piece with cloth twice its length, placing one on either side of the leg.
Secure the splints at three points on both ends and in the middle to prevent the leg from moving sideways. You may also use a wooden splint along the length of the leg for additional support.
Insert it to keep the fracture from moving, especially if there is an associated wound. Then examine the leg daily and pay close attention to any swelling, inflammation, or other signs. If any of these issues arise, treat them appropriately until they heal. The tibia usually heals in about thirty days or thereabouts.
Chapter Seventeen
On fractures of the foot and toes.
The heel rarely fractures, but bones in the foot may fracture, as can the toes, although dislocation is more common.
If a fracture of the leg bones is exposed and you see that some of them have approached each other, then the patient should place his foot on the ground upright as if walking. Then you come and place your foot on what has risen among those bones and press and smooth it.
Until they return to their places, then bring the splint and the bandage from above and place a small board with two flat ends under the foot. Then tie the foot firmly after wrapping it with cloth and other necessary materials.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage. If you find that the bones are aligned and were previously broken or dislocated, proceed accordingly. But if only some of the fingers were broken, straighten them and align them as I have described for the reduction of fingers.
Then make a splint for the broken finger along its length, slightly wider. Then place the board I have described under the foot and tie it firmly if two or three or more bones were broken.
Make a splint for each finger according to its size, tapered at both ends and wrapped in soft cloth. Tie the board under the foot and ensure that each end of the board rests outside the sole of the foot for proper adjustment.
And you must not forget to treat all fractures according to the signs that have been mentioned and apply the appropriate treatment for each condition.
Chapter Eighteen.
Chapter 18: Fracture of the woman's hipbone and pubic bone in men.
When the woman's pubic bone is fractured, make her sit upright. Then tilt her slightly backwards and hold her from behind. The midwife should then pack cotton into her vagina until it is full and forms a ball-like shape.
Then shake the woman gently and raise her pelvis slightly. The cotton will come out until it is at the entrance of the vagina like a ball, allowing the bone to realign. Then place a support on her back. Whenever she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can do so.
Return it as it was initially placed, then go back to its original position. Do this for seven days or thereabouts; it will then be healed. If you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie a reed tube onto its mouth and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow air forcefully into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vagina, causing the fracture to heal. Then fill it with cotton and maintain it as described until healed. If a bone in the pubic region of a man or woman is fractured, this treatment should be used.
And it is to be done as we have described for the hip bone. And you should not be in doubt about these strange fractures, which occur infrequently, especially if you have some experience and understand this book well, since the fracture itself usually indicates how to treat it.
Understand the correct method of its reduction and immobilization. As for a man whose thighbone has been fractured, insert the bone of a goose's leg into him, then apply a bandage made of cloth and leave it on for three days or thereabouts until healed.
Chapter Nineteen
In the reduction of fractures when accompanied by wounds.
It is necessary, especially if a large bone such as the thighbone or forearm is fractured accompanied by a wound, that you act swiftly and perform phlebotomy immediately, provided you meet the conditions for bloodletting as previously mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding profusely, you should immediately apply powdered soda to it. If nothing else is available, then proceed with reducing the fracture on that same day without delay.
If there is no inflammation, then proceed with reduction. But if there is inflammation, leave it until the ninth day to allow the inflammation to subside. Do not approach the fracture on the third and fourth days, as it will have adverse symptoms.
If the fractured bone is exposed and protruding through the skin, you should gently reduce and align it with your hands. If this is not possible, use this instrument made of iron, approximately seven or eight fingers long. Display it according to the extent of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should take from it three or four pieces according to what is needed for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be thick enough so that they do not bend when pressed upon during work.
And it should have a sharp edge with a bend at its tip, being thicker above and much thinner below; this is its shape:
It is called in Greek "perma," meaning a small lever, and its sharp bent tip should be placed against the protruding end of the bone and pushed gently until the bone returns to place and the edges of the fracture are even.
If its broken tip is thin and the tool has not grasped it well, then cut off that tip of the bone so that the tool can grip it. If you are still unable to reduce the bone as described, use one of the cuts mentioned earlier that resembles it.
Or else, spread it with one of the saws as much as possible, then remove any roughness or splinters from the remaining bone. If after reducing the bone there is severe, painful swelling, know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can return it to its natural position, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. After completing the reduction of the bone, dip a cloth in black adhesive syrup, especially if it is summer, and do not apply any ointment containing oil to the wound.
So that no gangrene or corruption occurs, then use dressings during the setting of the bone and leave the wound open by cutting a hole in the bandages corresponding to the size of the wound.
Be very careful not to pull on the wound while reducing the fracture, as many ignorant physicians have done this in the past and caused either death or shortening of limbs for their patients. Your pulling should be gentle and yielding, unlike the pulling used for other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some of the bad symptoms described, and there is persistent pain in the area, do not apply dressings. Instead, make a bandage from thick cloth and use it to wrap the wound tightly.
If after a day or two you find that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the bandage placed on it with wine, then apply the wrappings and ointments which we usually use for wounds, such as the 'Rabbee' ointment and similar ones. It is also necessary for you to loosen the bandage.
And examine the wound every day, morning and evening, until it heals and is cured. And you should elevate the limb so that pus flows downwards easily. But if many days have passed and the wound has not healed nor has the pus ceased, know that there are small bone fragments still present.
It is not necessary to search for the wound with forceps; those fragments that are not exposed should be removed and extracted. And those that are not exposed but are causing pain and penetrating the flesh, remove them by cutting around them as much as you can.
However, if there is inflammation, swelling, or any other type of corruption and foulness in the wound, you should treat each manifestation with its appropriate remedy, as described in this book.
And what you should not do is attempt to remove or extract a large broken bone such as the femur or humerus. For often death occurs from attempting this, so it is better left alone until it decays and falls off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
So treat the wound if you see a place where treatment can be applied; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
In treating the complication that occurs after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after healing of a fracture, especially near joints, causing the limb to become deformed or even disabling it from performing its natural function.
If the complication is fresh, apply medicines that constrict such as aloes, storax, myrrh, frankincense, and coloquintida amongst others. Take some or all of these and mix them with an astringent liquid or egg white or vinegar.
Place it on a complicated pulley system, secure it well with a good knot, and leave the tension for many days without releasing it, then release it and bind another until the complication is gone, if Allah wills. Or you may fasten upon it a tight plate of lead.
If lead has the property of removing all that has collected in the organs, then if the complication has hardened and become severe and necessity has compelled its removal, cut through it from above and sever or strip off the protruding part with some scissors until it is gone, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating fractures, if the member remains thin afterwards, unlike its original condition.
If a fracture of the bones has occurred and the limb remained afterwards thin and weak, then that may be for many reasons. One of those reasons may be either excessive cutting and improper tying of the ligaments, or over-tightening of the ligaments causing food to cease reaching the limb, or excessive rubbing, or excessive movements out of their proper time, or insufficient blood supply in the body of the patient and its weakness.
And its treatment is to nourish the patient and enrich his body until blood increases therein, and the use of baths, causing him pleasure and joy, and similar things. Then apply ointment on the limb so that it may draw much food onto it, or continue rubbing with warm water. Until nourishment is restored therein and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In treating broken bones that have healed crookedly and have prevented proper functioning:
If a limb has healed after breaking with distortion, or shortening of the broken bone, or complication, causing deformity of the member, but if the limb has not been prevented from performing its natural function, then it is not permissible to accept the claim of those who assert that the bone should be broken from the head, as many ignorants among physicians and bone-setters do in our land.
And this action is greatly reprehensible, leading to severe harm and incurable injury. But if the distortion and complication are fresh, it is permissible to rub with warm water in which soothing herbs have been boiled, such as papyrus leaves, their roots, and celery, etc.
Wid'i madh al-'iqdimat al-murakhiat ka-l-diyakhalun al-muhkam as-sin'ah aw yutakhaddam 'ala abu al-khidmah wa-yudammad bih.
And the distortion is dissolved by gentle, continuous friction using the hands. Movement of the joint in all directions at all times is employed; however, if the deformity has progressed and hardened and its treatment requires iron.
It is necessary to cut above it, release the connection of the bone, and sever any remaining deformity or bone with fine cutting tools, using care and gentleness. Then treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three
The jawbone is a dislocated joint that has moved from its proper place, causing immobility and deformity of the member. It also causes severe pain and aching in the patient. Therefore, when dislocation of one of the jaws occurs, it should be immediately reduced.
Never delay it; for if delayed, the place swells and it becomes difficult to reduce the jawbone. Therefore, during swelling, it should not be moved or extended lest the patient suffer from convulsions and severe pain.
However, if it does occur, bleeding the patient first should be done, then left until the swelling subsides somewhat. Then the member is softened with warm water and oil, and gently reduced. Each member should be treated according to what is mentioned in its respective place.
And the chapters on the jaw have also been arranged as mentioned earlier regarding fractures, from top to bottom of the body.
Chapter Twenty-Four
On treating the lower jawbone.
The mandible rarely dislocates except in rare cases. Its dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either it partially slips out from its position and slightly relaxes, or it completely dislocates and fully relaxes. To the extent of the chest until saliva flows from the patient and he is unable to hold it back, nor can he bear to close his jaw or control his tongue for speech. But if he recovers quickly, he often returns to himself easily.
And if the dislocation is complete, do not rush to restore it quickly nor delay it unduly. A servant should hold the patient's head steady and the doctor should insert his thumb into the origin of the jaw inside the mouth on one side.
Or he may insert both thumbs if the jaw is dislocated on both sides, using his other fingers from outside to align it. He should then instruct the patient to relax their jaw and move it in all directions, while the doctor guides the jaw back into its proper place.
If returning the jaw is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, use compresses with hot water and oil to facilitate their reduction without delay. After they have returned and aligned, and the patient's mouth has closed but not relaxed, place dressings of linen soaked in a mixture of wax, oil, and rose.
Then bind it gently with a soft bandage, and have the patient lie on their back with their head propped up between two pillows to prevent movement from side to side. They should not chew anything but take only soft foods.
Until the pain has gone and the jaw has healed, he should eat gently without forcing his mouth open when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is fully recovered.
If the jaws cannot be reduced and do not return to their proper places, this often results in inflammation, persistent headache, abdominal pain, vomiting, or other ailments. If you observe these symptoms, know that the patient is severely afflicted.
He may die from this condition within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On the Reduction of the Shoulder Blade and Upper Arm.
As for the shoulder blade, it does not dislocate inwardly due to its attachment to the chest. If it dislocates outwardly, this can be felt. To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back and extend their arms.
Then press the site with your hand, pressing firmly; it will retreat. Then apply a bandage and bindings, tying them tightly; but as for its end which lies beyond the shoulder joint and is attached to it, it does not dislocate except rarely. If it dislocates once, do not return it nor set it right according to what was mentioned earlier.
Then apply the bandage and bindings, tying them tightly; and order the patient to observe calmness and rest until it heals. With this treatment alone, the shoulder joint will be restored if it has also dislocated.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On dislocation of the shoulder blade.
Know that the shoulder blade dislocates in three ways: one, towards the armpit and downwards; two, towards the chest; and rarely, upwards beyond the shoulder blade.
It does not dislocate backwards from the shoulder blade's location, nor forwards from its nerve; rather, it most commonly dislocates and exits downwards towards the armpit, especially in individuals with less fat, as it goes out quickly and enters quickly.
As for those with much flesh, it is different; it goes out slowly and enters slowly. Sometimes a person may receive a blow or fall, causing the shoulder blade to swell and become hot, leading him to believe that it has dislocated. Therefore, one must not test this until its condition is confirmed.
When that happens, proceed with its treatment. If the shoulder blade has dislocated downwards towards the armpit, you will find a clear difference between the dislocated and healthy ones when you bring them together. You will also feel that the head of the shoulder blade is rough and, upon touching it under the armpit, it feels like an egg.
The patient will not be able to raise his hand to his ear nor move it in all its motions. Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, this will also be evident upon examination and cannot be hidden.
This dislocation can be easily reduced if the joint is soft or the patient is young. To reduce it, have an assistant raise his hand upwards, then place your thumbs under his armpit and press the joint firmly upwards into its socket while the assistant raises his arm overhead and extends it.
Then it is placed underneath him, for he returns quickly; if he does not return as mentioned and the fever has been present for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use a relaxant ointment that softens and loosens, such as one made from linseed, chicory, and rue boiled in water.
Then the patient lies on their back, and a wool ball that is moderately soft but not too much so is placed under their armpit. Then the doctor places his elbow on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand while extending it downward.
And he pushes the ball with his foot, so that he returns to his place. If you wish, you may raise him up in this manner: bring a man taller than the patient and have him stand on one side of the bed, inserting his elbow under the patient's armpit and lifting it upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand down towards their abdomen. If the patient is frail, nothing else should be hung from them to weigh them down lest they return the fever immediately. It may also be returned in another manner.
And this is how it may also be done: Take a long, round-topped piece of wood, not too thick or thin, like the shape of a mortar. Place it on the ground and cover its top with a soft cloth. Then place it under the patient's armpit while they are standing upright along the board.
Then he extends his hand downwards from the other side and also leans his body strongly towards that side. The joint will return to its place quickly. If it is difficult to reduce, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And make its width about four fingers and its thickness about two fingers, with a round head that makes it easy to enter deep into the armpit like this:
Then tie a soft cloth onto the round head so that the wood does not hurt the patient. Then insert it under the armpit, extend the hand and arm completely downwards along the board, and tie the wood to the forearm and the end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a support. The torso is leaned against and the hand is extended downwards while the rest of the body hangs from the other side. If the joint enters its socket, a moderate-sized ball of wool should be placed beneath the armpit.
Then the bandage prepared from barley flour, incense, and egg white is applied around the entire shoulder as it rotates from above. Then the ball of wool is pulled tightly from under the armpit and tied to the bandage from above. The hand is then suspended around the neck.
He should leave his hand unmoved for seven days, and the patient's diet should be light until the member gains strength, as it hastens recovery. After seven or five days, movement should be resumed; if it holds firm without relaxing, then it has healed.
But if the joint dislocates repeatedly due to dampness or other cause, then it should be reduced using three splints as mentioned in the chapter on reduction. If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days but the joint remains unstable
If the bandage and its tension are reapplied several times without success, if it falls and relaxes, and the hand cannot be raised upwards, know that the tendon in the head of the shoulder has either snapped, stretched or relaxed. Know then that the joint will not remain stable in its place.
As for a dislocation towards the chest and breast, or backwards: it is reduced by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns. The same bandaging and treatment are used until it heals. However, if there is still considerable swelling in the member after healing, and movement is slower than before.
The patient should use hot baths many times until that swelling softens and returns to its original state
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also reduces with difficulty. It can dislocate in all directions, especially towards the front or back. You will easily recognize its dislocation as it is apparent to both sight and touch, regardless of how it has occurred.
If you examine the dislocated shoulder against the sound one, it will be evident that there is a shift in the joint and it cannot bend the arm or touch the neck with it. You should promptly reduce and return the shoulder before inflammation sets in.
If inflammation sets in, it becomes difficult to reduce the shoulder and it may not recover fully, especially if the joint is dislocated backwards. This type of dislocation is the worst and most painful of all kinds, often leading to death.
To reduce the shoulder if possible, an assistant should extend their arms and hands while the doctor places one hand above the elbow and another below it, using their thumbs or the base of their palm to push the joint back into place.
If the shoulder is dislocated forwards, it can be reduced by bending the elbow until the wrist hits the chest near the shoulder. If this does not work, apply strong and forceful traction using an assistant to extend the arm.
During traction, the patient should be held by assistants to prevent slipping. After reduction, wrap a cloth or towel around the arm and move it gently in all directions. Before manipulating the joint, lubricate your hands with oil for easier movement.
Then push the joint forcefully back into place. After reduction, apply a bandage with gentle pressure and egg white for fixation. Secure the arm to the patient's chest using a sling.
Then wait. If the joint remains in place, remove the bandage and leave it alone. But if the joint seems unstable, keep the bandage on for a few days until it becomes stronger, then remove it.
If the joint is stiff, use gentle massage and heat treatment in a bath to loosen it. Or apply a warm poultice made from suet for one day and night before removing it and bathing.
Then, you apply the oil repeatedly, first, second and third times with entering the bath until it softens. And if you wish to make a bull's penis moist and relaxed with lard and fat, do so repeatedly; for it will soften and return to its original state.
The Twenty-Eighth Chapter.
In treating dislocation of the elbow.
Dislocation of the elbow occurs frequently and reduction is easier than other joints, except that it is necessary to reduce it quickly at the time it happens before the place becomes stiff or inflammation appears.
To reduce it, place the patient's elbow on a board and have an assistant extend their hand. The physician should then place their hand on the condyle of the joint and push until it is reduced, but first make sure that the dislocation has gone back into the palm of the hand.
If the dislocation is prominent on the outer side of the hand, place the palm of the hand on the board so that the physician's hand rests on the same condyle of the joint. If it returns, well and good; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that has been soaked in an anti-inflammatory solution.
Leave him alone, do not attempt to reduce it again for it will not tolerate it nor can you succeed in reducing it after some days have passed. The joint will remain on its surface and will not harm the patient except if the hand becomes paralyzed and unable to grasp anything.
In that case, you will know that the nerve has been cut or stretched. There is no remedy for this except to try binding it with a bandage, which may help or may not. After reducing the elbow, apply the bandage we have described and keep it on for five days.
Then remove the bandage and exercise the hand. If its movement is difficult and there is stiffness, soften it with warm water and massage repeatedly until it becomes flexible.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
In treating dislocation of the fingers.
If a finger dislocates towards any direction, when it dislocates outwards or inwards, extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it is reduced. Then bind the head of the finger and suspend it towards the direction it dislocated to.
Leave it for two days, then release and extend it until it improves on its own during the day. If it happens at night, tie it with the same description; still loosen it during the day and train it with movement, tying it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And similarly, if it becomes detached towards the inner part of the hand, tie it to about the same side and do as you did initially until it heals. Similarly, do this whenever it becomes detached elsewhere.
Chapter Thirty.
In treating a dislocated lumbar vertebra.
If complete dislocation of one of the vertebrae in the back or neck occurs, or many vertebrae shift from their places, there is no treatment for it because death hastens to the patient.
The sign of this is that the patient's feces exit without control, and they cannot hold it in. Often, some of their limbs, either legs or arms or one of them, become paralyzed. But if only one vertebra shifts from its place, it often returns to its position.
If the dislocation occurs in four directions, that which dislocates backwards is called a hump. The treatment for this is to look at it; if the hump has occurred from birth, there is no treatment for it and it will never recover. But if it has happened due to a fall or a blow or something similar...
(The early authors) mentioned various treatments for this with lengthy discussions, but most of it is not useful. I have summarized what is beneficial from that and clarified the tool differently than they described it and explained it differently than they did.
I say that the hump which occurs in front of the chest and also those on both sides cannot be treated or healed. Only the ones occurring in the back can be treated, particularly with the method I will describe: lay the patient on their face.
Place a flat, level bed near a wall and put a wet cloth underneath it so that it does not hurt the patient's chest. Then insert an upright wooden post into a hole dug in the ground at the head of the bed, and another one towards the feet at the other end of the bed.
A servant holds one end of the splint, ensuring it is not secure in the hole, while another servant holds the other end in the same manner. Then, a soft, tight bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under their armpits, with one end of the bandage tied to the head-end splint.
The other end of the bandage is then pulled over their hips, knees, and ankles, before all the bindings are gathered together and tied to the splint at his feet. Then each servant pulls on the splint at their end without moving the splints from their initial positions.
However, they are not secure as mentioned earlier. The doctor places his hands on the knot with force until it returns or places a board on top of it and leans on the board with his legs until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, he takes a board that is about three arms in length.
A hole is dug into the wall mentioned earlier, near the patient. The board's end is inserted into this hole and its center placed on the hump. The doctor places his feet on the other end of the board and pulls with sufficient force to snap the knot.
And it returns to its place. If you wish, it can be made with a device called the 'screw,' which is twisted by hand: two splints are inserted into the ground at the patient's head-end of the bed, each one arm-length long, and likewise another two splints are placed at his foot-end.
And there should be a span between each splint. Then, holes are made in each splint through which the screw can pass and the wood is securely fixed into the ground so that it does not move at all. The screw rod, around which the bandage will be twisted, is inserted into these holes.
And at one end, there is a hole in which an arm-length rod has been securely fixed for twisting the bandage. The other two splints have similar holes. Then, the bandage that was tied around the patient's chest is wound around the screw at his head-end, and the bandages tied to his legs are wound around the screw at his foot-end.
Then he stands at each screw of the assistant who turns it with the wrench used for turning screws, and the doctor levels the hump as we have shown. This is a picture of the screw, tongs, and the limb.
Then, after the ligature is released and the site is leveled, it is necessary to wrap the dried bandage with egg white, then with gauze, and then place a splint made of wood over the bandage, its width being three fingers or approximately that, and its length being sufficient to cover the hump.
Then, some corrective bandages are applied, and the limb is bound with a bandage that should be used until it heals. If there remains some stiffness in the place after healing, then medicaments that soften and relax should be employed along with the splint we have described earlier for an extended period.
It is possible to use a lead plate for this purpose. If stiffness persists even after bandaging, it might be thought that the spine has become dislocated and that there is an extra bone present. Do not attempt this treatment in such cases, as death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One
In treating a dislocation of the hip joint.
Know that the hip and shoulder joints are only subject to dislocation, unlike other joints which may also undergo slight displacement orluxation. The hip joint can dislocate in four directions.
This occurs when the hip joint dislocates inwardly, outwardly, anteriorly or posteriorly. Most commonly it dislocates inwardly and less commonly anteriorly or posteriorly. The sign of inward dislocation is that when you compare the length of the healthy leg with the affected one, the healthy one appears longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will not be able to flex their leg at the hip. The area below the hip will feel warm and hot due to the displacement of the femoral head towards that region, as we have explained previously.
The symptoms of outward dislocation are the opposite of those mentioned above. For anterior dislocation, the leg is fully extended but cannot be flexed at the knee without pain. The patient will not be able to walk forward freely due to this limitation.
He retains urine and his buttock trembles upon walking, and the sign of one afflicted with this condition is that he cannot straighten his knee nor bend it before bending his thigh, and his leg is also shorter than the other.
And the thigh is flaccid, and the head of the femur is located at the point of the ischium; but the remedy for this condition is to look, for if the hip has become old with its owner without attempting to set it right and has remained in its state, then there is no cure for it.
So one should not expose him to it, but if the hip is recent and of any kind among the four types of dislocation, hasten to dislocate the joint, extend it inwards and outwards, and move it right and left perhaps.
If it does not return thus, then one should not consider him as having been helped by a strong servant. Instead, two servants should be summoned; one should extend the leg from below either with his hands or using a splint tied above the knee, while another servant extends it from above.
To enter his hands under the armpits, then pull with a soft bandage around the root of the thigh, and another servant holds the end of the bandage while stretching the leg either from in front towards the shoulder or from behind towards the back.
And they all stretch simultaneously until the patient rises from the ground and remains suspended. This method of stretching is common to all four types, but if the dislocation does not return as described above, then each type requires its specific treatment which I will mention.
If the dislocation is inwards, then the patient should lie on his sound side, and a bandage should be tied around the root of the thigh between the head of the femur and the point below the buttock.
Then the bandage should be extended upwards from the buttock towards the shoulder, and another strong servant should take hold of the thick part of the thigh bone of the affected leg with his arms and pull it outwards forcefully until it returns to its place.
This type is easier than other types of treatment used to dislocate this member. If you find it difficult and cannot use this type of treatment, then you should bind both legs of the patient with a strong yet flexible bandage around the heels and knees.
After each of these, leave one person to accompany him, the distance being that of four fingers. The affected leg should be extended two fingers' length more than the other. Then have the patient hang from a wooden beam found in the house, with his feet about two arm-lengths off the ground.
Then command a strong boy to embrace the hip, and another boy to pull him. Push the boy embracing the hip with force, and the joint will quickly return to its place. But if it is particularly dislocated outwards.
It is necessary that the patient lie on the couch as we described for treating dislocation of the hip, and tie the bandage tightly around his affected thigh, particularly his chest. Then place one board under his legs and another under his head. A third board should be placed in the middle of the couch, firmly secured.
Wrap a wet cloth around it so as not to harm the patient, and place the board between his thighs lest he slip downwards during traction. Then each servant pulls from his side while the doctor uses both hands to reduce the hip. If it responds.
If not, then place the board on it and press according to what we mentioned in dislocation of the hip joint until it becomes equal. But the patient should lie on his sound side, and if the dislocation is anterior, then extend the leg of the affected limb while lying thus.
The doctor places his right hand on the affected buttock and presses it with his left hand while maintaining continuous pressure downwards towards the knee. But if the dislocation is posterior, do not extend the limb downwards.
And he should be elevated off the ground, but rather placed on a solid object. This also applies if the hip has dislocated outwards according to what we mentioned regarding his position on the couch while lying face down with the bandages tightened.
Upon what we have mentioned earlier, it is necessary to use the compression board also on the spot where the joint has come out. When the hip joint has returned as desired, its sign will not be hidden from you, for the patient's legs will extend.
When you see them straight and the patient grasps his leg and extends it without difficulty, know that the joint has returned as desired. Then, tie the thighs together and apply the bandage, pulling it tight with a cloth so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain still for three or four days, then remove the bandage and splint, and test the leg by moving it. If you find them equal in strength, know that the hip has healed and allow the patient to walk. But if there is some laxity...
...return and bind it again, tightening it as before. Leave it like that for three more days, then remove it and gradually allow walking on it until strength returns, if Allah wills.
Chapter Two-Thirty.
In the treatment of knee fracture.
The knee breaks into three directions: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward). It does not break forward. The sign of its fracture is when the patient cannot bring his leg towards his thigh.
If it does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured. To set all sides of its fracture, make the patient sit with his legs extended and another person sit behind him holding his waist and pulling him slightly backwards, then you sit on his thighs.
Lean your back against his face, place his leg between yours, then press his knee with both hands and interlock your fingers over it. Then squeeze the sides of the knee with force while another assistant extends the patient's other leg until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of its return is when the leg sticks to the thigh smoothly without resistance. Then bind it, adhering the leg to the thigh, and tie them together with a bandage for three or four days. After that, allow gradual walking until strength returns.
If its return proves difficult according to what we have described, then use the strong bandage with the bindings that are mentioned earlier in treating the hip until it returns.
The Thirty-Third Chapter.
In treating the heel bone fracture.
The heel can slip away easily or it can snap completely, its fracture being either inward or outward. The sign of a fracture is that the heel appears swollen and prominent on the side towards which it has snapped.
However, if it has slipped, its reduction is easy; one should gently pull with the hands until it returns to its place. But if it has snapped completely, then the patient should not sit down and a strong servant should hold him firmly from behind at his waist.
Then, holding the patient's foot with your right hand at its upper part and with your left hand below it at the ankle joint, pull the foot towards you with your right hand twice then with your left, then stretch it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand gently towards the leg.
Repeat this procedure twice as described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg on the third attempt while pulling at the ankle joint. If it returns in one or two attempts and you see that the foot is straight, otherwise repeat the process until it returns.
If it does not return according to what we have described, then lay the patient on their back on the ground and drive two pegs into the ground securely so that they fall between his thighs. Cover them with a cloth so as not to harm the patient, then adjust a servant's thigh.
Then another servant pulls the patient's leg either with his hands or with a bandage tied around the patient's thigh. Then each servant pulls in turn while keeping the post upright between the patient's thighs to prevent the body from sliding down during traction, then the physician straightens the fracture by hand.
Then another servant holds the healthy leg downwards; if the fracture returns quickly, then bind the splint and weights and tie it securely with bandages taking care not to harm the nerve that lies above the heel from behind.
To prevent the bandage from being too tight on him, causing harm, then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage loosens, tie it again on the third or fourth day. The patient should refrain from walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this period, it is not safe that the jawbone will dislocate and spoil, and afterwards it will not accept treatment. If he has a hot swelling, do not use what we have mentioned above for its relief except in another place of treatment and bandaging until it subsides.
Chapter Forty-Three.
In the treatment of the jawbone of the foot.
It is necessary to set what has dislocated from it with a slight extension without force, for this is not difficult but easy. If the jawbone of one of the toes on the back of the foot is dislocated, it is necessary for the patient to place his foot on a level place on the ground.
Or, while standing like walking, place your foot on the raised part of those joints and press it with your foot forcefully until it returns to its place and you see that it has been set without any swelling appearing.
Then place a board with two raised ends under the sole of his foot, wrap it tightly and securely for three days. Then release it and keep him from walking for many days until it becomes strong and its return is assured, if Allah wills.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
In the types of dislocation that occurs with a wound or with a fracture or with both.
If any of these happens, neglecting its treatment and care often results in death.
Therefore, it is not permissible for anyone to undertake the treatment of such cases except someone who is skilled in this art with long experience, careful, gentle, cautious, and not hasty or reckless. He should start by using remedies that reduce hot swellings only, and then entrust the patient [to him].
O Allah, decree for him only what You have decreed for his safety from harm with ease of the disease and some hope in him. Therefore, return him immediately to his original state before the hot swelling appears.
If the limb returns to its intended state, use the method that treats hot swellings and apply appropriate dried poultices to the wound. If the jaw is broken with splinters protruding, remove them. And follow what we mentioned earlier in simple diseases as indicated in their respective places.
And strive diligently to avoid the path of error that we have warned you against, for that is best for your honor and safest for your practice, if God wills.
